All the Wild Horses

by Emilia_Rowan

Summary

Agatha and Rio's whirlwind romance ended when Rio left town for veterinary school and Agatha stayed to take over her family's ranch. Little did they know that they both left the relationship with more than broken hearts. Seventeen years later, Rio returns to Westview with her daughter in tow. When Tori Vidal strikes up a friendship with Nicky Harkness, it's only a matter of time before the girls dig up their moms' old secrets.

Chapter 1: Rio

Twenty-one hours. That was how long Rio had been driving. She had driven ten hours yesterday before stopping at a cheap roadside motel and catching a few hours of sleep. Now she was going on hour eleven of today’s drive, a drive that was only supposed to take eight hours but had been delayed by construction and accidents, and she was starting to question her sanity. The Montana hills stretched on for miles and miles on either side of the highway, only ending where the Rocky Mountains rose up against the western horizon. To anyone else, every mile looked the same, but these hills and this stretch of road were etched into Rio’s memory. Even without her GPS, she knew she was getting close.

An exit sign flashed in Rio’s headlights: Westview, 2 miles. Rio sighed in relief, releasing her hold on the steering wheel to flex her fingers. She couldn’t wait to get out of this truck; she was fairly certain the seat now had a permanent imprint from her ass.

“Are we almost there?”

Rio glanced up at the rearview mirror. Her daughter, Tori, sat slumped sideways against the truck door, her long legs stretched across the seat. She stared at the Nintendo Switch in her hands, her phone having died several hours earlier. Her black shirt was covered in crumbs from the bag of chips she had snacked on earlier, and her hair had fallen half out of the braid she had styled it in at the motel that morning. She looked just like Rio: same skin tone, same dark hair, same lithe build. She reminded Rio of a younger version of herself, too, dressing in dark clothes and wearing far too much eyeliner. The only real difference between them was their eyes. Where Rio’s eyes were dark brown, Tori’s eyes were a piercing shade of blue.

“Almost, mija,” Rio replied. “Are you excited to see tito?”

“I guess,” Tori mumbled. “What do you think Tito will think of Draco?”

Draco was Tori’s pet bearded dragon. The lizard was currently in a plastic tote on the seat beside Tori. Rio couldn’t see the little guy, but she thought he was probably angry about the accommodations. Then again, bearded dragons always seemed to be angry, especially this one.

“I think he’ll ask why you didn’t get a puppy like a normal kid,” Rio teased.

“Puppies are basic. Draco’s cool,” Tori told her.

“Whatever you say, mija,” Rio chuckled.

Rio had made it clear to her father that the bearded dragon was coming with them, whether he liked it or not. She was moving them away from everything Tori had known for the last ten years, her school, her friends, away from San Diego to the middle of nowhere Montana. She wasn’t about to make her give up her pet, even if it was a prickly little reptile.

Rio pulled off the exit and headed into town. They passed the Welcome to Westview sign, and she felt her stomach flip. It had been seventeen years since she left Westview, moving to California to study veterinary medicine. She hadn’t known when she left that she was taking something, or rather someone, with her. Tori was born in California, in May of her first year of veterinary school. Rio had taken her exams in the hospital room. For the next three years, Rio worked her ass off to balance veterinary school with being a single mother. Her father helped as much as possible, in spite of living paycheck to paycheck himself. But Rio had managed. She made it through four years of school, then two years of clinicals, and two years of working in a chain veterinary practice before she landed her dream job at San Diego Zoo working with the big cats. That was where she had stayed for the last eight years, only visiting Westview on holidays every other year so that her daughter could spend time with her grandfather.

Her relationship with her father had always been… strained. Mateo was old fashioned in that he expected everyone to bend to his will as head of the household. Whatever he said was the rule, no argument or discussion. While he never hit her, Mateo Vidal could get loud, and as a girl Rio often reacted to his temper by crying, which only angered her father further. Then, as she got older, instead of crying, Rio would simply shout back.

Rio couldn’t wait to turn eighteen, go to college, and get out of town. And that’s exactly what she did. She worked hard and got good grades. She started barrel racing, with her father’s help, and earned scholarships to put herself through school. Her goal was to go to college, become a veterinarian, and then travel around the world taking care of exotic and endangered animals.

Her parents never understood her dreams, not really. They understood her desire to be a veterinarian because vets made good money and were in high demand around Westview. But they never understood why she wanted to move away. Her father, in particular, made his opinion on the matter known. He felt that his support of her, especially any financial support, meant that he had the final say in Rio’s life and any decisions she made. He didn’t understand that every time they argued, every time he tried to manipulate or guilt trip her into coming home, it just hardened Rio’s resolve to stay away.

And then she got pregnant. To say her father had been disappointed would be the understatement of the century. Their fight when Rio told him had been nuclear. They hadn’t spoken for two weeks after.

But then, halfway through her pregnancy, Rio’s mother died in a car accident. Rio expected her father to double down on his demands for her to move back home, but he surprised her. Almost overnight Mateo became her biggest supporter. Once Tori was born, he even sent money to pay for daycare, so that Rio could continue going to class. It was an abrupt shift, but one Rio was extremely grateful for.

In the years that followed, Rio only visited a couple times a year. She and her father still butted heads, but she kept coming back, for Tori. For all his faults, Mateo doted on his granddaughter, and Tori loved him in return.

Then, two weeks ago, Rio got a call from her father’s neighbor. Mateo had fallen walking from his house to the barn, and he couldn’t get up. He was stuck there for eight hours, until the neighbor got home from work and happened to see him in the beams of his headlights. Rio hated to even think of what might’ve happened to her father if the neighbor hadn’t spotted him.

That was when Rio discovered that her father had Parkinson’s disease. It wasn’t a new diagnosis, oh no, he had been diagnosed two years before. Sure, Rio had noticed during their visits that his hands shook sometimes, and he had started walking with a cane. But she thought that was just part of aging. She never imagined that her father had been diagnosed with a degenerative disease and kept it from her.

Rio knew then that she had to come back. It wasn’t what she wanted, but it was what she felt like she had to do. Her father was a stubborn man, sure, but he was still her father. He had worked hard to provide for her growing up, had supported her and Tori so that Rio could finish her education. Everything Rio was, she owed to him.

The town of Westview seemed to appear out of nowhere, rising up out of the grassy plains around them. They passed the school that housed all the grades, pre-K through twelve, the town hall, the library, the bank, the sheriff’s office. Small side streets were lined with stores, hair salons, and restaurants. Everything looked exactly the same as Rio remembered it, just as beaten up and dusty as it had been seventeen years ago. Then, almost as soon as the town had appeared, it disappeared, and they were back among the hills and pines.

“Is that it?” Tori asked, sitting up and pressing her face against the window to look behind her. “It’s even smaller than I remember.”

“Everything seems bigger when you’re a kid,” Rio commented. “Bozeman’s only an hour drive, though.”

“A whole hour?” Tori exclaimed. “I don’t even have a car. Or a driver’s license!”

“I promise you’ll survive,” Rio said, fighting the smirk that threatened to overtake her face. “I mean, I survived, and I didn’t even have a cell phone or Amazon Prime.”

“Yeah, ‘cause you grew up in the Stone Age,” Tori grumbled.

A few miles past town, Rio turned onto a secondary road, then a side road, and finally onto a gravel drive. She tried to avoid as many ruts as possible, but even so she could see the U-haul behind her bobbing and bucking as she went. Finally they came to the end of the drive, to an old singlewide trailer with brown metal siding. A small barn sat about fifty yards away from the house, along with a round pen, and a pasture ran along the back of the property. The porchlight was on, and as Rio put the truck in park, the front door opened and a slender figure emerged from inside, leaning heavily on a cane.

“Tito doesn’t look so good,” Tori said, her voice quiet.

Rio nodded solemnly. “That’s why we’re here, mija.”

Whatever resentment Tori had been carrying for the last two weeks, it disappeared as soon as she hopped out of the truck. She ran toward her grandfather on long legs, her dark hair streaming behind her. She had the old man wrapped in a tight hug before Rio could even get around the truck on her stiff legs.

“Oy, nieta, you’re going to squeeze me in two!” Mateo exclaimed, but he hugged his granddaughter back just as tightly.

“I missed you, Tito,” Tori said, and Rio knew it was true.

“We both did,” Rio said, standing a few feet away.

“Mija,” Mateo greeted, looking her over. “You look good.”

“What about me? Do I look good?” Tori asked.

Mateo chuckled. “You grabbed me so fast, nieta, I didn’t even get a good look at you.”

Tori stood back, running her fingers through her hair. “What do you think?”

“Muy bonita, si,” Mateo replied, looking her over. Then his eyes narrowed. “Victoria! ¿Por que? What have you done to your hair?”

Tori laughed. “I dyed it.”

“Ach! Mija, you let her do this?” Mateo asked, reaching out and stroking the purple streak in the teenager’s hair.

“You try telling her not to do something when she’s determined,” Rio replied. “She’s worse than I ever was at her age, papi. I’m just glad it was hair dye and not a nose piercing. Or a tattoo.”

Tori’s eyes glittered in the dim porchlight. “Someday.”

“Only after you turn eighteen,” Rio told her.

“I fixed up your mom’s old room for you, nieta,” Mateo said, shaking his head. “I can help you bring your stuff inside, if you want.”

“Okay,” Tori replied, handing him her small knapsack before going back to the truck and grabbing a plastic tote. “Tito, this is Draco.”

“What the hell is that?” Mateo asked, peering at the tote in the dim light of the porchlight.

“Draco is a bearded dragon,” Tori told him.

“I told you about the lizard, Papi,” Rio reminded him.

“I hoped you’d forget it in California,” Mateo grumbled. “Alright, nieta, I’ll help you get that thing settled.”

“Thank you, Tito!” Tori exclaimed, beaming as they headed into the house. “Mami, can you get his enclosure for me?”

“Yeah, I’ll bring it inside in a few minutes,” Rio replied, a grin tugging at her lips.

To say that her father had been disappointed when she got pregnant at twenty-two was the understatement of the century. He had even suggested she get an abortion. But Rio was stubborn, a trait she had inherited from both parents, and when she decided to keep her baby, her father had turned into her biggest supporter. The moment Tori was born, Rio had been instantly replaced as her father’s favorite girl, but it was a demotion that she was perfectly fine with. Even though they lived far away and only visited for holidays, Tori had a wonderful relationship with her grandparents, and Rio would always be grateful for that.

Instead of opening the U-Haul full of their belongings, Rio headed to the barn. It wasn’t anything fancy, and as soon as she turned on the lights she spotted places that needed work— rusted metal, worn wood, peeling paint. Still, this had been her favorite place growing up. There had been a time that she resented her chores, just like most kids, feeding the horses before school, hours mucking the stalls after. And yet she spent every spare moment in the barn, talking to the horses as if they could understand her. Sometimes she felt like the animals understood her better than any human ever could, her father included.

Rio made her way down the center of the barn. Most of the stalls were empty now. Her father had sold many of his own horses when he got sick, and he wasn’t able to take on many clients for training. But a few long noses poked out of their stalls, inspecting Rio as she passed. When Rio came to the opposite end of the barn, two familiar heads greeted her.

“Hello, girls,” Rio greeted, walking up to the first horse. The black mare nickered softly at Rio before nuzzling her. Rio held the big head with one hand and stroked the mare’s white blaze with the other. “I’m back, old girl. Home for good this time.”

The mare just snorted in response. The horse’s name was Lady Death, a name that teenage Rio had thought sounded extremely badass twenty-two years before when she started riding the mare. With Lady under the saddle, Rio had earned enough prize money and scholarships from barrel racing to put herself through college and pay for most of her veterinary school tuition. The mare had long since retired from racing, but still, Mateo kept her. She had been a broodmare for many years, producing beautiful foals, most of which went on to run barrels themselves, but now she was just a pet. Still, Rio knew that her father would never sell her. He was sentimental like that, and so was she.

The mare in the stall beside Lady’s whinnied impatiently. Rio chuckled and gave Lady a kiss on her muzzle before moving to her daughter. Luna was Lady’s last foal, born on one of Rio and Tori’s visits, and Tori had immediately fallen in love with the blue roan filly. Mateo had trained the mare specifically for his granddaughter, and the two had grown up together on their visits to Westview. Last summer Tori had even entered the mare in a couple of barrel races, and they had done pretty well. Rio could see a lot of promise in both of them, but she had never thought it would come to fruition.

But, well, sometimes things change.

“Tori will come see you in the morning, sweet girl,” Rio told the mare. “She’s just tired.”

Luna snorted and nosed at Rio’s coat pocket. Rio just laughed.

“I don’t have any peppermints, silly,” Rio said.

Unimpressed, Luna pulled her head back into the stall and went back to her hay. Rio laughed and went back to Lady.

“Looks like we both have daughters with attitude, girl,” Rio said, giving her one more pat before leaving the barn.

The U-haul trailer was open and the giant PVC enclosure was missing, so Rio surmised that Tori had gotten impatient and moved it inside herself. She closed the hatch and then got their suitcases out of the truck before going inside. They could unload the rest in the morning.

Every time Rio visited, the inside of the trailer where she grew up felt smaller. With the sofa pulled out into a bed, the already small living room felt even tinier. Her father had layered the thin mattress with one of those foam toppers, then put his nicest set of sheets on the small bed. Her mother’s favorite quilt was spread on top, and Rio’s heart gave a lurch at the sight of the double wedding ring pattern.

“Are you sure you don’t want to stay in my room?” Mateo asked, emerging from the hallway. “I can sleep on the sofa.”

“No, Papi, this is great,” Rio replied. She couldn’t imagine her father getting up and down off of the low sofa bed. “I’ll be fine here, don’t worry. I just hate that it’s in your way.”

He scoffed. “Nonsense, mija, you’re never in my way.”

“Is Tori already asleep?” Rio asked with a chuckle.

“Almost,” her father replied. “You’d think she spent the whole day in the saddle instead of riding in a truck. But she insisted on getting that damn lizard set up.”

“For people like us, I think riding in a truck with nothing else to do might be the more exhausting option,” Rio commented, and her father nodded in agreement.

Rio made her way down the short, narrow hallway. On one side was the air return, but the other side was decorated with framed photos of Rio and her cousins. At the end of the hall was a small bathroom and a bedroom. The bedroom walls were still decorated with movie posters from the late 90s and early 2000s, and all of Rio’s trophies and ribbons hung on a shelf beside the closet. A twin bed sat in the corner, with its own fresh sheets and a quilt made by Rio’s mother. Tori had changed into her pajamas and was checking her phone, but her eyes drooped with exhaustion.

Rio knocked softly on the open door to announce her presence. “You okay in here?”

“Yeah, I’m okay,” Tori replied. “Did you go see Lady and Luna? I should’ve gone, too, but I was talking to Tito. And look, we got Draco’s tank set up!”

“Luna forgives you. I told her you’d come see her in the morning,” Rio replied. She turned to look at the dresser. All of her trophies had been moved to make room for the large PVC reptile enclosure. The dragon was sitting on top of its fake rock, basking in the heat from its heat lamp. “Draco looks happy.”

“I’ll get all his plants and things situated tomorrow,” Tori told her. “For now I think he’s just happy to be out of that tote.”

“Yeah, he definitely wasn’t happy with his travel accommodations,” Rio chuckled. She glanced toward the door and then back to her daughter. “Your grandpa seemed happy to see us.”

“Yeah,” Tori sighed. She rolled onto her stomach and buried her face in her arms for a moment before looking back at Rio. “I miss home but… I understand why we had to come now.”

Rio sat on the edge of the bed and ran her fingers through the ends of her daughter’s hair. “This can be home, too, now that we’re here. You’ll see. Once you start school and make friends, you’ll learn to love it.”

“You didn’t love it. You left it.”

“Well, all the vet schools just happen to be in other states,” Rio told her. “And I had big dreams of traveling around the world, helping endangered species.”

“But you didn’t,” Tori frowned.

Rio touched the purple streak in her daughter’s hair, letting it twist through her fingers. “Dreams change.”

“Because of me?” Tori asked.

“Mmhmm,” Rio nodded. “But sometimes, reality is better than any dream you can think up. Life has a way of surprising us like that.”

Tori didn’t look like she fully understood, but she didn’t say anything more. Rio bent and kissed her daughter’s forehead before pushing herself off the bed. “You better go to sleep. Tomorrow we have to unpack everything, and then on Monday we need to get you enrolled at the local high school. Go Mustangs!”

Tori groaned. “Do we really have to do that so soon? We just got here.”

“Yep, gotta happen on Monday,” Rio replied. “You need to start school and I have to start work. The longer we put it off, the worse it will be.”

Tori groaned again, burying her face in her bedding. Rio just grinned as she turned out the light. “Good night, mija.”

Rio barely caught a muffled “G’night” as she closed the door. She made her way back to the main area of the house to find her father shuffling around the kitchen. With shaking hands he placed two glass tumblers on the countertop, then pulled a bottle of whisky from the top shelf of the cabinet.

“She’s just like you were at that age,” Mateo said, expertly pouring the whiskey in spite of his unsteady hands. He capped the bottle and handed a glass to Rio before picking up his own and carrying it to the round kitchen table. Rio sat beside him and sipped the amber liquid. “And she looks just like you, thank goodness. Except for those eyes, but I suppose—“

“Papi,” Rio cut him off, her voice low. She knew her father’s opinion of her daughter’s other parent. He had made it extremely clear from the moment Rio revealed her pregnancy, and he couldn’t seem to stop himself from bringing it up in some way every single time they visited, even though it always led to both of them getting angry.

“I’m just saying, you’ve done a good job with her, mija,” Mateo said, swirling his whiskey in the glass. “I know it’s been hard, doing it alone, but in the end I think it’s better that way. You’re much better off than if you had—”

“Dad,” Rio said, her voice firm, “that’s enough.”

Mateo’s eyes widened at her tone. “Rio—”

“I don’t want to talk about that,” Rio told him. “My god, I’ve barely been home for an hour. Do you have to bring that up?”

“I just wanted you to know how good of a mother you are,” her father argued. “You never needed—”

“Just stop, please,” Rio said, her voice strained. “Please.”

Mateo studied her for a minute, but Rio refused to meet his eyes. Instead her gaze fell on an aged drawing held to the refrigerator door by a magnet. There was a black four-legged shape that was supposed to be a horse, and beside that two stick figures, one tall and one short, holding hands. There was a large empty space beside the stick figures, something that had always bothered Rio. Tori had drawn the picture when she was six and they were visiting for Christmas. It had hung in the same place on the fridge for ten years, and that empty space had always haunted Rio. Perhaps her daughter had meant to draw more stick figures in the space, to represent her grandparents. But Rio had always thought it signified something more.

“Is she still around?” Rio asked, her mouth speaking the question before her mind could catch up to her.

Mateo looked like he didn’t want to answer, but finally he nodded. “Jim died a few months ago. She runs the ranch now.”

Rio nodded in response. She downed the rest of her whiskey in two gulps. “I think I’ll try and get some sleep now, if that’s okay with you.”

“Of course, mija. You’ve had a long trip,” her father agreed. He drank the rest of his whiskey then put both of their glasses in the sink before turning and heading to his own bedroom at the opposite end of the house.

Rio sighed and turned off the kitchen light. She changed into her pajamas and tucked herself in on the sofa bed. Even with the foam topper she could almost feel the wire springs in the thin mattress, but she could tolerate it. She pulled the covers up to her chin and breathed in the familiar scent of the detergent and fabric softener her parents had used for as long as she could remember.

It smelled like home.

Chapter 2: Agatha

Chapter Notes

Each chapter will be told from a different character’s (or characters’) POV, and whose POV it is will be in the chapter title.

The law offices of Kale and Walters looked more like a spa than a law firm. The air was scented with calming essential oils, the lighting was kept dim, and soft music played throughout the office. Agatha sat in an ergonomic chair made specifically to put clients at ease, even in the midst of tense legal arguments.

Agatha was not relaxed. Agatha was at war.

“My client is proposing a buyout of Mrs. Harkness’s half of the ranch,” Jen said, sliding papers across the table. “Based on current market values, the sum is more than fair. My client is also offering to pay the remainder of Mrs. Harkness’s mortgage on her New York City brownstone.”

“My client isn’t interested in selling,” her mother’s attorney replied, not even looking at the papers. “We’re filing a motion to have James Harkness’s will declared void due to mental incapacity.”

“Mental incapacity?” Agatha asked, ignoring Jen’s look of warning. “Grandpa was of sound mind all the way up until the day he died. Not that you would know that, Mother.” She turned and glared at her mother. “You hate the ranch. You hate everything about the ranch and Montana and horses and cows. You hate it all. You have your life in Boston. Just take the buyout and be done.”

“I don’t want half,” Evanora said, her voice worn from years of cigarettes but sharp as a knife. “I’m his daughter. With both of my brothers dead, by rights, the entire property should’ve passed directly to me.”

“Grandpa knew you wouldn’t want it,” Agatha pointed out. “He knew you’d sell out before his body was even cold. That’s why he changed his will. He wanted me to buy you out, so you wouldn’t be left with nothing. So take the fucking money. That’s what this is about, isn’t it?”

“No,” Evanora replied, a smug look on her face. “It’s not about money. It’s about getting what’s rightfully mine.”

“And cheating me out of what’s mine!” Agatha argued.

“Ladies—” Evanora’s attorney tried to cut them off, but they were on a roll now.

“Do you really hate me that much?” Agatha asked. “Do you hate your grandchildren that much?”

“I’ll make sure that Nicole and Oliver are taken care of,” Evanora replied, gesturing to her attorney. “I’ll make sure their tuition is covered at the finest private schools on the East Coast. I’ll even let you have a say in which ones they attend. I know Oliver has special needs that can make placement challenging, but for Nicole, I'm considering Salem Academy. They did such good things for you.”

Agatha could feel the vein in her forehead beginning to twitch. “Forty million, Mother. I’ll raise my offer to forty million.”

“Agatha—” Jen interjected.

“No,” Evanora replied, glaring at her daughter with unconcealed contempt.

“Mrs. Harkness, maybe we should consider—” Evanora’s attorney said. Clearly his payment was based on whatever Evanora won from this lawsuit.

“The ranch is appraised to sell at sixty-four million, land only, with six million worth of livestock,” Agatha said. “Take the forty.”

“No,” Evanora said again. Her expression was serene, but Agatha could see the seething rage and hatred just beneath the surface. It was a look Agatha had seen many times before, throughout her entire life.

“Fine,” Agatha growled, pushing her chair back and standing abruptly. “I’ll see you in court.”

She left the conference room in a huff, making her way down the hall to Jen’s office. She let herself in, slamming the door behind her. The office was modern and professional, all rich brown and gold, but with enough pale pink to know it belonged to Jennifer Kale. It reminded Agatha of Jen’s half of their college dorm room two decades earlier. A plush sofa sat against one wall, an abstract painting in soft pinks, reds, and gold hanging above it. At each end of the sofa was an end table, each one holding a crystal vase filled with pink tulips.

Agatha grabbed one of the glass vases and with a roar she hurled it against the wall. Shards of glass and water flew in all directions, cut up green stems and torn petals littered the floor. Agatha took a pillow from the sofa and held it to her face, screaming into the embroidered fabric. Once her voice failed, she pulled at the fabric, ripping it down the middle until the seams burst and white polyester fiber fell to the floor.

“Are you done?”

Agatha didn’t even look up at her attorney and friend. “Not by a long shot.”

Agatha started toward the other vase, but Jen stepped in front of her, cutting her off. “Nuh-uh, nope. You can go home and destroy your own shit all you want, but you better get yourself under control in my office. Do we understand each other?”

Agatha glared at her for a moment, then nodded reluctantly.

Jen sighed and looked around. “You’re paying for that, by the way.”

“Add it to my tab,” Agatha growled, sitting on the sofa with a huff. “Do you have anything to drink?”

“I have water,” Jen replied.

“You know that’s not what I mean,” Agatha said, leaning back into the cushions.

Jen went behind her desk and opened a cabinet, pulling out a crystal decanter of amber liquor and two glasses. “I’m only pouring you a small one. You still have to drive home.”

“Whatever,” Agatha sighed. Jen handed her a glass and she took a sip of the whiskey. She stuck her tongue out in disgust. “Ugh… so cheap.”

Jen rolled her eyes. “Do you remember when we talked about you keeping your mouth shut and letting me take charge of the meeting with your mother? Do you remember us having that conversation at all? Because I seem to remember that conversation happening right here, in this very office, not even an hour ago.”

“I remember,” Agatha mumbled, taking another sip of whiskey.

“Oh, good, because I thought you might’ve hit your head or something,” Jen spat. “What the hell is wrong with you?”

“She just… She knows how to push my buttons,” Agatha said.

“Agatha, you don’t have buttons,” Jen told her. “You are a button. Just one giant button. One giant red button with lights and a label that says, ‘Press here for fireworks.’”

Agatha glared at her attorney. “Are you done?”

Jen sighed and sat in the chair across from her. “You and I both know that her suit has no grounds. Your grandfather was definitely competent when he had his attorney put you in the will.”

“The only incompetent thing he did was leave her in it.” Agatha closed her eyes and tried to take calming breaths. It wasn’t working. “I tried to convince him to leave the whole thing to me, but he was adamant. And as much as I hate my mother, I understand why he wouldn’t. She was his daughter, even if they were estranged. He was a mean son of a bitch, but he wanted to make sure she was taken care of, and he didn’t trust that I wouldn’t just kick her to the curb if he didn’t leave her anything.”

“And would you have?”

“Damn straight I would have,” Agatha confirmed. “What do you think she plans to do with me if she were to somehow win and get the entire ranch?”

“Well, it’s a good thing that’s not going to happen,” Jen assured her. “I don’t understand why she won’t just take the buyout.”

“Because she fucking hates me,” Agatha explained. “Probably has something to do with my no good, piece of shit father. Whatever the reason, for as long as I can remember, my mother has hated everything about me. That’s why she shipped me off to boarding school as soon as I was old enough, why she’d ship me off to grandpa’s ranch every summer and holiday. She didn’t want me around. And good fucking riddance, honestly.”

“Eventually she’s going to have to take it,” Jen pointed out. “She’s just delaying the inevitable.”

“That’s the whole point,” Agatha sighed. “Until this is settled, I can’t do anything with the ranch. I can’t sell anything, not the land, none of the horses, none of the cattle. I can’t move forward with breeding season for next year’s crop of foals or calves unless I pull the funds from my personal savings. I can maintain the day to day function of the ranch, like paying the ranch hands and utilities and such, but I can only cover those costs for so long without selling the animals I produce to make a profit.”

“She’s trying to run you out of business,” Jen surmised.

“If she delays this in court for much longer, she’ll succeed,” Agatha told her. “It’s been four months since grandpa died. It was winter, so things were slow, but now that spring is here…”

“I get it,” Jen nodded. Agatha knew she didn’t. Not really. Unless someone lived and breathed the life Agatha led, they couldn’t fully understand.

A loud buzzing sound interrupted them. Agatha threw back the remainder of her whiskey before digging through her purse for her phone.

“Lilia took Nicky and Ollie with her to a livestock auction today,” she explained. “They’re getting some heifers that I can breed while the other stock is tied up in this lawsuit.”

“I bet they had a good time,” Jen said brightly.

Based on the number of missed calls, voicemails, and text message notifications on Agatha’s phone screen, they had too good of a time.

“Shit, I have to go,” Agatha said, standing and turning toward the door.

“Is everything okay?” Jen asked.

There were six missed calls from her ranch manager. And there were thirty-two text message notifications, twenty-six of which were from her daughter.

“Jesus fucking Christ,” Agatha muttered as she clicked on the text message thread. A picture of two miniature highland cows popped up on her screen, along with her daughter’s smiling face. There were more pictures, but rather than scroll through them, Agatha closed the messages and pulled up the phone app.

“Yeah, everything’s fine,” she told Jen as she pressed the icon to call her ranch manager. “I might murder my daughter, but everything’s fine.”

Lilia answered on the first ring. “Agatha—”

“Hi Lilia,” Agatha greeted, plastering a fake smile onto her face as she hurried out of the office. “What the actual fuck?”

 

The drive home wasn’t a long one. Even so, Agatha could almost feel her blood pressure rising as she drove between the stone pillars and beneath the wooden arch that marked the drive to the Harkness Ranch. Her great-great grandfather Hiram Harkness had put up the wooden arch himself, after he claimed the original property as a homesteader in 1887. The stone pillars were added by her grandfather, to make the entrance look more imposing. The ranch was home to nearly a thousand cattle, along with a herd of forty-seven horses. It was a major operation, one that had been passed down in Agatha’s family now for six generations.

Her mother had been the first Harkness in five generations to leave. Agatha couldn’t really blame her: her grandfather was a cold man, and he didn’t have much interest in his only daughter. It was only after his sons died childless that he had even shown interest in Evanora and through her, Agatha.

Agatha had spent every summer from the time she was six years old on the ranch. Her mother couldn’t stand her, so it made sense that she would ship her off whenever she could. Her grandfather didn’t seem to know exactly what to do with her either, but Agatha didn’t care. On the ranch she could run wild, away from her mother. She loved the horses, the cattle, the wide open skies. The ranch was her home. Agatha would be damned if her mother sold it out from under her.

As Agatha rounded the bend, the gravel drive split. She could drive to the top of the hill, where the main house sat, or to the base, where the main barns and paddocks were located. Agatha took the lower drive, making her way to the barns. While she wanted nothing more than to go to the house, take an Ambien, and sleep for at least sixteen hours, she had to check on the ranch operations first.

Agatha parked her Lexus between the pickup trucks and livestock trailers before making her way into the barn. She barely made it through the door when something small and furry almost knocked her off her feet.

“Mama!”

The first thing Agatha saw was her daughter. At just over seventeen years old, Nicky was the same height as her. Her dark hair was long and sleek, with golden highlights at the ends. Her cheeks were dotted with freckles from days spent on horseback under the clear Montana sun. Everyone said she looked exactly like Agatha, except for her eyes, which were brown instead of blue.

The second thing Agatha saw was the two lead ropes in Nicky’s hands. And the third and fourth thing she saw were two very small and very fluffy calves, one solid red, the other white with black speckling, both following behind her daughter like she was their new mama.

“What the—?” Agatha began.

“Surprise?” Nicky said, grinning sheepishly, her braces on full display.

Agatha closed her eyes and rubbed her temples. “Nicole Elizabeth Harkness—”

“But Mama, listen—”

“We raise black angus and hereford cattle—”

“I know, but—”

“We are running a business—”

“Yeah, but—”

“We raise beef cattle—”

“Technically highland cows are beef cattle.”

Agatha opened her eyes. “Nicky, you and I both know damn well that you have no intention of sending these two fluff balls to a meat processor.”

Nicky dragged the toe of her boot across the barn floor. “Well no…”

“I sent you all to the auction for heifers. Angus heifers,” Agatha added as soon as she saw her daughter open her mouth. “Where the hell is Lilia?”

Nicky opened her mouth to speak, but before she could make a sound there was an ungodly squeal from the other end of the barn, in the indoor arena. Agatha was not an idiot. She had grown up surrounded by cattle and horses. She would know that sound anywhere.

She looked at her daughter who, impossibly, looked even more sheepish than before. “Oh you have got to be kidding me.”

She sidestepped the mini cows and hurried through the barn. Nicky followed, going as fast as she could with two short-legged calves trailing her. Agatha rounded the corner and ran down the ramp to the arena just in time to see two of her most experienced ranch hands jump the fence as a blur of brown and white fur slid to a stop, sending dirt and sand flying through the air. The two ranch hands whooped and hollered, high-fiving each other as the wild beast in the arena turned and ran in the opposite direction. It looked like every other employee of the ranch was also standing around the arena, watching the commotion.

Agatha turned to her daughter, who was running down the ramp with the two calves in tow. “You go put those two… somewhere, and then get your ass back down here. Understood?”

“Yes, Mama,” Nicky replied, turning and hurrying back in the opposite direction. Agatha sighed and turned back to the audience gathered around the arena.

“What the fuck is going on here?” Agatha roared, drawing everyone’s attention. In seconds the crowd that had gathered dispersed, all hurrying to return to their actual work. Only one figure remained, standing on a rail halfway up the metal fencing, leaning over to watch the horse running wild in the arena. Agatha groaned and heaved herself up beside her ranch manager, holding on for dear life.

“Lilia,” she greeted. “I thought I sent you to the auction for heifers.”

“Oh, we got heifers,” Lilia replied. “Eighteen black angus from Ross’s operation and sixteen hereford from Three C’s. Already got them unloaded into pastures nine and ten. Vet should be out to check them in the morning. Three C’s tried to sell me on some nice Charlais, but I told them I’d have to run that by you first.”

“You needed to check with me to purchase Charlais but you let my daughter buy mini highlands and a wild animal?” Agatha asked, gesturing to the horse that was still running wild from one end of the arena to the other. “What did Ollie buy? A zebra?”

“No,” Lilia replied. Then she added, under her breath, “But he did buy a dozen new chicks. He’s setting up the brooder for them now.”

“This day just keeps getting better,” Agatha spat sarcastically.

“They got hold of my auction paddle,” Lilia told her.

“And where were you?” Agatha asked.

“I was talking to Phil about those Charlais. I swear, I was only gone for like five minutes.” Lilia’s face drew serious. “You hired me to manage this ranch, not babysit your children. And while I’m happy to help with them when things come up, I cannot do both jobs at the same time.”

Agatha looked back at the horse in the arena. It had finally slowed down, a thick layer of sweat building in its fur. She had to admit, her daughter had good taste. The horse had a beautiful buckskin base coat, with an overo paint pattern. It was flashy and built solid, taller than Nicky’s current mount, and from the way it ran around the arena, Agatha could already tell it had the potential to go fast.

“Isn’t he beautiful?”

Nicky clambered up onto the railing beside them, staring at the horse with an enamoured expression on her face.

“He’s something,” Agatha replied, unwilling to give an inch. “Nicky, I sent you to the auction with Lilia to learn what to look for in cattle, not to buy a horse.”

“I know, but a few months ago you said it was about time that we started looking for my next prospect, something a little more challenging than Bullseye, something bigger, faster.” Nicky gestured to the horse in the arena. “This is it!”

“Nicky, when I said that, I meant we should look at horses that have already raced,” Agatha told her. “Something with more speed, yes, a push horse, sure. Not a young, green project horse that will take time to train up.”

“But you should see his bloodlines, Mama. His sire is the Adios Pantalones,” Nicky argued. “He’s gonna be a powerhouse, I just know it.”

“Someday, sure, but what about now?” Agatha demanded. “Now he’s… insane.”

“He’s not insane, he just got spooked when they got him out of the trailer,” Nicky argued. “He’s a sweet boy, Mama, I promise.”

Agatha doubted that, but Nicky’s words had reminded her of something else. “Oh, and that’s another thing. He. Nicky, you know I don’t want you riding a stallion—”

“I can handle it,” Nicky insisted.

“I don’t even want a stallion in my barn!” Agatha continued. “There’s a reason that the three stallions that I own are living in a breeding facility in Colorado. I have breeding mares here, Nicky. A stallion hellbent on breeding can be a dangerous thing in the right hands, much less in the hands of a sixteen year old.”

“I can handle—”

“Do not say you can handle it, you have no idea what you’re even dealing with!” Agatha exclaimed. She sighed and looked toward the stallion. He had slowed down, but was still pacing around the edge of the arena. Agatha climbed down from the gate and Nicky and Lilia followed her. Agatha grabbed a leadrope off a nearby hook and held it out to her daughter. “Here. If you think you can handle him, go get him.”

Nicky’s eyes widened. “What?”

“You bought these animals, they’re your responsibility,” Agatha told her. “Those calves look like they’re still bottle babies. I expect you to feed them every morning before school and every evening. I expect you to clean their stall and make sure they have plenty of food and water. And I expect you to figure out where we’re going to pasture them when they go outside.”

“I can do that,” Nicky said, nodding.

“I’m not done,” Agatha said. She pointed to the stallion. “He’s your responsibility. You feed him. You water him. You get him to and from the pasture. You arrange his training. If at any point I think he’s a danger to you or to my staff, we sell him. Do you understand?”

“Yes, ma’am,” Nicky replied.

“Starting now,” Agatha told her, gesturing to the rope. “I assume you already have a stall prepared for him?” Nicky nodded. “Well, go get him and put him away.”

Nicky turned toward the arena and took a deep breath before opening the gate and stepping into the open space. Agatha closed it behind her, but stood at the ready to open the gate quickly should the need arise.

“You sure that’s the best idea, sending her in there alone?” Lilia asked, her voice low.

“She doesn’t understand the risk, and she won’t until she’s experienced it,” Agatha murmured. “Plus she’s fucking stubborn.”

Lilia snorted. “I wonder where she got that from?”

She got it honest, Agatha thought, her eyes trained on the stallion as her daughter approached. The horse slowed his pacing as Nicky slowly stepped toward him.

“Easy,” Nicky said softly as she approached. “Hey, good boy, take it easy. That’s it.”

The horse stopped his pacing, coming to a complete stop and staring straight at Nicky. Instead of stepping toward the horse, Nicky also stopped, standing still just a few yards away from the horse. She kept her posture relaxed, the lead rope by her side.

And then she waited.

Agatha watched, her pulse pounding in her ears, hands shaking on the gate. Beside her Lilia was just as still, barely even breathing as they watched the girl and the horse. This was the make or break moment, and both of them knew it.

It took several minutes. Agatha felt as if it took a small eternity. But finally the horse took a slow step toward Nicky. Then he took another and another, until he came within arm’s length of the teenager. Still Nicky didn’t move, waiting patiently. Another minute passed, then another, and then the horse stepped forward, sniffing and prodding at Nicky’s shoulders and arms. The girl reached up and stroked the horse’s muzzle, smiling and laughing softly as she did so. Then, with zero fanfare, she clipped the lead rope to the horse’s halter and turned to lead him from the arena. The stallion followed like a well-trained golden retriever.

Agatha’s jaw dropped.

“Well I’ll be damned,” Lilia muttered. Then the older woman started laughing. “She’s your daughter alright, Harkness. Taming wild beasts through sheer tenacity, I tell you.”

Agatha’s slack-jawed expression quickly morphed into a deep frown. “She didn’t get that from me.”

Before Lilia could ask what she meant, Nicky was at the gate. Agatha opened it slowly, careful not to startle the horse again, but he looked perfectly content to follow the teenager wherever she might lead.

“You got a name for him?” Lilia asked, beaming as Nicky walked past.

“I think I’ll call him… Scratchy. Señor Scratchy Pants,” Nicky replied, reaching up and scratching beside the stallion’s ear. He leaned into her touch like a house cat. Nicky grinned proudly at her mother, but Agatha’s scowl had her quickly schooling her features. “I’ll just, um, go…”

Agatha’s gaze followed her daughter as she made her way up the ramp and into the main horse barn. Only once Nicky had rounded the corner out of her sight did Agatha release the breath she had been holding.

“She’s a good kid, Agatha,” Lilia assured her. “Just strongwilled.”

“I know. But damn if she doesn’t give me one hell of a headache sometimes,” Agatha replied with a yawn. “I think I’ll check out those heifers tomorrow. I need some rest.”

“Suit yourself,” Lilia told her. “But don’t forget to go by the chicken coop and see Ollie’s chickens.”

“Shit! I almost forgot about those,” Agatha hissed. Lilia laughed and Agatha glared at her. “I’m beginning to think you enjoy seeing me suffer.”

“I do not,” Lilia assured her. “I just think those kids are karma for how wild you were as a youngster.”

Agatha waved her off and headed outside. Instead of driving to the house, she headed around to the back of the barn. There, about a hundred yards from the round pen, was a large chicken coop with a wire-enclosed run attached. Thirty chickens already called the coop home, all various breeds that Agatha didn’t know the names of. There were chickens with tan feathers, chickens with black feathers, chickens with black and white stripes— Agatha hadn’t even known they made chickens with stripes, but here they were. There were even two silkie chickens, their fluffy white feathers making them stick out like sore thumbs amongst all the others.

Agatha opened the door to the coop and found her son bent over a large metal stock tank. His jeans were covered in wood shavings and dust, and because he wore them a size too big they were sliding halfway down his butt, revealing his superhero underwear. He had abandoned his shirt at some point, now only wearing a white tank top, and he had replaced his boots with his camouflage-print crocs. His brown hair was getting a bit too long; it stuck up in all directions on top while the back fell into short waves. Agatha was already dreading the battle his haircut would be, but it had to be done, because as soon as Ollie’s hair so much as brushed the back of his neck he would have a meltdown.

Agatha knelt in the shavings beside her son and rested her arms on the side of the tank. Inside a dozen— No, Agatha realized upon further inspection, two dozen, Lilia had fucking lied— chicks ran around the space, running from feeder to waterer on spindly little legs, chirping and cheeping as they went. Some had typical yellow feathers, but most were either black and yellow or gray in color.

“Those are lavender orpingtons,” Ollie said, pointing to the gray chicks. “The black ones are black orpingtons, and the yellow ones are buff orpingtons.”

“So we have orpingtons now?” Agatha asked.

Ollie nodded. “They lay lots of eggs.”

“Cool,” Agatha nodded. Like we need more fucking eggs. “Are they all orpingtons?”

Ollie pointed to a cluster of chicks that didn’t look any different than the rest to Agatha’s eyes, but clearly something about them stuck out to him. “These are Americaunas. They lay blue eggs.”

“That’s neat.”

“And these two are silkies,” Ollie told her, pointing to two chicks that might’ve been a bit fluffier than the others. “I thought Fluffy and Buffy needed some new friends.”

Agatha was fairly certain that Fluffy and Buffy didn’t give a shit, but she wasn’t about to tell her son that.

Agatha never intended to be a mother. She could distinctly remember being given a baby doll for her sixth birthday and throwing it into the trash as soon as the guests had left. She was so certain in her decision that she had started taking birth control pills at fourteen, two years before she ever started dating, and for nine years afterward those pills had worked just fine.

Then she got pregnant with Nicky. Allergies turned into a sinus infection, she took antibiotics, those antibiotics made her birth control fail, and a few months later she was holding a tiny person that was half her and half someone who was, at that time, several states away. But the moment Agatha looked into her daughter’s eyes, every belief about motherhood that she had held melted away, overtaken by sheer love and a need to protect the little life she had created.

Hormones were a hell of a thing.

After Nicky’s birth, Agatha focused on two things: her daughter, and her grandfather’s ranch. When Nicky was six, Agatha started dating the daughter of another ranch owner, and a year later she and Rachel got married. Their marriage meant another six thousand acres was added to the ranch’s property.

Oliver James Harkness was born two years later, and just like with her daughter, Agatha fell head over heels for her son, even if she hadn’t been the one to carry him. But as he got older, it became obvious that Ollie wasn’t like other children his age. He didn’t babble like other babies, and his facial expression didn’t change when he was being spoken to, though it was clear that he was observing and absorbing everything around him. His speech was delayed, but when he did finally speak at three years old, his pronunciation was perfect and he could string together entire sentences. He was an extremely picky eater, having complete meltdowns whenever his food wasn’t exactly how he liked it. But the biggest indicator that something wasn’t quite right was Ollie’s aversion to touch. Unlike his sister, who had clung to Agatha as a toddler, Ollie was more standoffish. If someone hugged him he would react violently, screaming and pushing the other person off him.

Ollie was diagnosed with autism when he was three and a half. Rachel filed for divorce before his fourth birthday. The reality was Rachel had been checked out of their marriage for a while; Ollie’s diagnosis was just the last excuse she needed to run. Agatha let her go. She made sure Rachel signed away all of her parental rights so she could never come back into their son’s life again. And because Jen had drawn up an airtight prenup before Rachel and Agatha ever said “I do,” Rachel had no claim to the ranch or continued alimony, just one lump payment of half a million dollars for every year of marriage. Agatha cut her a check and sent her on her way. Last Agatha heard she was living somewhere in Rhode Island.

Ollie had been the only good thing to come from her failed marriage. Well, Ollie and the property that was added to the ranch. Not such a bad deal, in Agatha’s mind.

Ollie looked up from the chicks finally, gazing toward Agatha’s face, but his blue eyes didn’t quite meet hers. “Is grandma dead?”

Agatha choked back a laugh at his flat delivery of the question. “No, grandma’s not dead,” she replied. Unfortunately.

“Oh,” Ollie said. His brow wrinkled in thought for a moment, but quickly returned to normal. He looked back toward the chicks. “What’s for dinner?”

“Um, I don’t know,” Agatha replied. “I think Remy might be making tacos tonight.”

“Can I have noodles instead?” he asked.

“Yeah, of course,” Agatha nodded. She studied her son for another moment before speaking again. “Hey, Ollie, I could really use a hug right now. Any chance you feel up to it?”

“Not really,” he said, nose wrinkling.

“Okay,” Agatha said, a bit disappointed but she would live. With years of therapy, Ollie was much more open to physical contact and affection, but she always made sure to ask first, and sometimes he simply wasn’t up for it.

“I can do a high five,” Ollie suggested instead, raising his hand expectantly.

Agatha grinned and gave him a high five. Ollie beamed at her for a moment before looking back down at his chickens.

“Love you, Ollie,” Agatha told him, standing to leave and go into the house.

“Love you, too, Mama.”

Chapter 3: Tori

Chapter Summary

Tori’s first day at Westview High. I wonder who she’ll meet?

Montana was called Big Sky Country, but so far the sky seemed like the only big thing in Westview. Tori’s high school in San Diego had nearly three thousand students. Westview High had around two hundred. The high school was really just one building on a campus that also housed the elementary and middle school. There was one parking lot, one auditorium, and one gymnasium shared between all three schools. It had been this way since her mom went to school here, and in a tiny town like Westview, it would likely never change.

Tori tugged nervously at her sweater as she followed her mother into the building. Her mom had made her change twice that morning. She said something about first impressions, but Tori thought her mom seemed nervous about going back to her alma mater. Rio was dressed even nicer than normal, in a crisp emerald green western blouse that she tucked into her nicest jeans. Tori had foregone her usual loose plaid for a chunky green and black cardigan over a Conan Gray t-shirt and a pair of baggy black jeans.

Inside the office, Rio stepped over to the receptionist’s desk. The redheaded woman sitting at the desk didn’t even look up from her computer screen.

“Good morning,” Rio greeted. “I have an appointment to enroll my daughter this morning. Rio and Victoria Vidal.”

The receptionist’s head shot up as soon as she heard Rio’s name, her ponytail bobbing as she did so. “Rio?”

Her mom’s eyes widened. “Wanda?”

The redheaded woman squealed in excitement as she stood and rounded the desk, pulling Rio into a hug.

“Oh my goodness! What are you doing back in Westview?” the receptionist asked.

“My dad’s been having a hard go of it, so we’ve moved back in with him,” Rio told her. “I’ll be working out of Doctor McCoy’s practice.”

“Oh, that’ll be wonderful! It’s so good to see you again!” The redhead turned to look at Tori. “Oh, and this must be your daughter. Victoria, did you say?”

“I go by Tori,” Tori replied, reaching out and shaking the woman’s hand.

“Well, it’s a pleasure to meet you, Tori,” the receptionist said. “I’m Wanda Maximoff, you can call me Mrs. Maximoff, and I’m the data manager for the middle and high school. I’ll be the one processing your paperwork and getting your classes scheduled today.”

“I take it you two know each other?” Tori asked, looking between Mrs. Maximoff and her mom.

“We went to school together,” Rio replied. She looked at Wanda curiously. “Mrs. Maximoff?”

“I married Vision,” Mrs. Maximoff told her. “Going on eighteen years now. But we decided that I would keep my last name, and that the kids would have it, since I’m the last of my family and he’s got so many siblings.”

“That’s very… modern,” Rio commented.

“It was the talk of the town, that’s for sure,” Mrs. Maximoff said with a laugh. “Not that they have much else to talk about around here besides horses and cattle. I’m sure once it gets out that you’re back in town, it’ll cause quite a stir.”

“I hope not,” Rio sighed. “Anyway, this meeting with the principal… Who’s this Mrs. Davis? I don’t recognize the name?”

Mrs. Maximoff laughed. “Oh, well, that’s interesting because—”

“I certainly recognize you.”

When Tori was five years old she had fallen at the park and hit her head on the concrete. Blood flowed from the wound, and even with all her training, Rio had gone white as a sheet and panicked until a stranger called 911 and they were taken to the emergency room. That was the first time in her life Tori had ever seen her mother look afraid of anything.

But here, in the office of Westview High, Rio’s face went white again. Her eyes were big as saucers as she turned around and faced the principal. Tori turned to see the principal as well, and she couldn’t figure out what the fuss was about. She was a tiny woman with graying blonde hair that hung just past her chin. She wore a floral blouse under a pale green cardigan and plain gray slacks. She looked like someone’s grandmother, and yet Rio was looking at her like she was afraid for her life.

“Ms. Hart?” Rio asked.

“It’s Mrs. Davis now, dear,” the principal replied. “You two can come right this way. Wanda, can you bring the enrollment forms?”

“Of course, Mrs. Davis,” the redhead replied.

As Tori and Rio followed the principal down the hall to her office, Rio leaned over and whispered in Tori’s ear. “I am so sorry.”

“Why?” Tori asked, but before Rio could reply, they were in the principal’s office. They sat at the desk across from Mrs. Davis, who looked at them with a wide smile.

“Well, Victoria, I certainly hope you’re not sent to this office as frequently as your mother was when she was a student here,” Mrs. Davis said.

Tori looked from the principal to her mother with surprise. She had always thought her mother was the perfect student, a straight-A rule follower. She had no idea that she had ever gotten in enough trouble to be sent to the principal’s office, much less frequently.

“Um, no, I try not to,” Tori replied.

“Well, that’s good to hear,” Mrs. Davis laughed. “I always told Rio that she was far too bright to be getting in so much trouble. She just got too close to some bad influences.”

Tori glanced at her mother and Rio’s face was bright pink with embarrassment. Tori looked at her lap and smirked; she was totally bringing this up at dinner. She knew her grandpa would love spilling the beans, and was surprised he hadn’t already.

Mrs. Maximoff joined them with a stack of paperwork and a laptop. She handed the largest part of the stack to Rio before sitting beside Tori and opening the laptop.

“Here, you can fill out the contact information and health documentation,” the redhead said. “Tori and I will figure out her class schedule. What classes were you taking at your last school, Tori?”

“I had Honors English, AP Pre-Calc, Pottery, and Phys Ed,” Tori replied.

“Okay, let’s see what we have available,” Mrs. Maximoff said, peering at the computer screen. “We don’t have a pottery class, I’m afraid, just Visual Arts, but I can put you in that class for first period. Then we have an Honors English section for your second block.” She looked up at Tori with a smile. “You’ll be in those classes with my son, Billy.”

“You have kids?” Rio asked, looking up from the form she was filling out.

“Twin boys, Billy and Tommy. They’re the same age as Tori,” Wanda replied.

“Small world,” Rio muttered, looking back down at her paperwork.

“After lunch you’ll go to Pre-Calculus,” Mrs. Maximoff continued.

“I was in an Advanced Placement class at my old school,” Tori corrected.

“Unfortunately our Pre-Calculus class here doesn’t qualify as an Advanced Placement course,” Mrs. Davis told her. “It’s still a very advanced course. Most of our students don’t go that far in the math program.”

“Yeah, but if it’s not AP then it doesn’t count as college credit,” Tori explained.

“I’m sorry, dear, but we simply don’t offer an AP Pre-Calculus course here,” Mrs. Davis said.

“But—” Tori began.

“It’s fine,” Rio said, cutting her off abruptly. “Regular Pre-Calc will be fine.”

Tori sighed. “Yeah, fine.”

“And finally you’ll have Health and PE fourth period,” Mrs. Maximoff continued. She looked at her computer and cringed. “Oh, you’ve chosen an interesting week to enroll.”

“What do you mean?” Tori asked.

“It’s sex-ed week,” Mrs. Maximoff told her, cringing slightly with empathy.

“I already learned all that at my old school,” Tori said.

“Well, we do have a form that your mother can sign to opt you out of it,” Mrs. Davis told her. “Then you could go sit in the library during the lesson.”

“No,” Rio said, before Tori could get another word in edgewise. “She’ll take part in the lesson.”

Tori turned to her mother with wide eyes. “But Mami—”

“I know it will be awkward, but being the new kid will be hard enough without you going off to the library during the sex-ed lesson, trust me,” Rio told her. “Besides, you never know, you might learn something here that they didn’t teach you in California. And you can never be too careful with something like that.”

Tori slumped back in her seat and crossed her arms. “Didn’t make you careful enough.”

Rio’s mouth opened and closed a few times before she schooled her features and turned back to the principal and data manager. “Well… Anyway, I think this one’s finished.”

She handed the first form to the principal, who put in her glasses and peered at the paper. “It looks like you’ve missed the section about Victoria’s— I’m sorry— Tori’s second parent?”

“That’s because there is no second parent,” Rio replied brusquely. “Not in the picture, anyway.”

Tori knew very little about her other parent. She knew it was a woman, her mom had told her that much. And she knew her mom had found out she was pregnant right after she started veterinary school. But she didn’t know if her other mother was one of her mom’s classmates, an ex-girlfriend, a one-night stand… For sixteen years her mom had been completely tight-lipped about the situation.

The only thing Tori knew was that any time she mentioned her other mother around her grandfather, he would get angry. He made her feel like even asking was her being ungrateful for everything he and her mom had done for her. So she didn’t bring it up anymore.

But sometimes she wondered…

“I’ll just make a note for our records,” Mrs. Davis said, writing a quick N/A in the second parent box on the form.

“Tori?” Mrs. Maximoff said, drawing her attention again. “You look like you’ve brought all your things. Did you want to start classes today? Or were you planning to wait until tomorrow?”

“Today’s fine,” Tori said, clutching the strap on her backpack. “I didn’t bring much, just a notebook and some pencils. And my sketchbook in case I got bored. I wasn’t sure if I should bring my laptop, so I left it at home.”

“We have school devices you can use, but if you’d rather bring your own we can get you added to the network tomorrow,” Mrs. Davis assured her. “But Mrs. Maximoff is right, we should get you to class. Wanda, why don’t you call Billy up to be her guide for today? I bet they would get along well.”

When Billy Maximoff entered the office, Tori immediately understood what the principal meant. He didn’t look much like his mom, with dark curly hair and dark eyes that were lined with heavy black liner. His clothing was dark and baggy, much like what Tori was wearing. Tori wanted to be offended. Just because they shared a similar style didn’t mean they would be friends.

“Hey Mom, hi Mrs. Davis. Mr. Kirby said you needed me for something?” he said, as he entered the office.

“We have a new student,” Mrs. Davis explained, gesturing to where Tori and Rio were still sitting at the desk.

“Hi, I’m Billy,” the boy said, pulling his hand from his pocket to wave awkwardly.

“This is Tori and her mom, Rio,” Wanda told him. “Rio and I went to school together.”

“Oh, that’s cool,” Billy nodded.

“Tori has Visual Art and English with you,” Mrs. Davis continued. “We were hoping you could show her around, help her find her classes, and introduce her to the teachers?”

“Yeah, sure, I can do that,” Billy agreed. He looked at Tori. “Are you ready to go now, or…?”

“Uh, yeah, I guess so,” Tori nodded, standing up from her chair. She looked back toward her mother uncertainly.

“I’ll see you this afternoon, mija,” Rio assured her. “Be good.”

Tori rolled her eyes. “Yes, Mami.”

She tossed her backpack over one shoulder and followed Billy out of the office. Once they were out of the office, the boy looked at her awkwardly.

“So, uh, where are you from?”

“San Diego,” Tori told him.

“Cool,” he said. “I like your t-shirt, by the way.”

“You listen to Ethel Cain?”

“Obviously,” Billy replied, rolling his eyes. “What’s your favorite song? Mine’s American Teenager.”

“Oooo, it’s so hard to decide,” Tori said, thinking. “But I think it’s Sun Bleached Flies.”

“That’s a good one,” Billy agreed.

Tori grinned. So maybe some stereotypes were right. Sometimes.

Mr. Kirby was an old man with gray hair and obviously failing eyesight. Tori wasn’t entirely sure how he could see the artwork his students were doing, but he was kind and patient and perhaps that made all the difference. He assigned her a locker, showed her around the classroom, and then looked through her sketchbook while the other students finished the projects they were working on.

“You have a good eye for movement,” he said, pointing to one of her drawings. Tori nodded, still completely flabbergasted that his cataract-covered eyes could see anything at all.

Second period was English, and Tori quickly learned that it was Billy’s favorite class. Again, some stereotypes were based on fact. The teacher, Mrs. Schaeffer, had a small pride flag on her desk, and a poster for the school’s GSA club posted behind her desk. Along with regular chairs and desks, she had several bean bags, a sofa, and other more comfortable seating options positioned around her classroom, and instead of using the overhead lights she had fairy lights and lamps around the room. 

After English was lunch. They headed to the large cafeteria on the far side of the school, and they were soon joined by Billy’s boyfriend, Teddy, and their friend, Yelena.

“What other classes do you have?” Teddy asked, peering over Billy’s shoulder at Tori’s schedule. He had blond hair with lime green streaks throughout.

“Pre-Calc with Mr. Strange and then Health and PE with Coach Danvers,” Tori replied.

“Oh, it’s Dr. Strange,” Yelena corrected, rolling her eyes.

“Yeah, make sure you call him doctor, he gets pissed if you don’t,” Billy told her.

“Honestly, he’s kind of an asshole either way,” Teddy said, dipping his fries in an obscene amount of ketchup. “So call him whatever you want.”

“Noted,” Tori nodded. “What about Coach Danvers?”

“Oh, she’s awesome,” Teddy assured her. “She coaches softball in the spring and girls’ soccer in the fall. And she helps Mrs. Schaeffer with the GSA. You’ll love her.”

“Sucks that you’re starting during sex-ed week, though,” Yelena said, her accent thick. “I had it this morning. Coach Danvers is cool, but some of the jocks were being stupid.”

As if summoned, a boy with shaggy brown hair wearing a letterman jacket plopped down in the seat beside Billy, snagging some fries off his plate.

“Which jocks were being stupid?” he asked, placing his own plate down. He had double servings of everything on his plate, though he was just as lean built as Billy.

“Just some of the idiots on the football team,” Yelena told him.

“Hey, eat your own fries!” Billy exclaimed.

“I have a track meet today, I need the calories!” the boy explained.

Billy moved his plate closer to Teddy and away from the newcomer. “So are you eating with us today?”

“You wish,” he said. “Just waiting for my girlfriend. And I wanted to meet the new kid.”

Billy sighed. “Tori, this is my brother, Tommy. Tommy, this is Tori.”

Tommy flashed her a megawatt smile. Tori rolled her eyes.

“So, Tori, is my brother being a good tour guide so far?” Tommy asked, elbowing Billy in the ribs. “If not, I’d be happy to show you the parts of the school that goody two shoes here doesn’t know about.”

“Dude,” Teddy said, scowling.

“You already have a girlfriend,” Yelena reminded him.

“I’m not asking her to be my girlfriend,” Tommy argued.

“Good, because I’m not interested,” Tori told him. “I’m pretty sure we play for the same team, if you know what I mean.”

For a second Tommy definitely did not know what she meant, but Tori could see the moment the realization hit him.

“Oh. Ohhh! Okay, I get it,” Tommy said, laughing. “That’s cool. And hey, obviously I support, right?” He gestured to his brother and Teddy. “Love is love, baby.”

“Idiot,” Yelena muttered.

“Speaking of love, my girl’s here,” Tommy said, standing and picking up his plate. “Later, dorks!”

Tori watched him speed toward the door where he met a whole gaggle of girls.

“I cannot believe you two are related,” Yelena told Billy.

“I shared a womb with him and sometimes I can’t believe it,” Billy agreed.

“You’re twins?” Tori asked.

“Yep,” Billy sighed. “Clearly not identical. The genetic distribution was a complete split.”

“Which one of them is his girlfriend?” Tori asked, looking at the group of girls surrounding Tommy.

“The brunette in the pink jacket,” Teddy told her. “Morgan Stark. Her dad’s some big shot tech mogul from California.”

“It’s not gonna last,” Billy said, his words muffled by the bite of sandwich he was still chewing. “I don’t even think he likes her that much.”

“Then why date her?” Tori asked.

“I dunno. ‘Cause he’s an idiot?” Billy shrugged.

“She is very pretty,” Yelena remarked.

“Pretty bitchy,” Billy muttered.

“Your brother just likes fast things,” Teddy surmised. “Not surprising he’s dating a barrel racer.”

Tori’s head shot up. “She’s a barrel racer?”

The three of them groaned in unison.

“Not you, too,” Billy said.

“What?” Tori asked, looking around the table. When no one spoke up, she repeated herself. “What?”

“You see that girl?” Teddy asked, pointing to one of the girls sitting near Tommy and his girlfriend. This one had long brown hair and bright blue eyes. “Her name’s Kate Bishop. She was Yelena’s girlfriend for a little while last year. Then she started barrel racing and started hanging out with Morgan and her posse. A week later she broke up with Yelena and stopped hanging out with us at all.”

“That’s terrible,” Tori said with a frown. Yelena stabbed at her food with a bit more force than necessary.

“And the girl on the end,” Teddy continued, “that’s Cassie Lang. Her grandpa teaches science. She used to be nice to everyone. Then she started barrel racing and hanging out with Morgan. Now she’s just as much of a world class bitch as the rest of them.”

Tori looked back at the gaggle of girls. There was one more girl amongst them, sitting beside Morgan. There was something weirdly familiar about her, though Tori couldn’t quite put her finger on what. She had long brown hair with blonde highlights at the ends, skin a shade lighter than Tori’s, brown eyes… Tori was sure that she had never seen the girl, but there was something about her…

“Oh, don’t waste your time on her,” Billy said. “She definitely wouldn’t be interested.”

“That’s not— I’m not even thinking that!” Tori defended.

“Sure,” Teddy teased.

“I wasn’t,” Tori huffed. “Who is she, anyway?”

“Nicky Harkness,” Yelena replied. “Her family owns the biggest ranch in the valley. Huge outfit.”

“She a bitch, too?” Tori asked.

“No, worse,” Billy replied. “She’s fucking psycho.”

“Yeah, her friends will just hurt you emotionally, call you names, maybe post some made up shit on social media,” Teddy said. “Nicky will fuck you up.”

“She got suspended last year for breaking one of Ava Lance’s teeth,” Yelena told her.

“Yeah, but in her defence, Ava did say something mean about Nicky’s little brother,” Billy added.

“She broke her tooth, Billy,” Teddy reiterated.

“I know,” Billy said. “I’m just saying, if I had an autistic brother and someone called him the R-word, I’d want to beat them up, too.”

“Same,” Yelena agreed.

“She also cussed out a teacher last year,” Teddy added. “Mrs. Grant.”

“I thought her mom was the one who cussed out Mrs. Grant?” Yelena asked.

“I’m pretty sure Nicky cussed her out and then when Mrs. Davis called her mom in to discuss things, her mom cussed out Mrs. Davis,” Billy said.

“Girl’s got brass balls, I’ll give her that,” Yelena commented.

“I’d just hate to be on the receiving end of her temper,” Teddy noted.

They all nodded in agreement.

Chapter 4: Nicky

Chapter Summary

Meet Nicky. Also, a brief explanation of fantastic biology that explains how Tori and Nicky exist.

Chapter Notes

CW: homophobic language, also basic sexual anatomy descriptions in case that yucks your yum

Nicky could name half a dozen different places she would rather be:

  • Riding Scratchy
  • At her internship
  • Mucking stalls
  • Moving cattle in the rain
  • Getting her braces tightened
  • Dinner with her grandmother

Nicky wasn’t religious, she didn’t believe in hell, but if it did exist, she was pretty sure it couldn’t be worse than Dr. Strange’s third period pre-calc class. Nicky didn’t like math, wasn’t even that good at it, but her mother insisted that she take the most advanced classes. Nicky only agreed because she knew that she needed the course to get into a good college. So there she was, trapped for an hour and a half, fighting the after-lunch slump while Dr. Strange explained math badly to a class of fifteen students who would all rather be anywhere else.

Nicky sat slumped over her desk, staring out the window instead of paying attention. Her entire mouth was hurting from her orthodontist appointment that morning, and her stomach was growling because she’d only eaten a few bites at lunch. It didn’t really matter if she was paying attention anyway. Class always started the same way: Dr. Strange berating them over not doing their homework correctly. Nevermind that he didn’t teach the concepts well enough for them to know how to do it. She almost completely missed the new student entering the room, only looking up to pay attention when Dr. Strange clapped his hands to draw the entire class’s attention.

“Attention, everyone, we have a new student!” he announced brightly, gesturing to the new girl dramatically. The girl's face was beet red as the entire class stared at her, looking down at her feet instead of up at all the people. Dr. Strange was either completely ignorant of the embarrassment he was causing, or he enjoyed it.

Nicky thought it was probably the latter.

“Introduce yourself!” Dr. Strange ordered, looking at the girl expectantly. She raised her head sharply and looked at him with wide blue eyes.

“Wh— what do I say?” she asked.

“Tell us your name,” Dr. Strange scoffed.

“Oh, uh, I’m Tori,” she said, giving an awkward little wave.

“The roster said Victoria,” Dr. Strange argued, picking up the paper and squinting down at the small print. “Victoria Vidal.”

“Yeah, I mean, yes,” the girl said. “But I go by Tori.”

“Well, Victoria,” Dr. Strange said pointedly, “tell us a bit more about yourself.”

“Um, I’m from San Diego,” the girl— Tori— replied. “My pronouns are she/her.” Nicky saw Dr. Strange roll his eyes at that. “And, um… I have a pet bearded dragon named Draco.”

“Well that’s… interesting,” Dr. Strange said, though his tone sounded bored. “Does anyone have any questions for Victoria?”

Nicky could see the girl bristle at the use of her full name. The rest of the class sat in silence, but Nicky raised her hand.

“Yes, Nicole?” Dr. Strange called.

“Hi Tori, I’m Nicky,” she said. The girl immediately brightened at the use of her nickname. “I was just wondering, what’s the coolest thing you’ve ever done?”

“Oh, um…” Tori stuttered. “I guess, uh…” Suddenly her expression turned sure and she smiled. “One time I got to go to work with my mom and help bottle feed two baby mountain lions.”

Nicky’s brows shot up. “Really?”

Tori nodded. “Really. My mom was a vet at San Diego Zoo before we moved here. I have pictures on my phone if you wanna—”

“Phones aren’t allowed in class, Victoria,” Dr. Strange interrupted.

“Oh, sorry,” Tori replied, chagrined.

“Well, Victoria, why don’t you take the empty seat beside Nicole, and we’ll get started with class,” Dr. Strange said.

Before Nicky could object— not that Dr. Strange would care— the new girl sat in the chair beside her. She watched out of the corner of her eyes as Tori draped her sweater across the back of her chair and then pulled a notebook and pencil out of her backpack. Once she was settled, she turned to Nicky.

“Hi,” she whispered, since Dr. Strange had already started droning on.

“Hi,” Nicky muttered back.

“Thanks for that.”

Nicky glanced at her again. “No problem. Strange is an asshole.”

Tori grinned. “Definitely.”

Nicky tried to focus on the example she was supposed to be copying, but there was still something on her mind. “Did you really bottle feed mountain lions?”

“Yeah, I did,” Tori nodded.

Nicky bit her lip. “That’s cool.”

Dr. Strange cleared his throat loudly, and when Nicky looked back toward the board, he was glaring at the two of them. She shot the new girl a look, and Tori rolled her eyes before looking back at her notebook sheepishly. Nicky grinned.

Her new seat partner might be tolerable, at least.

A few minutes later, something poked Nicky’s leg beneath the edge of the desk. She looked down to see Tori poking her with the corner of her cell phone. Nicky peered down at the screen to see a picture of two sandy-furred felines, one held by Tori and the other by a woman who looked like an older version of Tori.

“Holy shit,” Nicky exclaimed in a whisper. Tori smiled proudly before quickly tucking her phone into her pocket.

Okay, so maybe her new seat partner was cool. Kind of.

Ninety neverending minutes later, the bell signaling class change rang. A class full of nearly-comatose students came to life in the blink of an eye, shooting up out of their seats and hurrying out the door before they even got their belongings fully stuffed into their backpacks. Nobody wanted to stay in Strange’s classroom any longer than necessary.

Nicky was seconds from bolting when she realized that the new girl was looking at a wrinkled piece of paper with a confused expression. Nicky looked around, hoping there might be someone else who could help the girl, but everyone else had already left. She sighed and turned to her seatmate.

“What class do you have next?” she asked.

Tori looked up at her. “Health and PE with Coach Danvers.”

“I’m actually headed there next, too,” Nicky replied, shouldering her bag. “Just follow me.”

Tori shoved her schedule into her pocket and followed her. “Thanks.”

“No problem,” Nicky assured her.

Nicky headed off at a brisk pace, partly to get out of Strange’s classroom, partly to get to class faster. The gym and attached health classroom were on the far side of the campus, and even though the school wasn’t very large, it was sometimes difficult to navigate the crowded halls and make it to fourth period on time. Coach Danvers was one of the more understanding teachers at the school, but Nicky didn’t want to push her luck.

Contrary to popular opinion, Nicky didn’t try to get in trouble. Trouble just had a way of finding her. She didn’t start fights, but if someone messed with her first, she would finish them. She didn’t mean to be disrespectful, but she could be argumentative, because if someone said something she disagreed with, she couldn’t just keep her mouth shut.
