She cared about school and tried to get good grades, because she knew it was important for her future, but she also knew that test scores and report cards weren’t the most important things in life, and she refused to treat them as if they were.

Nicky wasn’t sure what to make of the new girl. The way she dressed told Nicky that she wanted people to think she was different or edgy, that she didn’t care what anyone else thought of her. In Nicky’s experience, it was people like that who actually cared the most. They just wanted to try and control the narrative. It wasn’t a bad thing, everyone did it. Nicky was just smart enough to see through it.

They made it to the health classroom just as the tardy bell rang. Nicky pointed Tori toward the teacher at the front of the room, then made her way to her usual seat. Morgan was already in the seat beside hers, and she kicked Nicky’s chair out from under the table for her as she approached.

“You hanging out with the new kid, Harkness?” Morgan asked, raising a brow.

Nicky shrugged. “Strange made her sit beside me in pre-calc, and she asked me where the health room was. I told her she could just follow me.”

“So nice of you,” Morgan remarked, but it wasn’t a compliment.

Nicky’s family might own the largest ranch in the area, but the Starks were the richest people in town. Morgan’s father was a former tech mogul from Silicon Valley. By all accounts he was a billionaire. When Morgan was five, he and his family moved to Westview and bought a ranch. Then he bought another, and another. Nicky knew that he had offered to buy the Harkness ranch at least three times from her grandfather, but he had refused. Now Tony Stark owned the second biggest ranch in the area, but he also owned land used for other things. Housing developments, department stores, strip malls… Stark was responsible for much of the town’s growth over the last decade. Nicky had never completely understood why all that was such a bad thing, but her grandfather and other ranchers like him hated the development.

All that meant that when Morgan first started at Westview Elementary, she was treated like a pariah. Nicky hadn’t had much to do with her, either, for the first couple of years she lived in Westview. But then Morgan started barrel racing, and Nicky would see her at the local rodeos and competitions. That was how their friendship had started, and through the years they had gradually added Kate and Cassie to their friend group.

Morgan wasn’t exactly popular. She could be abrasive, to put it nicely. Some people would probably call her stuck up. But Nicky could relate to those things as well, so she stuck with her. If people didn’t like them, then those people could just fuck off.

“Her mom’s a vet,” Nicky told Morgan, glancing up to where Tori was still talking to Coach Danvers. “She showed me a picture of these mountain lion cubs that she got to bottle feed when her mom worked at a zoo.”

“So?” Morgan asked, unimpressed.

Nicky shrugged. “I thought it was cool.”

Morgan snorted. “Yeah, right. Look at her. She’s a freak.”

Morgan’s voice climbed in volume with every word, and just like that the entire class’s eyes were on them, including Tori’s. Nicky shifted uncomfortably in her seat.

“Nicky?” Morgan prodded.

“Yeah, she’s a freak,” Nicky agreed, suddenly feeling hot all over.

The lights turned off so that the only light in the room was the interactive board behind Coach Danvers. Nicky watched as Tori made her way down the aisle between desks, getting closer to them. When she caught her eye, Tori just frowned and kept walking past her, sitting two rows behind her with Teddy, the green-haired boy that was dating Tommy’s brother.

“Looks like she’s gay, too,” Morgan whispered judgmentally. Nicky clenched her fists beneath her desk.

“Attention, everyone! Time to get this show on the road!” Coach Danvers shouted, her voice carrying over the overcrowded classroom. “Today’s topic is The Female Reproductive System. We’ll be discussing all of the organs and body parts associated with the female reproductive system, common diseases and disorders that can affect the female reproductive system, as well as pregnancy and contraception.”

A football player named Aiden raised his hand. “Why do the guys have to learn about this?”

“Aiden, do you have a mother?” Coach Danvers asked.

“Yeah, duh,” Aiden scoffed.

“Sisters?”

“Yes,” Aiden replied.

“Do you have a girlfriend?”

Aiden’s cheeks turned red. “Uh… No.”

“Do you want one? Maybe even a wife someday?”

“Someday, sure,” he replied.

“Then it’s probably a good idea to have some idea of what’s going on with their anatomy, don’t you think?” Coach Danvers told him. “Tomorrow we’ll cover male reproductive anatomy, and the girls have to learn about that, too, don’t you worry.

“Now, I realize that some of today’s material may make you uncomfortable,” Coach Danvers continued. “If you need to step out at any time you are welcome to do so. However, I urge you to pay attention, be respectful, and use the information you learn today to make safe decisions moving forward. Any questions or concerns before we begin?”

For the next hour, Coach Danvers went through a slideshow that covered the internal and external female anatomy. The slides had cutesy drawings of the organs, because they weren’t about to show photos of actual breasts and vulva to a class of high schoolers. Once they had covered all of the parts and pieces, they moved on to pregnancy. Coach Danvers went through a slideshow showing fetal development through every month of pregnancy, and along with the little drawings there were some actual ultrasound images.

“Now, someday many of you might decide to become parents,” Coach Danvers said. “But until you’re ready, there are ways to prevent unintended pregnancies…”

Nicky’s eyes drooped in the dim light as Coach Danvers talked about condoms, even going so far as to demonstrate how to put one on. She briefly debated which was worse, pre-calc or sex-ed, but in the end decided that watching Coach Danvers put a condom on a banana was at least entertaining.

Aiden raised his hand again. “What if they don’t make condoms big enough?”

Nicky rolled her eyes. She could almost see Coach Danvers fighting the urge to roll hers, but the teacher was a professional. Instead of answering, Coach Danvers picked up another foil packet. She ripped it open, took out the condom, and carefully began rolling it over her hand. It stretched and stretched as she rolled it up further and further, until her entire arm was enclosed in latex.

“I don’t think that will be a problem,” she replied, her voice even. The football player blushed, and some of the girls giggled. “Any other questions?”

Once she was finished talking about condoms, Coach Danvers discussed IUDs and various hormonal birth controls. Her mom had insisted Nicky start taking the pill a year ago, even if she didn’t even have a boyfriend yet, so Nicky mostly zoned out of the presentation.

“It’s important to remember with hormonal types of birth control that they can prevent pregnancy, but they don’t prevent the transmission of sexually transmitted infections, which we’ll cover later this week,” Coach Danvers told them. “Also, they only reduce the chance of pregnancy. No birth control method, be it hormonal or barrier method, is one hundred percent effective. Class, what is the only one hundred percent effective method to prevent pregnancy and STIs?”

“Abstinence,” Nicky monotoned along with the entire rest of the class. Except for one dumbass in the back of the room who shouted, “Lesbianism!”

The class erupted into laughter, but the glare on Coach Danvers’s face silenced it almost immediately.

“Actually, that’s not entirely true,” Coach Danvers said, once the class was silent. “It’s been widely known for much of human history that some women have the ability to impregnate other women. Due to the stigmatization of same-sex relationships, however, not much was known about this phenomenon until the last decade or so.”

Nicky was suddenly wide awake, fully focused on what Coach Danvers was saying.

“Some females have what is called the Female Androgen Fertilization Zero gene,” Coach Danvers continued. “The FAF-0 gene causes those women to produce what are called female fertilization cells, or FFCs. Like sperm cells, FFCs contain genetic material that can fertilize the egg of another woman. The FFCs are produced in the tissue surrounding the clitoris, so fertilization is possible with direct genital on genital contact or when an AFAB person touches themselves and then their partner.”

“What’s a clitoris?” asked the dumbass in the back.

“Something you’ll never find,” Nicky muttered.

“You would know the answer to that question if you were paying attention during the presentation, Kevin,” Coach Danvers said with a fake smile.

Tori raised her hand. “How does a woman know she has the gene? And does that mean that lesbians need to take birth control, too?”

“A genetic test that screens for the FAF-0 gene was developed about ten years ago, and it is highly recommended that women in same-sex relationships get tested for the FAF-0 gene before engaging in any kind of sexual intercourse, so that they can take precautions to prevent pregnancy, if they so desire,” Coach Danvers answered. “Hormonal birth control can prevent ovulation and prevent the production of FFCs. But again, it’s not one hundred percent effective all the time.”

“So… Hypothetically,” Teddy began, “if two women in a same-sex relationship both had this gene, could they both get pregnant by each other? Possibly even at the same time?”

“It’s possible,” Coach Danvers nodded. “Extremely unlikely considering the statistics, but not impossible.”

The words were barely out of Coach Danvers’s mouth when the final dismissal bell rang out.

“If any of you have more questions or you think of one after school, feel free to email me,” Coach Danvers shouted over the cacophony of students packing up and hurrying out the door. “Have a good afternoon and drive safe!”

Nicky packed up her things and walked alongside Morgan as they made their way from the classroom out to the courtyard.

“Well, that was disgusting,” Morgan announced, gagging dramatically.

“Yeah, it was gross,” Nicky agreed.

“I can’t believe she taught us about that,” Morgan continued.

“Safe sex and STIs? I’m pretty sure that’s, like, required by the state,” Nicky said.

“Not that,” Morgan said. “That last part. You know, the stuff about women getting other women pregnant.”

Nicky frowned. “She just wants to make sure everyone is safe. No accidental babies, you know?”

“They shouldn’t teach that stuff in school,” Morgan declared. “When I tell my father, he’ll go ballistic. I wouldn’t be surprised if he goes to the school board.”

“Don’t you think that’s taking it a little far?” Nicky asked. “Coach Danvers is a good teacher. She’s just trying to take care of everyone, even the gay kids.”

“She shouldn’t be teaching anything that promotes that kind of lifestyle, and she’s not a very good teacher if she does,” Morgan stated with the kind of moral superiority that she was known for.

“Morgan, you do realize my mom was married to another woman and had my brother with her wife?” Nicky reminded her.

“Yeah, and you see how that turned out,” Morgan shrugged. “Divorced. Rachel left your mom and her kid. She clearly has low morals, and it’s just sad that your mom fell for that.”

Nicky unclenched her fists that had balled up at her sides. “Sure… yeah.”

“Anyway, you wanna go grab something to eat? Maybe do some shopping?”

“I can’t, I have to take Ollie home. And then I have a bunch of chores and homework to do,” Nicky replied.

Morgan frowned. “I thought when you got your truck you’d be able to hang out more.”

Nicky shrugged. “Sorry, can’t help it.”

“You’re no fun,” Morgan huffed. Then she saw Cassie and Kate from across the parking lot and waved at them. “Well, I’ll see you tomorrow, anyway.”

“Yep, see you tomorrow,” Nicky replied, waving to her other friends before turning away from the high school parking lot and walking toward the elementary school.

She didn’t agree with what Morgan had said, and yet Morgan was the one person she couldn’t seem to speak up against. She was Nicky’s oldest friend, and even though through the years they had developed different opinions and personalities, ten years of friendship had to count for something. Besides, Nicky wasn’t exactly a social butterfly. If she wasn’t friends with Morgan and the others, she wouldn’t have any friends at all.

Ollie and his teacher were waiting at the corner of the elementary building. Ms. Grey smiled at Nicky as she approached, but Ollie seemed too preoccupied with a fidget toy to pay attention.

“You ready to go, Ollie?” Nicky asked.

“I’m ready,” Ollie replied, still looking down at the little cube he was playing with.

“He won the class spelling bee today and the prize was a Needoh cube,” Mrs. Grey told her. “Please tell your mother that he had a very good day today.”

“Will do,” Nicky assured her. “C’mon, Ollie, let’s go.”

They walked back to the parking lot, which was mostly cleared out by now. Nicky was grateful for that. She wasn’t embarrassed by Ollie, not anymore, but he didn’t like loud noises like car engines, or the smell of exhaust, and she preferred to avoid a sensory meltdown. She got him buckled into his booster seat and then got into the car herself and headed home.

“Mrs. Grey said you had a good day,” Nicky said, trying to get him to converse.

“Yeah,” Ollie nodded. “Was your day bad?”

Nicky’s brows shot up in confusion. “No, it was okay.”

Ollie squeezed his cube. “Your face isn’t okay.”

For someone on the autism spectrum, Ollie was surprisingly observant when it came to other people’s moods, especially his family members’. Nicky slumped in her seat.

“One of my friends is being kind of mean,” she said, simplifying the situation.

“You shouldn’t be friends with mean people,” Ollie said resolutely. “You’re nice. You should be friends with nice people.”

“I’m not that nice,” Nicky muttered. “I don’t have many nice friends.”

“Then make more,” Ollie told her. “To make a new friend, you just go up to them and say, ‘Hi, my name is Ollie, what’s your name?’ Except you’d say Nicky instead of Ollie ‘cause that’s your name.”

“Right, yeah, good idea,” Nicky agreed. “I thought I made a new friend, but then I blew it.”

Ollie’s forehead crinkled in confusion. “Did you say ‘sorry?’”

“No,” Nicky replied.

“Try saying sorry,” he said simply.

Nicky grinned. “Ollie, has anyone ever told you how smart you are?”

“Yes,” he replied. “I won the spelling bee.”

Chapter 5: Rio

Chapter Summary

Rio meets her intern, reunites with an old friend, and runs into the love of her life.

Chapter Notes

CW: descriptions of veterinary procedures

Flashbacks in italics

“Tori, you ready to go?” Rio called as she fastened her belt buckle. She took one last look in the mirror before opening the bathroom door and stepping into the main part of the house.

“Been ready,” Tori said around a mouthful of fried egg.

“I made breakfast, mija,” Mateo said, holding up a pan of eggs.

Rio took an insulated cup from the cabinet and poured it full of coffee. She grabbed the vanilla creamer from the fridge and mixed it in as fast as she could, trying not to spill anything on the formica countertop.

“I don’t wanna be late for my second day of work, Papi,” she said, taking a sip and scalding her mouth on the still-bitter liquid. “Ach… remind me to get you a new coffee pot.”

“What’s wrong with the one I have?” Mateo asked. “It’s just stronger than you like.”

“That bitter taste isn’t because the coffee’s strong, it’s because it’s burned,” Rio told him. “Anyway, we gotta go.”

“Here,” Mateo said, handing her an egg sandwich wrapped in a paper towel. “For the road.”

Rio kissed his cheek. “Gracias.” She looked at her daughter. “Let’s go, mija.”

“Instead of a coffee maker, maybe you should buy your daughter some pants that don’t have holes in them,” her father said.

“Believe me, I would if I could,” Rio scoffed.

“That’s the style, Tito,” Tori told him, giving him a kiss on the cheek before following her mother out the door. “See you later.”

Rio downed half her sandwich and reached for her coffee, only to find the cupholder empty. She looked over to see Tori sipping tentatively from the cup, her face puckered with disgust.

“Hey!” Rio exclaimed.

“Ugh, that really is gross,” Tori said, handing the cup over. “We need a Starbucks.”

“I doubt Westview’s ever gonna get a Starbucks, mija. But they sell coffee out of the bookstore that’s pretty good,” Rio chuckled, sipping the coffee. “Hey, I know I got in pretty late last night so we didn’t get to talk, but how was your first day?”

Tori shrugged. “It was fine, I guess.”

Rio glanced at her once, twice, three times, and realized her daughter wasn’t going to elaborate. “Good, good. How were your classes? Are your teachers nice?”

“Yeah,” Tori replied. “The pre-calc teacher seems like kind of a jerk, not gonna lie. And he explained the process really badly when he was teaching.”

“Is that gonna be a problem?” Rio asked.

“Nah, I think we already covered most of what he’ll be teaching at my old school,” Tori told her. “So I’ll be fine. I feel bad for the other kids, though.”

“Okay. Well, just try to stay on his good side. Keep your head down,” Rio told her.

“You know I will,” Tori replied.

Rio did know that. She was fortunate that Tori had always been a fairly easy child when it came to school. She was always respectful, and she maintained good grades. Rio couldn’t have asked for an easier kid to parent.

“What about the other students?” Rio asked. “Was everyone nice to you? Did you make friends?”

“Billy was nice, and he introduced me to his friends,” Tori told her. “I’ll probably keep hanging out with them, they seem pretty nice.”

“That’s good,” Rio said proudly.

“There was this one other girl,” Tori said with a frown. “I thought we might get along, but she turned out to be kind of a bitch.”

“Oh,” Rio said as they pulled into the drop off line. “I’m sorry.”

“It’s alright. There are other people.” Tori unbuckled her seatbelt and put her bag over her shoulder as they pulled up to the dropoff area.

“Hey,” Rio called as her daughter opened the door of the truck. “I’m proud of you, mija.”

“I know, Mami,” Tori replied, rolling her eyes.

“Just wanted to make sure,” Rio said with a wink. “Love you. Have a good day.”

“You, too,” Tori replied, waving as she closed the door behind her.

As she pulled away from the curb, Rio couldn’t help but glance back at her daughter in the rearview mirror, her eyes following her as she walked into the building. Once she disappeared through the doors, Rio sighed and set her eyes back on the road in front of her.

Tori was a good kid, a tough kid. She would be okay.

Westview Veterinary Hospital was just outside of town, set slightly back from the highway, surrounded by pastures. The clinic saw both household pets and livestock. It was a far cry from the exotic animals Rio had worked with at the zoo, but Rio had been a farm girl first. She figured if she could clean a lion’s teeth, she could definitely give vaccines to a house cat.

“Good morning!” Rio called cheerily as she entered the clinic. The black cat on the counter meowed at her, and Rio paused to pet his chin. “Good morning, Binx.”

“He’s already taken a liking to you, Dr. Vidal,” said the receptionist, a young woman named Rahne Sinclair.

“It’s the chin scratches. Wins them over every time,” Rio told her with a wink. “Is Dr. McCoy in?”

“Yep, he’s out in the barn with Logan and the intern.”

“Intern?” Rio asked.

“High school student. She comes here most mornings for a couple of hours,” Rahne told her. “She does odd jobs, helps with paperwork and cleaning. She’s a good kid, you’ll like her.”

“Oh, that’s nice. Thanks, Rahne.” Rio gave Binx one more chin scratch before heading further into the clinic. She dropped her belongings off in the break room and grabbed her stethoscope and penlight before making her way to the barn.

Dr. Hank McCoy had been the vet in Westview for as Rio had been alive. He was nearly seventy now, balding, but just as sharp as ever when it came to veterinary medicine. He was standing with Logan, a short, surly man who managed the barn area of the clinic, and a teenage girl that Rio had never met before.

“Ah, Dr. Vidal!” Dr. McCoy called in greeting. “You haven’t met our intern yet, have you?”

“No, I haven’t had the opportunity,” Rio replied, making her way to the group. The girl was almost as tall as Rio, with long brown hair pulled into a ponytail.

“Rio, this is Nicky,” Dr. McCoy told her. “She’s with us most mornings until about nine-fifteen. She’s a junior this year, isn’t that right, Nicky?”

“That’s right,” Nicky replied, nodding.

“And Nicky, this is Dr. Rio Vidal,” Dr. McCoy said proudly. “She was once an intern here just like you, and now look at her! She’s gone from being my intern to being my coworker!”

Rio felt her cheeks flush at the old man’s enthusiasm. “Couldn’t have done it without you, sir.”

“Nonsense, of course you would have,” Dr. McCoy scoffed. “But I do like to think I helped, just a little.”

“More than a little,” Rio assured him. She looked at Nicky. “You’re a junior?” Nicky nodded. “You’re in the same grade as my daughter, then. She just started yesterday—”

“You’re Tori’s mom?” Nicky exclaimed, her eyes widening.

“Oh, so you met,” Rio said.

“We have pre-calc and PE together,” Nicky replied. “She mentioned that you used to work at a zoo? With big cats?”

“Guilty as charged,” Rio told her.

“That’s so cool,” Nicky said, grinning wide so that her lime green braces caught the light. Her brown eyes shimmered with a fervor that was eerily familiar to Rio, but she couldn’t quite place what it reminded her of.

“I was gonna have Nicky help Logan hand walk some of the horses this morning,” Dr. McCoy said, watching their exchange with open fondness. “But I think it might be a good idea for her to shadow you for a bit, Dr. Vidal. You two can get to know each other a little better, and she’ll get to see how you handle appointments. How does that sound?”

“That’s fine with me,” Rio replied. She looked at the girl. “Alright with you?”

“Yeah, I can do that,” Nicky nodded.

“Good. Our first appointment should be arriving any minute, so let’s head inside.”

Rio made her way back into the building and to the waiting area, with Nicky hot on her heels. The room was filled with clients and their pets, most waiting to be checked in for surgery. While Rio saw patients, Dr. McCoy would be performing routine dental cleanings as well as spay and neuter surgeries. Rio picked up a clipboard from the corner of the reception desk and her eyes widened as she read over the name.

“Deputy Wu-Gulliver and Bear?” she called, looking up. Sure enough, a familiar woman in a deputy’s uniform stood up, her dark hair swept back in a tight bun, a wide grin on her face. Beside her trotted a well-behaved German Shepherd.

“Well, well, well, look what the cat dragged in,” Alice greeted with a smile, holding her arms out to embrace Rio in a hug. Rio’s mouth was still hanging open in surprise as she hugged her old friend.

“Oh my god,” Rio mumbled. “Hi. Oh, yeah, come on back.”

“I was surprised to see your name on the card when I got my appointment reminder,” Alice said as they made their way back to the room. “I never thought I’d see you back in Westview.”

“Yeah, well, I never thought I would be back,” Rio said with a shrug. “But dad needed me, so here I am.”

“I can understand that,” Alice said.

“So you’re a deputy now?” Rio asked, her tone colored with disbelief.

“Don’t sound so shocked,” Alice argued.

“Deputy Wu-Gulliver is the resource officer at our school,” Nicky piped up.

“And Bear is a drug dog with the sheriff’s office,” Alice said. “Some days he comes with me to school, and some days he’s out in the field with another handler. Today, though, he needs his checkup and vaccines.”

“Well, we’ll make sure he’s taken care of, won’t we, Nicky?” Rio said, and the teenager nodded.

The appointment was a standard check up with vaccines. Rio checked the dog over, and as expected for a working dog, he was extremely well behaved.

“Bear’s heart and lungs sound great. Ears and eyes are clear, gums are normal color, temperature’s perfect,” Rio said. “The only thing I might recommend soon is a dental cleaning. I can have Rahne draw up an estimate for you or your supervisor to consider.”

“Yeah, I’d appreciate that,” Alice replied.

“Next I need to administer Bear’s vaccines,” Rio said. “And I noticed that his nails are a bit overgrown. I can trim those while he’s here, if you’d like.”

“Yes, please,” Alice said. “But fair warning, he hates having his nails clipped. He won’t bite or anything, he just refuses to stay still, and with a dog his size, it’s a pain.”

“We’ll see what we can do,” Rio assured her. She looked at Nicky. “You’re not allergic to peanuts, are you?”

“No?” Nicky replied, her brows furrowing in confusion.

“Good. I’ll be right back.”

Rio went to the other room and prepared the vaccine doses. On her way back into the room, she grabbed a wooden tongue depressor and a small cup of peanut butter.

“Alright, Nicky, I want you to hold him like this,” Rio instructed, showing Nicky how to hold the dog with one arm. “And with your other hand, you’re going to hold this in front of his face. Try not to let him eat it too fast.”

She handed Nicky the tongue depressor with a dollop of peanut butter on one end.

“I can do that,” Nicky assured her.

Nicky held Bear just like Rio had instructed and the big dog lapped eagerly at the peanut butter. He didn’t even whine as Rio administered two vaccines, and he only wiggled a bit as Rio clipped his nails.

“There we go!” Rio announced happily as she finished the final nail. Nicky let go of the dog and he turned to Rio with a wagging tail. Rio ruffled his ears. “Good boy, Bear.”

“I think that’s the best he’s ever done for a nail trimming,” Alice told her.

“I am not above bribery,” Rio joked, gesturing to the peanut butter. “Nicky, I’m going to walk Deputy Wu-Gulliver and Bear out. Can you clean up the room?”

“Yeah, I can do that,” Nicky replied.

“How have you been?” Alice asked. “How is it being back in Westview?”

”It’s weird,” Rio admitted. “It’s definitely a transition, for me and Tori.”

”Tori?” Alice asked.

”Yeah,” Rio said, retrieving her phone from her pocket and showing Alice a picture. “She’s sixteen.”

“Oh… sixteen, wow,” Alice said.

”Yeah, she just started at the high school yesterday, so maybe you’ll see her around,” Rio continued.

“I’ll be sure to look out for her,” Alice assured her.

“Thank you,” Rio said, handing the chart to Rahne so Alice could check out. Rio sighed. “We seriously need to catch up sometime.”

Alice sighed. “I’d love to, but my schedule is pretty full. Between work and Eli, I barely have time to do anything fun anymore.”

“Eli?”

Alice grinned and held up her phone. Her lock screen photo was a picture of her and another woman with a little boy between them.

“That’s my wife, Jennifer, you remember her, and our son, Elias,” Alice said proudly. “He’s seven.”

“Oh my god, Alice, you got married, had a kid—”

“Yeah, well, you weren’t here,” Alice said, her voice low. “And I didn’t know you had a kid, either.”

Rio’s face fell. “I’m sorry—”

“No, don’t apologize,” Alice said. “You went out and lived your life. You did what so many people dream of doing. So don’t you dare apologize.”

“Yeah, but… You were my friend,” Rio said sadly.

“I’m still your friend, if that’s what you want,” Alice told her.

“God, you have no idea how much I’d like that,” Rio sighed.

“Then we’ll figure out a time to hang out,” Alice said. “Here, put your number in my phone and I’ll text you later.”

Rio did just that, triple checking that she had the right number before pressing save. She gave Alice one last hug goodbye before heading back to the exam room.When she got back, the room smelled of antiseptic and Nicky was throwing her paper towels away.

“So you and Deputy Wu-Gulliver know each other?” Nicky asked as soon as she entered.

“We went to high school together,” Rio told her. She looked around the room, which was now impeccably clean. “Good work, kid. You make a darn good assistant.”

“Thanks,” Nicky replied, beaming at the praise. “What’s next?”

Rio checked her schedule on her phone. “You ever knocked up a horse before?”

Nicky’s eyes widened. “What?”

They headed back out to the barn, where a horse trailer was waiting. Rio set up her equipment at the cross ties while Logan and Nicky got the horses off the trailer. Rio heard the clip-clop of hooves on concrete as they approached, and then she heard an agitated voice.

“I thought my appointment was with Dr. McCoy.”

“Dr. McCoy is in surgery,” Logan said. “Dr. Vidal will be doing the procedure today.”

“I would prefer Dr. McCoy.” They rounded the corner, and Rio saw a sharply-dressed man walking beside Logan. He scowled at Rio as they approached, his mustache dipping with the expression.

“Hello, I’m Dr. Vidal,” Rio said, keeping her voice calm. “Is everything alright?”

“Reed Richards,” the man said. “And I’m sorry, I don’t mean any offense, but I was under the impression that Dr. McCoy would be the one performing the insemination on my horses.”

“Dr. McCoy is in surgery right now,” Rio told him, repeating what Logan had told him. “But I assure you, Mr. Richards, that I am more than capable of—”

“I’m sure you’re capable,” Richards said. “I would just prefer someone more experienced.”

Rio stood straighter. “Mr. Richards, I have performed insemination procedures on hundreds of animals over the last decade, on several species including amur leopards, giraffes, zebra, rhinos, and, yes, horses. I assure you, I know what I’m doing, and I will take care of your horses.”

The man studied her and sighed. “The stud these horses are being bred to is the highest earning cutting horse in the United States for the last three years—”

“And amur leopards are a critically endangered species of which there are fewer than 200 left in the wild and fewer than 300 in captivity,” Rio said. “I can appreciate handling valuable materials, Mr. Richards.”

The man finally nodded in agreement. “Fine. I suppose it’s fine.”

“Thank you for your confidence. We’ll take good care of them and call you to pick them up once the sedation has worn off,” Rio told him.

Rio moved the first mare into the cross ties and gave her a mild sedative. She tied the mare’s tail to the side, and then she gloved up— a long glove that reached up to her shoulder.

“What’s that for?” Nicky asked, watching her warily.

“You’ve never seen a horse be artificially inseminated?” Rio asked.

“No,” Nicky replied. “Mom usually makes those appointments for when I’m at school.”

“You have breeding horses?” Rio asked. She took the ultrasound probe and smeared lubricant on it and her hand and forearm.

“Yeah we have— Oh, holy shit!” Nicky exclaimed.

Rio snickered as she shoved her hand further up the horse’s rectum. “That was my reaction the first time I saw it done, too.”

“Do you have your hand in her vagina?” Nicky asked.

“Nope, rectum,” Rio replied.

“Oh, gross!” Nicky exclaimed, gagging dramatically.

“Hey, if you’re gonna be a vet, you have to deal with a lot of shit,” Rio said. “Not to mention blood and vomit and all kinds of gross bodily fluids. Just wait until you have to do a semen collection.”

“No thank you,” Nicky said, shaking her head.

“It’s all part of the job, kid,” Rio told her, squinting at the ultrasound screen. “Now, let’s see here…”

“What are you looking for?” Nicky asked, her curiosity overcoming her disgust.

“Just making sure she ovulated,” Rio said. “According to Dr. McCoy’s records, she had a large follicle on her left ovary on Friday. If I can get the angle right I should be able to see… Yep, she’s ovulated. Good, onto the next step.”

The next step was the actual insemination process. Rio prepared the syringe and the tubing and placed it inside the mare. Then she looked at Nicky.

“Would you like to do the honors?”

Nicky’s eyes widened again. “Me?”

“Yeah, you,” Rio chuckled. “How many teenage girls can say they impregnated a horse?”

“I’m not sure that’s something I’ll be telling people,” Nicky muttered, but she stepped closer. “Are you sure I should be touching that? Mr. Richards seemed pretty worried just thinking it would be you doing the procedure. I don’t think he’d like it if he knew an intern did it.”

“Well, what Mr. Richards doesn’t know won’t hurt him,” Rio replied. “Besides, I’ve got it all set up. All you have to do is slowly push in the plunger. The only way you can screw it up at this point is to yeet this syringe across the room.”

Nicky stepped closer and took hold of the syringe. “Did you seriously just say yeet?”

“I told you, I have a teenager at home,” Rio shrugged. “Now slowly press down on the plunger.”

Nicky pursed her lips in concentration as she followed Rio’s direction to the letter. Once the plunger was all the way down, her features relaxed and she looked at Rio with a triumphant grin.

“Did I do it?” she asked.

“You did it!” Rio declared.

“So cool!” Nicky grinned. “So now she’s pregnant?”

“Almost,” Rio replied. “We’ve done our part, now it’s Mother Nature’s turn. We’ll preg-check her in a couple of weeks, and hopefully we’ll see a baby in there.”

“That’s amazing,” Nicky said. Suddenly a melodic beeping sounded from Nicky’s pocket, and she reached for her phone to turn off her alarm. “Crap, I gotta get to school.”

“Too bad, I was gonna let you do the rectal ultrasound on the next one,” Rio teased.

“Nope, no thank you.” Nicky shook her head.

Rio grinned. “Thanks for your help today, Nicky. You did a great job.”

“It was nice to meet you, Dr. Vidal,” Nicky said. “See you tomorrow.”

Rio watched as the teenager hurried out of the barn and toward the parking lot. As she got into a truck and drove away, Rio couldn’t shake the feeling that there was something familiar about the kid, but no matter how hard she thought about it, she couldn’t figure out exactly what it was.

“Hey, Dr. Vidal,” Logan called, drawing her attention back to the job at hand. “You ready for the next one?”

Rio shook her head and turned back toward the stalls. “Yeah, I’m ready. Let’s switch them out.”

Rio was exhausted by the time she pulled into the afternoon pickup line at the high school. It wasn’t the work itself, per se, that was so tiring. Rio was used to animals, and at this point in her career nothing really surprised her when it came to animal care and medicine. It was the people that wore her out. Some of the locals remembered her from her teenage years— which could be a pro or a con, depending on the person. And Dr. McCoy had put his full trust in her. But Rio still had to prove herself to every single client, which meant she had to be perfect at all times. No mistakes, no slip ups, always professional and polite, even when she wanted to call someone a dumbass.

It was a challenge. But Rio wasn’t one to give up.

Tori was waiting at the curb when Rio pulled up, talking to another student. She waved goodbye and hopped up into the passenger’s seat.

“You made a friend?” Rio asked.

“Yeah, I guess,” Tori said, buckling her seatbelt. “That’s Yelena.”

Rio glanced at the girl again before pulling away. “I see you two have been watching the same eyeliner tutorials.”

“I’ve seen the pictures in your high school yearbook, Mami,” Tori reminded her.

“Touché,” Rio chuckled. “How was today?”

“Okay,” Tori told her. “We’re starting a new project in art. Mr. Kirby decided to change up his syllabus and do a pottery unit since I told him that was the class I was taking back ho— I mean, back at my old school.”

Rio caught the slip up, but she didn’t say anything. “That’s nice of him.”

“Yeah, what I didn’t tell him was that I was shit at pottery, so…” Tori’s voice trailed off.

“I’m sure you’ll do fine,” Rio said. “What about your other classes?”

“We started reading The Crucible in English, and it sucks,” Tori said. “Not the play itself, but having to read parts. It’s so boring, and nobody understands inflection. I read one sentence with the tiniest bit of emotion and Ms. Schaeffer was begging me to audition for the school play next year.”

“And will you?” Rio asked.

“Absolutely not. I hate being in front of people.”

“You never know, it might be good for you,” Rio told her. “And you never mind being in front of a crowd when you’re racing.”

“Yeah, but that’s because they’re looking at the horse, not me,” Tori explained.

Rio couldn’t argue with that logic. She pulled into the grocery store parking lot and parked the truck.

“Your grandfather asked me to pick up a few things on my way home,” Rio explained. “You wanna go with me or do you wanna stay in the truck?”

“Is it okay if I just stay here? I want to work on a sketch.” She was pulling her sketchbook out of her bookbag before even finishing the question.

“No problem. Lock the doors once I’m out, okay?”

The grocery store was busy at this time of day, or as busy as anything ever was in Westview. Rio made her way up and down the aisles, dodging other customers, looking for items. She didn’t have to look very hard: the store hadn’t changed since the nineties. Salmon and teal tiles decorated each aisle, and neon signs reading Produce, Dairy, Bakery, etc. lit up each wall. Rio had worked in the store for a short time when she was sixteen, saving up money to purchase Lady. It was where she became friends with Alice, and where she had first met—

“Hello.”

Rio looked down at the end of her cart to see a little boy watching her intently. She guessed he was probably around seven or eight years old, with thick brown hair and curious blue eyes.

“Um… Hi,” Rio said.

“Can you reach the honey mustard?” he asked, pointing to a shelf far over his head.

“Yeah,” Rio replied, stepping around her cart. She picked up two bottles of the honey mustard in question from the shelf.

“I only need one,” the boy said.

“One for you and one for me,” Rio told him, handing over one of the bottles. “My daughter likes this stuff, too.”

“On chicken nuggets?” he asked.

“Yep,” Rio chuckled.

“What’s her name?”

“Her name is Tori. And my name is Rio. What’s yours?”

“My name is Ollie,” he said. “It’s short for Oliver.”

“Well, Ollie, it’s nice to meet you,” Rio said with a grin. “Are you here with your parents?”

“Just my Mama,” he replied. “She’s right there— Mama!”

 

Some people didn’t believe in love at first sight, but Rio knew that those people were long. Rio was sixteen years old, stocking shelves in the Save ‘n’ Go. They were in the middle of a heat wave, and even the industrial air conditioners couldn’t keep up, so Rio was covered in a thin layer of sweat. She was bent over, putting items on the lowest shelf, when someone cleared their throat. At first Rio ignored it, but then the person cleared their throat again, and Rio turned to look.

Standing over her was the most beautiful girl Rio had ever seen. Her long brown hair was slightly damp, pulled back in a messy braid that went halfway down her back. She wore a sleeveless floral dress that emphasized her narrow waist and was low cut enough to show cleavage, especially with her arms crossed over her chest. Her pale skin was slightly pink with sunburn and she had a smattering of freckles across her nose and high cheekbones. But her eyes were the thing that caught Rio’s attention the most: they were the bluest eyes she had ever seen, the color of the sky on a clear spring day.

The girl cleared her throat again, and Rio scrambled to stand. She dusted her khakis off and stood up straight in front of the girl.

“Can I help you?” Rio asked.

The blue-eyed beauty gave Rio a once-over before pointing to the shelf. “Your cart is blocking the Koolaid.”

“Oh, sorry about that,” Rio said, quickly rolling the cart out of the way. “Can I help you with anything else, Miss, uh…?”

“Agatha,” the girl replied, her lips turning up in a smirk. “And I think I’ve got it under control, Rio.”

Rio’s heart skipped a beat. “How did you know my name?”

“It’s on your nametag, silly,” Agatha teased.

Rio looked down at the nametag in question, pinned to her shirt. “Oh, right.”

“Rio? That’s Spanish for river, right?”

“Yeah,” Rio nodded.

“It’s pretty,” Agatha said.

“You’re pretty,” Rio said automatically. “I mean, Agatha’s a pretty name, too. Pretty name for a pretty girl.”

Agatha looked Rio up and down again. “You know what, Rio, I might need some help on the cereal aisle. Do you think you could lend me a hand?”

“Yeah, of course,” Rio nodded. She was pretty sure she’d do anything this girl asked.

 

Twenty-three years later, Agatha was just as beautiful as the day Rio met her. Her hair was pinned up in a loose twist atop her head, her features were just as sharp as ever, and she wore a flowy floral prairie dress that looked just as soft as the ones Rio remembered. But it was her eyes that made Rio’s heart hammer in her chest, bright blue and achingly familiar.

“Ollie, I told you to wait and I would get the— Oh.”

By some miracle, Rio had never once ran into Agatha on her visits to her parents. Probably because when she visited she and Tori stuck around her folks’ house and didn’t go into town. She should’ve known this would happen eventually, but even so she wasn’t sure anything could have prepared her for seeing Agatha again after all these years.

Nothing could’ve prepared her for the seething rage she felt boiling up inside her.

“Rio?” Agatha asked, staring at her in shock. “What are you doing here?”

“Shopping,” Rio said, gesturing vaguely to the grocery cart behind her.

“Obviously,” Agatha replied, her voice sharp. “I mean, what are you doing here? In Westview? Visiting your father?”

“Not visiting, no,” Rio told her. “I’ve moved back home.”

“But… Why?” Agatha asked.

“That’s really none of your concern,” Rio replied, crossing her arms over her chest.

“Mama, Rio got my honey mustard,” Ollie said, tugging at Agatha’s sleeve. Now that Rio saw them side-by-side, the resemblance was uncanny. In spite of everything, her heart lurched at the realization.

“Did you say thank you?” Agatha asked.

“Um… no,” the boy said, then he turned to Rio. “Thank you.”

“You’re welcome,” Rio replied automatically.

“Ollie, can you go put that in the cart please?” Agatha asked. Ollie nodded and headed to the opposite end of the aisle. Agatha drew in a deep breath and turned back to her. “Listen, Rio—”

“You have a son,” Rio said, and she couldn’t keep the bitterness out of her voice.

“I do,” Agatha nodded. “And a daughter.”

“You always said you didn’t want kids.”

Agatha looked at her strangely. “Things change.”

“Yeah, I guess they do,” Rio huffed.

“It wasn’t planned,” Agatha continued, as if that made things any better. “Surprised the hell out of me when my ex got pregnant. I didn’t even know I could—”

“What?” Rio exclaimed, confused.

Agatha’s expression turned confused as well. “What?”

“How can you say that?” Rio asked. “How can you say you didn’t know?”

Agatha’s mouth opened and closed in confusion. “I just—”

Rio didn’t wait for her to answer. Her heart was pounding so hard that it was all she could hear. She left her half-full grocery cart in the middle of the aisle and stormed down the aisle, past the checkout counters, and out of the store. Part of her wanted to run, but she couldn’t seem to make her legs move any faster. Her entire body felt like it had been filled with lead.

She opened the passenger door of the truck, and Tori startled awake, the book in her lap falling to the floor.

“What the— Mami? Where are the groceries?”

“I need you to drive,” Rio said. “Do you think you can get us to Tito’s house?”

“Yeah, I can do that,” Tori replied, unbuckling her seatbelt. “Are you okay? You look really pale. Do I need to take you to the hospital instead?”

“No, I just need to go home,” Rio told her. “I need to see your grandfather.”

“Okay, if you’re sure,” Tori nodded, hopping out of the passenger’s seat. Rio climbed in and fastened her seatbelt as Tori went around and started the truck.

“Tori?” Rio said as her daughter adjusted the mirrors. “Look at me.”

Tori stopped and looked at her mother. She looked so much like Rio, except for her eyes.

She had Agatha’s eyes.

“Drive,” Rio said, leaning her seat back and covering her face with her hands.

Rio started dating Agatha Harkness the summer she turned sixteen years old. Agatha had just moved in with her grandfather after being expelled from a private school in Massachusetts, where her mother lived, and that fall they both started their senior years at Westview High. Rio’s father didn’t like Agatha, but Rio didn’t care: she was in love.

Not that Agatha made it easy. She had a volatile temper and a jealous streak a mile wide. She kept people at arm’s length, not because she didn’t want to get close to them, but because she was afraid that if she let them in, they would just hurt her in return. But beneath that rough exterior, Rio saw a woman who had been hurt many times in her young life and still pushed on. She saw someone who, even when she tried not to, cared deeply for others.

That was the Agatha Harkness that Rio fell in love with.

It wasn’t perfect. They argued. They almost broke up on more than one occasion, and yet they always seemed to find their way back to one another.

There was one point of contention, however, that they couldn’t seem to overcome, and that was their plans for the future. Rio was determined to go to vet school and leave town for good. Agatha planned to take over her grandfather’s ranch and stay in Westview. They wanted two totally different things, and neither one of them was willing to give up their desires.

The first time Agatha tried to break up with Rio was the summer before she went to college for her undergrad. She said she didn’t want the pressure of a long distance relationship, that Rio would find someone else she wanted to be with more.

There would never be anyone Rio wanted to be with more than Agatha Harkness.

Rio outright refused to break up with her. She said no. She wasn’t going to break up with Agatha because maybe it wouldn’t work out or maybe she would meet someone else or maybe it would be difficult. Rio was going to fight to make it work.

For the next four years, the pattern played on repeat. Agatha would try to break up with her. Rio would refuse. Rio got the impression that Agatha enjoyed breaking up with her, because she needed proof that Rio would hold on, that Rio would fight for them. But, Rio had to admit, sometimes it was exhausting being the one who was fighting to hold on while the other person kept pushing her away.

After she graduated with her bachelor’s degree, Rio decided to take a gap year. She spent that year training horses, racing barrels, working at Dr. McCoy’s office, and loving Agatha Harkness. It was glorious.

Then she got accepted to UC Davis, the best veterinary school in the nation. It was too good of an opportunity to pass up, and she and Agatha both knew it. Reality hung over them, like a guillotine waiting to fall, and yet they clung to each other, clung to a romance that both of them knew wasn’t destined to work out.

In the end, it was Rio who ended things. It wasn’t what she wanted, but it felt so inevitable that she couldn’t wait around for things to crash. Agatha’s future was here, in Westview. And hers was somewhere else, somewhere far away from this little town and far away from Agatha.

At least, that’s what Rio had thought. But now here she was, back where it all started. And Agatha was still here.

Rio knew they were almost home when Tori put on the turn signal and the truck started lurching over the pits in the gravel drive. She sat her seat up and wiped the tears from her face as Tori parked the truck at the end of the driveway. She could see her father in the round pen, working with one of the horses he was training.

“I need you to go in the house and stay in your room,” Rio instructed. “Maybe put on some music.”

“Mami, is everything okay?” Tori asked, still concerned.

“I just need to talk to your grandfather about something,” Rio said. “I’ll be in when we’re finished.”

Tori nodded in agreement. Rio handed her her things and watched as she disappeared into the house. She took a deep steadying breath before making her way to her father.

She found out she was pregnant in October. She thought it was a stomach bug, but a visit to the school’s clinic had proven her wrong. She had tried to call Agatha first, and that was when she discovered that Agatha had either blocked her or changed her number. Then she called her parents.

“¡Hola, mija!” Mateo called from the middle of the pen.

“I need to talk to you,” Rio said, her voice rough with emotion.

“I’ll be done in ten minutes—” Mateo began.

“Now, Papi,” Rio said.

Mateo took one look at her and dropped the stick he was using to keep the horse moving and walked it closer so he could unclip the line on its halter. The horse trotted off to explore the arena, and Mateo slowly made his way over to where she was standing.

“Mija? Is everything alright?” he asked.

“I bumped into Agatha Harkness at the grocery store,” Rio told him.

Mateo’s expression immediately hardened. “Did she say something to you?”

“No— I mean—” Rio felt the tears coming again. “She was there with her son. Why didn’t you tell me she had a kid?”

Mateo crossed his arms. “I didn’t want to upset you.”

“And then she acted like… She acted like nothing had happened,” Rio exclaimed. “And she said… She said her son was a surprise, that she didn’t know she could get another woman pregnant.”

“Mierda,” Mateo spat.

“Why would she say that?” Rio demanded.

“I don’t know,” Mateo said. “I don’t know why she would lie to you.”

“You did speak to her, didn’t you?” Rio asked. “When I got pregnant and I asked you to go talk to her. You did it, right?”

Mateo’s expression softened. “Of course I did, mija.”

“What did she say?” Rio asked. “When you went to her, and you told her I was pregnant, what did she say?”

Mateo looked at her with pity. “She said that she was already seeing someone else, and she didn’t need you ruining that. Then she gave me five hundred dollars and to give to you to ‘take care of it.’”

Rio nodded. That was exactly what her father had told her seventeen years ago.

“Did Tori see her?” Mateo asked.

“No, she was in the car,” Rio replied.

“Good, she doesn’t need to get involved with them,” Mateo said. “The Harknesses are all the same, mija, no good.”

Rio looked up at him. “Your granddaughter is half Harkness.”

“She is a Vidal, and that is all that matters,” Mateo said firmly. “And she never needs to know otherwise.”

Rio nodded. Her father stared at her for a long moment, then pulled her into a tight hug.

“I’m so sorry, Rio” he said. “You never should’ve had to come back here and see her face again.”

“It’s alright, Papi. I’ll manage,” Rio said, sniffling. She straightened up and looked toward the barn. “I think I’ll saddle up Lady and take her for a ride. Can you make sure Tori does her homework?”

“I will,” her father agreed.

Rio made her way to the barn and tacked up the old mare. Riding had always been her escape. She couldn’t count the number of times she had cried while sitting atop Lady, and the horse always kept her safe, always made things better. Hell, this wasn’t even the first time Rio had ridden her while crying over Agatha Harkness.

But she swore this would be the last.

Chapter 6: Agatha

Chapter Notes

Flashbacks in italics

The alarm clock read 4:37am. The sun wouldn’t be up for two more hours. But Agatha was awake.

She pushed herself up and out of bed, her joints popping and muscles aching with each motion. Her head was pounding and her mouth was dry, all courtesy of the copious amount of liquor she had drank the night before so that she could get just a few hours of sleep. She shuffled from the bed to the bathroom, her stomach turning as she went, but she managed not to vomit. She turned on the shower and attempted to brush the tangles out of her hair as the water warmed up. She gave up halfway through and stripped off her pajamas before stepping under the steaming hot water. She turned the heat up another notch, until the water was so hot that it turned her skin pink and set her nerves ablaze.

This wasn’t a shower. It was penitence.

Once her skin was raw from scrubbing and scalding water, she wrapped herself in a towel and stepped out into the frigid air of her bedroom. Instead of a dress or a business suit, she pulled her most worn pair of jeans from the closet, a Jack Daniels t-shirt she had owned since college from the drawer, and a thick flannel button-up. She got dressed in the dark, her room only lit by a sliver of light from the bathroom door. Once she was done she dried her hair, not bothering to style it, and then braided the still-damp strands into a thick plait that fell halfway down her back. She grabbed a hat and her Carhartt work coat from behind the door, as well as her boots, and headed out.

The big house was quiet this time of morning, but soon Nicky and Ollie would be up and ready to start the day. It was Saturday, which meant they could sleep in for a little while, but not for long. Ranch life kept a schedule, even on weekends. But for now, Agatha had at least a few hours to herself. She padded past their bedroom doors, her sock-feet quiet on the hardwood floors, down the stairs to the kitchen. She only put on her boots once she got to the kitchen table, sighing once the well-worn leather was in place.

The sun was barely beginning to show over the horizon when she got to the barn. She was greeted by low nickers and a few impatient snorts. Agatha made her way to the stall at the furthest end of the barn, and the red mare inside barely even raised her head in greeting, too busy chewing the hay in the corner of her stall.

“Good morning to you, too,” Agatha muttered as she opened the stall door. She pulled a lead rope from the hook beside the door. “Let’s go, girl.”

Agatha tied the red mare in the crossties and brushed her coat and mane before tacking her up. Once she was saddled, the mare stomped impatiently, ready to ride. Agatha led her from the barn and saddled her up just outside the doors.

“Where should we go this morning?” Agatha murmured as they walked out between the round pens and into the open fields beyond. When the mare turned north toward the river, Agatha just sighed. “Yeah, I figured that’s what you’d want.”

Three days. It had been three days since she had seen Rio Vidal in the Westview Save ‘n’ Go. And for three days, she hadn’t been able to get her ex’s face out of her mind. Trying to sleep, she thought of Rio. Driving her car, she thought of Rio. Working in her office, she thought of Rio. Sitting at the dinner table, she thought of Rio.

Looking at their daughter, she thought of Rio.

Agatha was seventeen years old the first time she saw Rio Vidal. Rio was on her knees stocking a bottom shelf at the Save ‘n’ Go, dressed in the grocery store’s awful uniform of dark teal polo shirt and khaki pants. Rio’s hair was shorter then, just past her chin, her face a little rounder.

Agatha didn’t love Rio right then, no, that would come later. But she loved the way Rio looked at her: like the clouds had just cleared, and Agatha was the sun. Rio always looked at her like that, like Agatha was something precious and good. It did something to Agatha, on the inside. It made her feel powerful, made her feel valuable, made her confident in a way she had never felt before in her life. Agatha liked it when Rio looked at her like that, and she decided right then and there that she would do everything in her power to have Rio look at her like that for the rest of her life.

But just because Agatha wanted something didn’t mean she would get it.

Rio Vidal had big dreams, dreams that would take her far from Westview. She wanted to travel and help people and animals in every corner of the world. Agatha loved her for it, but she also realized that Rio’s dreams and Agatha’s reality would never lead to the same future. Agatha had her family’s ranch, and she was determined to prove to her grandfather and her mother that she had what it took to take over the operation. Agatha’s dreams kept her there, but Rio’s dreams were bigger than Westview, and Agatha wasn’t going to be the one who held her back.

 

“We should break up.”

Rio looked up from the circles she was tracing on Agatha’s belly. Her face was still shiny from eating Agatha out not five minutes earlier. Agatha’s skirt was still rucked up around her waist. They had been up in the hayloft for an hour and a half, and Lilia was bound to come looking for them soon.

“What?” Rio asked.

“We’re going to college in two different states, Rio,” Agatha sighed. “We should break up.”

Rio looked at her incredulously. “No.”

“No?” Agatha repeated.

“No,” Rio said, her voice firmer. “We’re not breaking up just because we’re going to college.”

“But… Long distance is hard,” Agatha argued.

“So?” Rio shrugged. “We’ll work it out.”

“And what if it doesn’t work?” Agatha asked.

“Then it just doesn’t,” Rio replied. “But I’m not breaking up with you without at least trying.”

“But what if you meet someone at school? Someone you want to date more than me?”

Rio rolled her eyes. “Agatha Harkness, there isn’t anyone on this planet that I want to date more than you.”

“Are you sure you want to do this again?”

Rio looked up from where she was packing her belongings into the bed of her truck. MSU’s fall semester started a week earlier than Harvard, so she would be leaving the following morning. Agatha had come over under the guise of helping her pack, but after half an hour of Rio’s father glaring silently at her, she had stayed outside and sat on Rio’s tailgate the whole time.

“Do what again?” Rio asked.

“You know. The long-distance thing,” Agatha said.

Rio stretched a bungee cord over her suitcase, then looked up at Agatha. “Do you?”

Agatha shrugged non-commitally. Rio walked around the back of the truck to stand between her knees.

“Are you trying to break up with me?” Rio asked.

“No,” Agatha said quickly. Then, “Yes.”

Rio shook her head. “We’re not breaking up.”

“We’re not?” Agatha asked.

“Nope,” Rio replied, grabbing her thighs and pulling her to the edge of the tailgate. “You do this every summer, mi amor, and every summer I tell you that we’re not breaking up. And every year, we make it work. Why do you think this year’s going to be any different from the last three years?”

“I don’t know,” Agatha sighed. “I guess I just like pushing you away, knowing you’ll always come back to me.”

“You got that right,” Rio said, pressing soft kisses to her cheek and the side of her neck. “You’re stuck with me, baby. Wild horses couldn’t drag me away.”

Agatha stared at the pregnancy test in her hands. Two pink lines stared back at her. She picked up the box from the counter, reading the instructions for the fourth or fifth time, she had lost count. One line meant negative. Two lines meant positive. And positive meant pregnant. Sure, there was a possibility of a false positive, but considering this was the third test that Agatha had taken, three days in a row, three different brands, and they all came back positive…

Yeah, she was pregnant.

“Fuck,” she muttered, sitting down on the toilet. Sure, she knew it was possible for a woman to get pregnant from sex with another woman, but the odds were slim. Hell, she and Rio had been together for over five years and this had never happened. Why now? Agatha was finished with college, but Rio had been accepted to UC Davis. She was leaving for California in less than a week. Sure they had made long-distance work in the past, but with a baby?

“Agatha? You alright in there?” her grandfather’s voice echoed through the bathroom door.

Agatha quickly put the pregnancy test and the box in the back of the drawer that held all her pads and tampons— not that she would need those for a few months— where her grandfather would never look.

“Yeah, Grandpa, I’m good. Gator’s chili from last night just didn’t sit right with me.”

“Gator’s chili don’t sit right with nobody,” her grandfather chuckled. Then his tone darkened. “That girl of yours is outside.”

Agatha bristled at his tone. It wasn’t a secret that her grandfather didn’t approve of her relationship with Rio. Not only was he homophobic, he thought she could do better, whatever that meant. Agatha didn't think there was anybody better for her than Rio Vidal.

“I’ll be right down,” Agatha said.

Agatha expected Rio to be waiting on the porch, or perhaps she would already be in the barn, saddling up their horses for a ride. Instead, Rio was still in the driveway, leaning against the tailgate of her pickup truck.

“Hello, my love,” Agatha greeted, wrapping her arms around Rio’s neck for a kiss. When she pulled back, Agatha looked around. “No trailer. You didn’t bring Lady? I thought we were going on a ride?”

Rio shifted her weight nervously from one foot to the other. “I, um, I didn’t bring her. I just came to talk for a bit.”

“Okay,” Agatha said. Rio’s obvious anxiety was making her nervous, and the new realization that there was a baby inside her was not helping.

“I’ve been thinking about the future,” Rio said, not meeting Agatha’s gaze. “I leave in three days and I think it might be best if we… If we aren’t together anymore.”

Agatha felt like the earth had dropped out from under her feet. She thought she might throw up, and it wasn’t morning sickness.

“What?” she asked.

“I know this is out of the blue, but…” Rio sighed, looking at the sky. “Your future is here, Agatha, in Westview. But my future… After I go to vet school, I’m not coming back. I want to travel and see the world, help animals and help people. I don’t want to be tied down to this place. I don’t want to be—”

“You don’t want to be tied down to me,” Agatha finished for her.

Rio’s expression remained cold. “It’s not like that. I love you, I do. But I’m just not sure that’s enough.”

Agatha stared at the woman she loved. She thought of the baby she was carrying, the child that was part her and part Rio. Every molecule of her being wanted to tell Rio about the baby, to beg her to stay.

If Agatha told her about the baby, Rio would stay. Agatha could see it in her mind’s eye, clear as day. She would tell Rio she was pregnant. They would get married. She would go to California with Rio so that they could be together while Rio finished vet school. Then, when Rio was done with school, they would move back to Westview. Agatha would run the ranch and Rio would work as a vet. Dr. McCoy loved her, so Agatha was sure he would hire Rio to work with him. They would raise their kid together and Agatha would be happy…

But Rio would be giving up her dreams. She would be giving up everything she had worked towards the entire time Agatha had known her. Sure, she might be happy with Agatha, with their kid. But the dream she was giving up would always be hanging over them like a shadow. And Agatha couldn’t have that.

”You’re right,” Agatha told her. Speaking felt like choking, and Agatha felt like her heart had taken up residence somewhere in her throat, like it was trying to silence her. “You should go.”

Rio’s eyes widened in surprise. “Yeah?”

”Of course,” Agatha nodded. “It was always gonna end up like this, wasn’t it? You and I were never meant to be together forever. It was just something fun until you were ready to move on and chase your dreams.”

Rio’s face fell. “Agatha…”

”Am I wrong?” Agatha asked.

Rio shook her head. She looked defeated. “I guess not.”



Until three days ago, Agatha thought that memory of Rio would be the last time she ever saw the other woman.

Agatha had never let herself imagine what it would be like to see Rio again. It was pointless, because Rio was gone. Agatha had made sure of that, changing her number and blocking Rio in every way after their breakup. She had never pictured some happy, romantic reunion. She had never let herself imagine seeing Rio across an open field and running into her arms. She had never fantasized the feeling of Rio’s arms around her or Rio’s lips on hers again.

Never. Not once. And if she lied to herself enough, she could almost make it true.

She never imagined she would see Rio at four-fifteen on a Tuesday afternoon on the condiment aisle of the Save ‘n’ Go. It was so mundane that it still felt unreal. But it was real, it had happened, and Agatha had replayed those moments over and over in her mind ever since.

Rio’s beautiful face had morphed from surprise to confusion to hurt and finally to anger in a matter of seconds. It had happened so quickly that Agatha wasn’t sure what had caused each shift of emotion. And then, as quickly as she had appeared, Rio was gone, leaving her entire cart and a very conflicted Agatha behind her.

Agatha tried to remember exactly what was said between them, but the more she tried to recall it, the more the entire interaction blurred in her mind. Her brain seemed to have stopped processing halfway through the conversation.

I’ve moved back home.

No, Agatha thought. Rio couldn’t do that. She couldn’t move back home. That would mean she had given up on all those big dreams she used to talk about. It would mean that Agatha had kept their daughter from her other mother for nothing.

It couldn’t have been for nothing.

The mare beneath her came to an abrupt stop and snorted, her ears swiveling. Agatha’s focus suddenly returned to her surroundings. One hand tightened on the reins while the other reached for the rifle mounted on her saddle. They had been walking along the edge of the river for a long while, on a well-traveled path, and were now close to the treeline. Agatha’s eyes scanned the trees for wolves or a bear. It would be unusual for either to come this close to the house and barns, but not unheard of. The horse stomped her feet and Agatha looked to her ears. They weren’t angled toward the forest, but toward a hill in front of them.

As Agatha watched, two forms emerged from the grassy hilltop, silhouetted against the pink and orange sunrise. Two doe elk walked side by side from the open field to the forest. A moment later another silhouette appeared, and then another, and another. The mothers led three fawns through the tall grasses. Two of the fawns were the same size, likely twins that belonged to one of the females, and the other was slightly smaller. As Agatha watched the small family made their way from the grasses to the edge of the trees. One doe, the largest of the two, paused at the treeline to look back as the fawns followed her partner to cover and safety. They would likely find somewhere in the woods to bed down for the day, and then emerge to graze again later that evening. The doe took one more look around, and her eyes landed on Agatha. She stared for a long moment before finally turning and following her family into the woods.

Agatha let out the breath she hadn’t realized she was holding. She could feel the mare beneath her relax again, though she kept her ears on a swivel.

“Good girl, River,” Agatha whispered, reaching down to pat the mare’s neck. “Let’s head home, yeah? I think it’s time for your breakfast.”

Agatha turned the mare back toward the barn, back toward her children, back toward home.

“Mama, do you think Eli could come over after the game?” Ollie asked, his voice muffled by the bite of waffle in his mouth. “I wanna show him my new chickens.”

“I’ll ask Eli’s moms,” Agatha replied, cutting her omelet. “And make sure you eat some of that sausage. You need protein.”

Ollie wrinkled his nose and poked at the sausage on his plate. “Is it spicy?”

“No, baby, it’s the kind you like. You’ve eaten it before,” Agatha told him.

Ollie still looked skeptical as he speared a piece of sausage on his fork. But he put the food in his mouth and chewed thoughtfully. Agatha held her breath until he finally swallowed and nodded.

“Oh, yeah, sausage is good,” Ollie declared.

There was a shuffling sound from the other room, alerting Agatha that Nicky was coming down the stairs. A minute later she came into the dining room, still dressed in her cat-print pajamas, a fleece blanket draped over her shoulders. Her hair was, somehow, even messier than Agatha’s had been when she got out of bed, and half of her face was still pink from how hard she had slept. Agatha raised a brow in appraisal as Nicky collapsed into the chair beside her.

“Late night?” Agatha asked.

“Morgan and Tommy broke up again,” Nicky huffed. “Morgan thinks he cheated on her. She kept calling me last night, and I was awake until like three.”

Agatha patted her daughter’s knee. “You’re a good friend, hon.”

Nicky snorted. “I don’t know why she’s so upset. I mean, Morgan’s talking to like three other boys. And she made out with some guy she just met at the rodeo dance last month. She doesn’t even like Tommy that much.”

“What does made out mean?” Ollie asked.

“You’ll find out when you’re older,” Nicky told him.

“Much older,” Agatha added.

Their cook, Remy, sat a second omelet in front of Nicky, who said a quick thanks before digging in with a moan. Agatha shook her head, but moments like this made her chest burn with the kind of happiness she had never thought she would experience.

“Hey, Mama, I have a question,” Nicky said after a beat. “Have you ever thought about breeding miniature horses?”

Agatha almost dropped her fork. “Nicky, we breed beef cattle and quarter horses. Why on earth would we breed miniature horses?”

“I dunno, I just think they’re cool,” Nicky said. “They can pull carts and stuff. Maybe we could use them to pull the hay cart?”

“We have tractors for that,” Agatha reminded her.

“People show mini horses,” Nicky told her. “There’s this lady in Eastview that raises them, and she brought her mares in to get checked this week. They were super cute. Dr. Vidal said they were really cool because mini horses have the same confirmation as big horses, they don’t have dwarfism or anything—”

Agatha froze, her fork halfway to her mouth. “Rewind. Did you say Dr. Vidal?”

“Yeah, she’s the new vet in Dr. McCoy’s clinic. I’ve been shadowing her this week,” Nicky replied, her eyes glowing with excitement. “She’s so cool, Mama. She worked with all kinds of animals at a zoo before she came here, like leopards and mountain lions. Dr. McCoy doesn’t really let me touch anything, but Dr. Vidal let me help her trim a police dog’s nails, and I got to play with kittens while she was giving them check ups. She even let me help artificially inseminate a horse! I got to use the syringe that shoots the semen into the—”

“Yeah, yeah, I got it,” Agatha said. She thought she might vomit up the half of her omelet she had eaten.

“What’s semen?” Ollie asked.

“Not something we should be talking about at the breakfast table,” Agatha replied automatically.

“Sorry,” Nicky said, looking chagrined. “It was just really cool. Dr. Vidal is amazing. You’d really like her.”

This couldn’t be real life.

“And you’ve been shadowing Dr. Vidal all week?” Agatha clarified.

“Uh-huh,” Nicky nodded.

Agatha’s headache was back. “Remy!”

Their cook poked his head through the door from the kitchen. “Oui?”

“I need more coffee,” Agatha replied, holding her mug out. The Cajun quickly disappeared back into the kitchen and returned a moment later with the coffee pot and refilled her cup.

“Anything else, ma’am?” he asked.

“I’d say whiskey, but I have to take Ollie to a basketball game, so I suppose that will have to wait,” Agatha sighed.

Remy gave her a slightly confused look before heading back to the kitchen. Agatha mixed creamer and sugar into her mug before taking a long sip of the hot liquid, closing her eyes at the heat. It wasn’t helping her headache, but it did make her feel slightly more human. When she opened her eyes, Nicky was looking at her curiously.

“What?”

“Do you know Dr. Vidal?” Nicky asked.

Intimately.

“No,” Agatha replied. “But I think Grandpa Jim knew her father. They didn’t like each other. So you might not want to mention your last name to Dr. Vidal, if you can help it.”

“Oh,” Nicky said, frowning.

Agatha’s heart twisted in her chest. “But you said she’s letting you help out. Is she… nice to you?”

“Yeah, of course,” Nicky replied. “She’s super nice. And she’s really smart, she knows everything about animals.”

Agatha’s lips turned up in a bittersweet smile. “I’m sure she does, hon. I’m sure she does.”

The gymnasium at Westview Elementary was full of parents and children when they arrived. Ollie immediately put on his noise-cancelling headphones, then hurried over to where his team was waiting. Agatha searched the crowd for a familiar face and then made her way up the bleachers to sit beside Jen.

“Where’s your other half?” she asked, squeezing in beside her friend.

“Alice is helping with security at a music festival outside of Bozeman,” Jen told her. “She hated to miss Ollie’s last game, but they needed a handler for the drug dog.”

“Makes sense. How did Eli take it?”

“He said he’d rather go with her to the music festival than go play basketball. I had to remind him that this was his last game of the season,” Jen chuckled. “Honestly, he might be biologically mine, but he is Alice’s kid through and through.”

Neither Jen nor Alice had the gene to get another woman pregnant, so they had used a sperm donor to have their son, Elias. He was a few months younger than Ollie, and the two had been fast friends ever since they were toddlers. Ollie wanted to do everything his friend Eli did, which was another reason Agatha had signed Ollie up for basketball.

The seven and under game ended, and part of the crowd started clearing out. Agatha watched as Ollie’s team took to the court, practicing their passes and dribbling the ball. She had worried that her son’s sensory issues would make playing too difficult, but thankfully the coach and refs were all understanding, and the boys and girls on the team all treated Ollie like any other kid. 

“So, um, Alice told me something very interesting this week,” Jen said, drawing Agatha’s attention back to her friend. “She had to take Bear into the vet’s office, and instead of Dr. McCoy—”

“She saw Rio,” Agatha finished for her.

“Did you know she was moving back to town?” Jen asked.

“I haven’t spoken to her in seventeen, almost eighteen years, Jen. Of course I didn’t know she was coming back,” Agatha scoffed. “I ran into her at the Save ‘n’ Go the other day. Surprised the hell out of me.”

“I would imagine so,” Jen said. She looked at Agatha expectantly, until Agatha couldn’t stand it anymore.

“What?”

“You know what.”

“I most certainly do not,” Agatha huffed. Jen had been her best friend since college, but sometimes she still irritated the snot out of her.

“What are you going to do?” Jen asked.

“About what?” Agatha asked.

“About Rio! About Nicky!” Jen exclaimed, though she kept her voice barely above a whisper.

“Oh. That.” Agatha shifted uncomfortably on the wooden bleachers and adjusted the skirt of her dress.

“Yes, that,” Jen hissed. “Alice said Nicky has an internship at the vet’s office and that she was working with Rio.”

“Mmhmm, I found out about that this morning,” Agatha nodded, taking a sip of coffee from the insulated cup she had brought with her. She really wished it had whisky in it.

“So what are you gonna do?” Jen repeated.

“What am I supposed to do, Jen?” Agatha asked. “I can’t exactly make Nicky quit her internship without giving her a reason. She loves working with Dr. McCoy, and apparently she loves working with Dr. Vidal even more. And believe me, the irony of my daughter wanting to be a vet is not lost on me.”

“So you’re just… not gonna tell them?” Jen clarified.

“What else do you want me to do?” Agatha demanded.

“I don’t know, maybe tell Rio about her kid,” Jen whispered.

Agatha scoffed. “Jen, be serious.”

“I am being serious,” Jen said. “You kept Nicky a secret from Rio because you wanted her to go chase her dreams, you didn’t want her tied down in Westview. Whatever. But now she’s here anyway. So what’s the point of keeping it a secret anymore?”

“The point is that I’ve kept it from her for seventeen years,” Agatha whispered. “What happens if I tell her, Jen? Hmm? How would you react if you found out you had a kid somewhere out in the world for seventeen years and their other parent didn’t tell you?”

Jen’s frown deepened. “I would be furious.”

“Yeah, exactly,” Agatha nodded. “Rio will hate me. Nicky will hate me. And that’s just the tip of the iceberg. What if Rio sues for custody? What if Nicky decides she wants to be with Rio instead of me? What if Mateo Vidal passes away and Rio moves back to California or wherever she’s been and she takes Nicky with her and I never see my daughter again? Hmm? What then?”

“You’ve been thinking about this a lot,” Jen noted.

“I haven’t slept in three days, Jennifer. I’ve gone down every rabbit trail of what ifs and each one is worse than the last,” Agatha shuddered, crossing her arms across her chest.

Jen looked at her sympathetically. “What if it isn’t that bad?”

“Jen, please don’t be stupid. You’re too intelligent for that.”

Jen was quiet for a few minutes as the game started. Agatha hoped she would just drop the subject, but knew she was probably just stewing. Finally, Jen spoke again.

“Rio deserves to know that she has a daughter, Agatha,” she said, her voice low. “What if it was you? What if Rio was the one who got pregnant and had a kid and she kept it from you? How would you feel?”

“I would hate her,” Agatha growled. “If the roles were reversed, then I would fucking hate her, Jen. Which is exactly why I can’t tell her.” Agatha sniffed and reached up to wipe her eyes, even though she wasn’t sure when they had teared up. “And you better keep your mouth shut. You hear me?”

“Of course I hear you,” Jen replied, though she didn’t sound happy about it. “I may not have agreed with this, but you’re my best friend. I’ve kept your secret for seventeen years. I haven’t even told my wife. But I don’t have to like it.”

Agatha looked at the ceiling and blinked rapidly, trying to get her eyes to stop watering. Then she sniffed one more time before looking back at Jen. She bumped her shoulder gently against the other woman’s.

“You really are my best friend. You know that, Kale?”

“Yeah, yeah, and you’re my favorite pain in the ass, Harkness,” Jen told her, grinning in spite of their disagreement.

Agatha forced a smile on her face and turned back to watch the basketball game. Deep down, part of her knew Jen was right. It was a truth she had known for seventeen years. But Agatha also knew that she was right. If Rio ever found out about Nicky, she would hate Agatha, and she would have every right to. And for that reason alone, Agatha would keep her secret.

She would take it with her to the grave.

Chapter 7: Tori

Tori looked around at the empty school parking lot and then at her phone screen again. She had a text from her mom saying there was an emergency at the clinic, and that she might be a little late to pick her up. That was sent an hour ago. Tori had texted Rio asking for an ETA, but she never replied. She could call her grandpa, but his doctor had advised him not to drive anymore due to how badly his hands shook. So Tori was stuck.

She clicked the button to call her mom’s phone, but it just rang and rang and then went to voicemail. Tori cursed and looked up at the sky in exasperation. What the fuck was she supposed to do now?

“Tori?”
