Rio gripped the steering wheel so tight that her knuckles turned white. “I know, mija. I know.”

At that moment, she hated herself, too.

Chapter 11: Nicky

Chapter Summary

Nicky and Tori come to a shocking realization (I’m sorry I used the same phrase three times in a row, I just couldn’t help myself)

“This sucks.”

Nicky looked up from her worksheet to see Tori half-slumped over the cafeteria table, half-heartedly poking at her spaghetti with a fork. It had been four days since they were called into Principal Davis’s office, and three days since they were allowed to hang out together after school. They still sat together in the classes they shared, and ate lunch together with the rest of their friends, but Tori was right, it definitely sucked.

What sucked just as much for Nicky was how Dr. Vidal was treating her during her internship. Shadowing the veterinarian had become the highlight of her days, but ever since Monday, Dr. Vidal had kept her distance. Nicky had shadowed Dr. McCoy when she could, but he spent most of his time in surgery or on house calls. So now Nicky mostly helped Rahne with paperwork and filing, and she was bored out of her mind.

“I thought you guys cleared up the whole cheating situation?” Billy asked.

“Yeah, didn’t they fire Dr. Strange?” Kate added.

“He was reassigned to the administrative offices,” Cassie said. She had stopped hanging out with Morgan and started hanging out with them shortly after Nicky broke off her own friendship with Morgan. “Apparently that’s what they do when a teacher sucks but they can’t outright fire them. They just move them to an office job until their contract runs out.”

“Figures,” Tori muttered.

“The entire class is doing better with Mr. Parker, even if he is just a kid,” Nicky told them.

“So what’s the problem?” Yelena asked.

“Our moms don’t want us spending time together,” Nicky replied.

“Mainly my mom,” Tori added.

“That’s so weird,” Tommy said.

“Tell me about it,” Tori sighed. “I don’t get it. We didn’t even do anything!”

“She didn’t give you any other reason?” Kate asked.

“She told me something about how my grandpa didn’t like Nicky’s grandpa because of some kind of land deal gone wrong a long time ago,” Tori told them. Nicky had already heard all this. “And she said something about Nicky’s mom. I think they went to school together and Agatha— Nicky’s mom— got them into trouble when they were here. And she’s worried that Nicky will get me into trouble or something.”

“Valid concern,” Teddy commented.

“Hey!” Nicky exclaimed.

“I’m just saying!” he replied, throwing his hands up in surrender. “You have a reputation.”

“I don’t mess with anyone unless they deserve it,” Nicky told him. “Plus, I’ve been trying to stay out of trouble since I started my internship. I won’t get into vet school if I have a bunch of behavior referrals in my record.”

“Plus, how would my mom know that you’ve been in trouble in the past?” Tori asked. “She shouldn’t be holding you responsible for anything your mom or grandpa did a long time ago.”

“It still seems weird,” Billy said.

“I agree. There has to be more to it than that,” Tommy added.

“You guys don’t think they—?” Yelena raised her eyebrows suggestively.

“What?” Tori asked, but Nicky knew what Yelena was implying.

“Ew, gross, I don’t want to think about our moms doing that!” Nicky exclaimed.

“I was just asking if they might’ve dated, that’s all!” Yelena defended. “You’re the one with your mind in the gutter.”

“Then why didn’t you just say that?” Nicky asked angrily.

“Okay, chill,” Kate said, pressing a hand to her girlfriend’s shoulder and holding the other toward Nicky. “You have to admit, it might explain a lot.”

Nicky sat back in her chair. “How so?”

“Emotions get really high when exes are involved,” Kate shrugged. “If they used to date, they might not be completely over it. Maybe your mom broke her mom’s heart, or vice versa. Maybe one of them cheated on the other one or something. Who knows?”

Nicky had to admit, that made more sense than any other theory they had come up with over the last week. But how could they find out for sure? She doubted either of their moms would admit to it, even if they asked them directly.

The bell for third period rang, and they quickly cleared their table and grabbed their things. She and Tori walked side-by-side through the hallway, but they didn’t talk. Nicky could tell that Tori was in a bad mood, and she wasn’t sure how to fix it. It was only when they passed by the office and her eyes fell on a familiar name on a door that it hit her.

“Oh my god,” Nicky said, coming to a stop. Tori kept walking. “Tori! Tori, come back!”

“What?” Tori asked, turning around and walking back to her.

“I know who we can ask about our moms,” Nicky said, grabbing her hand. “C’mon!”

“What are you talking about? We’re going to be late,” Tori grumbled.

“Tori, I know you’re a good kid, but let me introduce you to this little idea of skipping class.”

Nicky pulled her into the office and to the receptionist’s desk. “Hi, Mrs. Maximoff.”

The redhead looked up at them. “Oh, Nicky! Tori! Shouldn’t you two get to class? You’re going to be late.”

“We need to ask you a question,” Nicky told her. “It’s about our moms.”

Mrs. Maximoff’s expression turned sheepish. “Oh. Well… Alright, I suppose.”

“You went to school with them, right?” Nicky asked.

“Yes, although I wouldn’t say we were friends,” Mrs. Maximoff replied.

“Do you know if they were ever together?” Tori asked.

“Well, I suppose they had some classes together,” she answered.

“But did they date?” Nicky asked. “Each other?”

“Well, I don’t know if it’s my place—”

“That’s not a no,” Tori pointed out.

“Please, Mrs. Maximoff, we need to know,” Nicky pleaded.

“I believe they might have,” Mrs. Maximoff said, though she looked extremely nervous as she told them. “But I don’t know much of what happened between them once we graduated. We all went away to college, you understand—”

“Wanda? Is everything alright?”

Nicky’s chest bubbled with excitement as she turned to see Deputy Wu-Gulliver standing at the end of the administrative hallway.

“Yes, Nicky and Tori were just about to go—”

“Actually, we need to report something,” Tori said quickly. “Can we talk to you, Deputy Wu-Gulliver? It’s an emergency.”

“Yeah, of course,” the deputy replied. “Come on back to my office.”

Nicky couldn’t believe her luck. Not only did she remember Dr. Vidal said that she and Deputy Wu-Gulliver went to school together, but Deputy Wu-Gulliver was married to her mom’s best friend from college. If anyone knew about their moms’ history, surely she would.

“Bear, free,” Deputy Wu-Gulliver commanded as they went into her office and she closed the door. The police dog stood from his bed in the corner and greeted the girls with a wagging tail. “Have a seat, and feel free to pet him if you want.”

They sat in the chairs at the deputy’s desk and the dog sat between them, alternating who he paid more attention to as they scratched his ears.

“What did you two need to talk to me about?” Deputy Wu-Gulliver asked, taking a seat on the opposite side of the desk.

“We need to ask you about our moms,” Nicky told her. “You knew them when they were younger, right?”

“Nicky, I thought you wanted to report something.”

“Please, Deputy Wu-Gulliver. It’s really important,” Tori pleaded.

The deputy sighed. “What do you want to know?”

“Mrs. Maximoff said our moms dated when they were in high school,” Nicky said.

Deputy Wu-Gulliver raised her brow. “Yes? Was there a question in there?”

“Is it true?” Tori asked. Bear laid his head on her knee and she rubbed his ears.

“Yes, it’s true,” Deputy Wu-Gulliver said.

“How long did they date?” Nicky asked.

“They broke up right before Rio went to vet school,” the deputy replied.

“Why?” Nicky asked.

“Shouldn’t you girls ask your moms?”

“They won’t tell us anything,” Nicky replied. “And we’re trying to figure out why they won’t let us hang out together.”

The woman studied them for a long minute before answering. “Rio broke it off. She and Agatha both knew that their ideas for the future weren’t compatible. Rio wanted to travel the world, and Agatha planned to stay here in Westview. It just didn’t work out.”

Nicky’s brow furrowed in confusion. “That’s all?”

“That doesn’t sound very… dramatic,” Tori noted.

Deputy Wu-Gulliver snorted. “Have you met your mothers? Everything they’ve ever done has been dramatic.”

Nicky had to admit she had a point there. “What happened?”

“Those two were like magnets,” Deputy Wu-Gulliver told them. “Every summer, like clockwork, they’d get back together, and every August, they’d break it off. One summer Jen came to stay with Agatha on the ranch, and that’s how we ended up meeting. We both thought those two would end up together. Hell, everybody in town did. Even though Agatha’s grandfather and Rio’s dad couldn’t stand each other, we all really thought they would eventually get married and settle down on the ranch, have a couple kids…”

“But they didn’t,” Nicky concluded.

Deputy Wu-Gulliver nodded. “After they broke up, Agatha changed her number, deleted all her social media accounts, everything to make a clean break. It broke her heart to do it, but she was stubborn.”

“That’s so stupid,” Nicky huffed. “So I guess Dr. Vidal doesn’t want us to hang out because she’s still upset that they broke up? That’s crazy, don’t you think, Tori? Tori?”

Tori, however, was looking down at her fingers, counting something. She looked up, a strange expression on her face.

“Deputy Wu-Gulliver, what year was that?” she asked. “When our moms broke up for the last time, what year was it?”

“Um, let’s see,” Deputy Wu-Gulliver said, looking up at the ceiling as she thought. “We graduated in ‘03, so that would’ve been summer of ‘08, I believe.”

“Oh my god,” Tori muttered. She looked at Nicky with wide eyes. “Oh my god!”

“What?” Nicky asked.

“Hey, when are your birthdays again?” Deputy Wu-Gulliver asked.

“I think I’m gonna be sick,” Tori said, standing suddenly. “Thank you, Deputy Wu-Gulliver. Nicky, come on!”

Tori grabbed Nicky’s hand and pulled her out of her chair and out of the office. They ran down the hall, away from the office, and then Tori pulled her into a restroom. Nicky stood there, baffled, as Tori checked under all the stalls to make sure they were alone.

“Tori, what’s going on?”

“Oh my god, I know math isn’t your best subject, but keep up, please,” Tori huffed.

“What are you talking about?”

“Nicky, when is your birthday?” Tori asked.

“March sixteenth,” Nicky replied.

“What year?” Tori asked, speaking slowly, like she was spelling something out but Nicky hadn’t quite caught on yet.

“2009,” Nicky said.

“And nine months before that would’ve been when?”

“Well I was born a couple months early, so it would’ve been more like seven months but—” Nicky’s eyes widened as she realized. “Oh. Oh! Oh, shit!”

“Yeah, exactly,” Tori nodded.

“And your birthday is—”

“May first, 2009,” Tori finished for her.

“There’s no way,” Nicky argued. “There’s no way. I mean, that would mean that both of our moms have that gene and they got each other pregnant and that we’re…”

“Sisters,” Tori said, her voice full of wonder.

Nicky’s breath caught in her throat. Sisters. If it was true, then that would also mean that Dr. Vidal was her other mother. She turned away from Tori and looked in the mirror above the sink, staring at her reflection.

Everyone always said she looked like her mother, but there were differences. Her eyes, for one, were brown instead of blue. Her face was rounder, but everyone said that was just babyfat and would eventually go away. And she had a tiny gap in her front teeth that the braces were supposed to fix. She didn’t inherit any of those things from her mother, but she could’ve inherited them from Dr. Vidal.

Nicky looked at Tori. Tori looked like Dr. Vidal, but her hair was the same texture as Agatha’s, and her eyes—

“Oh my god,” Nicky whispered, reaching for Tori’s hand. The other girl took it willingly, squeezing tightly. “We’re sisters.”

Tori nodded. “We’re sisters.”

Nicky laughed. It was a raw, manic laugh, and a moment later Tori joined in. They laughed until they couldn’t breathe, until they could barely stand and had to lean on one another for support. When they finally caught their breath, Tori looked up at her with a joyous expression.

“What do we do now?” she asked, her voice barely above a whisper.

“I don’t know,” Nicky whispered back. “But whatever it is, we’ll figure it out together.”

Chapter 12: Tori

Chapter Summary

Tori finds out the truth (and Agatha’s DNA definitely makes itself known)

Tori had always been a good kid. It was a fact that she prided herself on. She was empathetic, sometimes to a fault. That meant that she saw how hard her mom worked every day, how exhausted she was physically, mentally, and emotionally. So Tori made it her goal to be easy for her mom to take care of, easy to love. She tried to be helpful. She tried to be kind. She listened to her teachers and worked hard to get good grades. She tried not to argue with her mom, though she wasn’t always successful, especially as she got older.

All that meant she often boxed up her feelings. Compartmentalization, that was what her old guidance counselor had called it. She could take her feelings and stuff them in little boxes and put them on a shelf in her mind until she was ready to deal with them. It was a handy coping strategy, until those boxes overflowed and the emotions exploded with dramatic effect.

Since moving to Westview, Tori found that riding Luna was one of the best ways for her to calm her emotions. Something about the repetitive sway of her body in the saddle, the feeling of the horse beneath her, the sound of Luna’s breath in her ears, all helped her center herself and keep her emotions in check. Mentally, she had to focus on the animal beneath her, not whatever was going on in her mind. It was tempting, sometimes, to focus on whatever was stressing her, but Tori knew that if she didn’t pay attention even the most broke horse could spook and accidentally injure her. Plus, horses fed off of and reacted to their rider’s emotions. Tori needed to keep a cool head when she was riding, and usually she could do just that.

Usually.

Today, Tori could feel the anger thrumming through her veins with every heartbeat. She focused on being calm for Luna, but it was still right there, just beneath the surface. Clouds were gathering overhead, sure signs that a storm was coming, but Tori stayed on Luna, alternating walking and trotting around the pen.

She and Nicky had decided to wait and make a plan about how to confront their mothers about the situation. Tori had agreed to the idea at school, agreeing that it was probably for the best. But with every hour that had passed since, she could feel the emotions swirling inside her, building and bubbling like a pot just about to boil over. The most prevailing emotion she felt was anger. And that anger needed somewhere to go.

The wind was picking up when her mother’s truck pulled into the driveway. Tori silently hoped that Rio would go inside without stopping to speak with her, but of course she couldn’t be that lucky. She turned Luna to go in the opposite direction as Rio approached, weaving her through the barrels in the pen before turning back.

“She’s looking good,” Rio called. “Storm’s about to roll in. You better put her up.”

“Just one more lap,” Tori shouted back. She went through the barrels again, at a lope this time, making sure she changed leads and kept her hands low and relaxed.

“Good job,” Rio shouted. Tori walked Luna to the gate, dismounting as her mother opened it so she could lead the horse through. “You’re improving.”

“Yeah, because I was practicing with Agatha,” Tori muttered as she walked past her mother.

Rio sighed. “Tori, one day—”

Tori snapped.

“Were you ever going to tell me that she’s my mother?” she demanded, turning back toward Rio.

Rio’s face paled. “Did she tell you?”

“I figured it out,” Tori replied, crossing her arms across her chest. “I’m not stupid, Mami.”

“I know you’re not, mija,” Rio said.

“Nicky and I figured it out today,” Tori told her. “Why didn’t you tell me I had a sister? Why didn’t you tell Nicky?”

“Because I didn’t know!” Rio exclaimed. “I didn’t know that Agatha was pregnant when I left, just like I didn’t know that I was. And Agatha never told me. I didn’t even know Nicky was her daughter until Monday.”

“So Agatha didn’t tell you she was pregnant, and you never told her either?” Tori asked.

“No, I told her,” Rio replied, her voice low. She pulled her jacket tighter around her shoulders as the wind picked up. “Come on, we should get Luna inside.”

“What do you mean, you told her?” Tori demanded. “If you told her, why wasn’t she part of our lives? Why wasn’t she around when I was little? Even if you guys weren’t together, shouldn’t she have had… visitation with me or something?”

“Tori, there are some things—”

“I need to know, Mami!” Tori exclaimed, and she started crying. Fuck, she hated getting angry because she always cried when she was angry and she hated crying. “Please, just tell me!”

“Fine!” Rio shouted. “I found out I was pregnant and I tried to get in touch with Agatha, but she had changed her number and deleted all her social media. So I told your grandpa, and I asked him to go tell her. And he did. And do you know what Agatha told him? She told him that she had already moved on with someone else, that she didn’t want anything to do with me or the baby I was carrying. And then she gave him money so that I could go and get an abortion.”

There was a block of ice in Tori’s stomach. “No, you’re lying.”

“Mija, I wouldn’t lie to you about this,” Rio said, and Tori could see that her eyes were full of tears. “It broke my heart, and I never wanted you to feel this pain if I could help it. But you wanted to know, and—”

“You’re lying!” Tori exclaimed. She climbed onto Luna’s back, ignoring the gusting wind and the tiny drops of rain that were beginning to fall. “You’re wrong! I know you’re wrong!”

“Tori, wait!” Rio shouted, but Tori didn’t. She turned Luna and charged out of the yard, running alongside the driveway.

Tori wasn’t sure how long it was before Luna slowed, but she knew that she had never pushed the mare that hard before. Luna’s coat was damp with a sheen of sweat and rain that was beginning to fall from the sky. When they reached the end of the driveway, Tori turned Luna to run alongside the road. She didn’t head toward town, but took backroads to her destination. She stuck to the side of the road, even as the rain began to pour down. Lightning lit up the sky, followed by rolling thunder, and Luna shivered beneath her.

“It’s okay, girl,” Tori whispered to her. “Just a little farther, okay?”

By the time she turned onto the dirt road beneath the wooden arches a half an hour later, Tori was soaked to the skin. She held on to Luna’s reins with numb fingers, barely even steering her at that point, but Luna knew their destination. At the end of the drive she came to the barn, and there was a light on inside. A familiar old woman with gray hair stood in the doorway, her face shocked as she watched Tori approach.

“Hi Lilia,” Tori said with a small smile, shivering. “I need to talk to Agatha.”

“Good lord, girl,” Lilia huffed. “Let me call up to the house.”

Agatha was there before Tori could even dismount, getting soaked in the rain as she came running toward her.

“Tori? Tori!” she shouted, catching her as she landed on the ground. Tori’s legs felt like rubber. “What on earth?”

“I need to talk to you,” Tori told her weakly.

“Lilia, take care of her horse,” Agatha ordered.

“Will do,” Lilia agreed, taking Luna’s reins and walking her toward the barn.

“Tori, what happened?” Agatha demanded, turning and leading her toward the house.

“Nicky and I figured it out,” Tori hiccoughed. “We talked to Mrs. Maximoff and Deputy Wu-Gulliver.”

“Figured out what?”

“That you’re my other mom, and that my mom is Nicky’s other mom,” Tori said. “And we’re sisters.”

Agatha sighed. “Of course you figured it out. My smart girls.”

Tori felt tears well up in her eyes. “So it’s true?”

“I think so, sweetie,” Agatha nodded.

“No, the other part,” Tori said.

“What other part?” Agatha asked. They had reached the porch now, and Nicky stopped at the top of the steps.

“Mami said that when she found out she was pregnant with me, she couldn’t get in touch with you, so she asked Tito to come and talk to you,” Tori explained. “She said that you told him to go away, that you didn’t want me, and then you gave him money so Mami could get an abortion.”

“What?” Agatha exclaimed, shock written on her face. “No, Tori, no. I wouldn’t… I mean, if that had been your mom’s choice at the time, I would’ve supported her. But I never would’ve…” She reached up and held Tori’s face in both of her hands. “Tori, please believe me. If I had known you existed, I never would’ve missed out on your life. If your Mami had told me she was pregnant, I would’ve taken care of both of you.”

“You wanted me?” Tori asked.

“I want you now, sweetie,” Agatha assured her.

The dam Tori had been holding back inside burst open with a sob. Agatha pulled her into her arms and held her as she cried into her shoulder, running her fingers through her soaked hair.

“Shh, there, there,” Agatha cooed. “That’s it, let it all out sweetie. It’s okay now.”

“Why would Mami tell me that if it isn’t true?” Tori mumbled into Agatha’s neck.

“I have a feeling your mom might think it’s true,” Agatha whispered. “But I don’t want you to worry about it. Us grownups are going to work this out. You and Nicky don’t need to trouble yourselves about any of this.”

“Nicky’s going to be mad at me,” Tori sighed. “We had agreed to wait, but I just couldn’t. It felt like I was gonna explode if I held it in any longer.”

“I think Nicky will relate to that far more than you realize,” Agatha chuckled. “You two unfortunately inherited poor emotional regulation from both sides.”

Agatha opened the door and ushered her inside, her arm still wrapped around her shoulders. Ollie was lying on the sofa, reading a book, and Nicky was sitting on the floor looking at her biology textbook on the coffee table. Ollie was the first one to look up, and his entire face lit up when he saw them.

“Tori!” he shouted, throwing his book down and running to her. He surprised them all by grabbing around the waist in a tight hug.

“Hey, Ollie,” Tori said, ruffling his hair instead of squeezing him back.

“You haven’t been here in forever!” Ollie whined into her shirt. Then he quickly pulled away from her. “Why are you all wet?”

“It’s raining,” Tori told him.

Nicky’s face was tight with concern as she approached. “Are you okay?”

Tori sniffled and wiped her nose on her wet sleeve. “I will be.”

“Nicky, I need you to find Tori some clothes,” Agatha ordered. “Tori, I want you to go take a shower and put on something dry. Ollie, go tell Remy that we’ll need an extra plate for dinner. Maybe even two.”

“Yes, Mama!” Ollie replied, taking off toward the kitchen.

“Two?” Nicky asked.

“After Tori showers, I don’t want you doing anymore homework,” Agatha told them, ignoring Nicky’s question. “Eat dinner, even if I’m not back yet. And then I want you three to pick out a movie to watch and cuddle up together and relax. Do you think you can handle that?”

“We can handle that,” Nicky nodded.

Tori nodded as well. “I should call my mom first.”

“Don’t worry about that,” Agatha told her. “I’m going to go deal with that right now. You just take care of yourself. Got that?”

“Yes,” Tori nodded.

“Good,” Agatha said. She pressed a kiss to Tori’s temple, then another to Nicky’s forehead. “I love you, girls. Both of you.”

“Love you, too, Mama,” Nicky beamed.

“Love you,” Tori murmured.

Chapter 13: Agatha

Chapter Summary

Agatha tries to set things straight (or, well… not straight-straight)

By the time Agatha turned down the gravel road that led to the Vidal farm, the bottom had fallen out. Rain fell in sheets, making it damn near impossible to see, lightning flashed, and thunder rumbled so loud that it shook her car. But the storm outside couldn’t compare to the one raging in Agatha’s mind.

Agatha Harkness didn’t have many regrets. She simply didn’t entertain the thought. She made choices, she lived with the consequences, and life went on. If she made the wrong choice, well, that was that. She would learn from it and move on.

She didn’t regret loving Rio. No, loving Rio and being loved by her in return had probably been the best and most beautiful thing Agatha had ever experienced. She didn’t regret having Nicky, either. Her love for Rio was only possibly eclipsed by her love for their daughter. It was a soul-encompassing love that Agatha never imagined she was even capable of until she became a mother.

Up until that moment, she hadn’t regretted keeping Nicky’s existence a secret from Rio. She had always believed it was for the best. But now that everything had come to light, now that she knew about Tori? Yes, she regretted it.

Tori’s existence threw even more regrets into the pot. Agatha cursed herself for cutting Rio off. At the time she thought it was best to completely shut that door, to make it impossible for Rio to change her mind, to make it impossible for Agatha to chase her. Now though…

Agatha parked the car in front of the trailer. The front door was open, and through the storm door Agatha could see two figures moving inside. She braced herself and stepped out into the rain, each cold drop sending a chill through her veins as she hurried onto the porch and knocked against the glass.

Rio looked at her like she had seen a ghost. Agatha knocked again, and the woman hurried to the door and opened it, but didn’t step aside to let her in.

“Agatha? What are you doing here?” she asked.

“We need to talk,” Agatha told her. “Can I come in?”

“Who is it?” Mateo called, stepping around behind Rio. As soon as he saw Agatha, his face fell into a deep scowl. “Get off my property! You’re not welcome here!”

“Agatha, now’s not really a good time,” Rio told her. “Tori—”

“Tori’s at the ranch,” Agatha said, cutting her off. “She rode all the way from here to there in the middle of a storm because she was upset.”

Rio swallowed hard. “Is she okay?”

“Physically? Yes,” Agatha replied. “Emotionally, she’s not great, but she’s better now that we’ve talked.”

“You’ve talked?”

“Yes. And I’d like to talk to you, too, if you’ll let me come in out of the rain.”

Rio stepped back and Agatha hurried into the trailer. She looked around at the kitchen and living room. The place hadn’t changed much, other than the pull-out sofa bed taking up most of the space. When they were younger Rio had been embarrassed by how small her home was compared to the big house at the ranch, but Agatha had always felt a bit jealous of Rio. The size of a house didn’t matter much when the people living inside were cold as ice.

“I said she’s not welcome!” Mateo Vidal shouted.

“I need to talk with her, Papi,” Rio argued.

“This is still my house!” Mateo challenged back.

“Yes, it’s your house. Your house, but I’m paying your mortgage. Your house, but I’m paying all the utilities,” Rio reminded him.

“I am still your father!” Mateo reminded her.

Agatha snorted. “Yeah, and you’ve been such a wonderful one.”

“Agatha, don’t,” Rio growled, cutting her eyes at her. She looked back at her father. “I need five minutes with her, and then she’ll go. If you could give us some privacy—”

“No, I think he needs to stay,” Agatha interjected. She walked over to the kitchen table and pulled out a chair. “Have a seat, Señor Vidal.”

“I won’t be spoken to like this in—”

“Sit down, Papi,” Rio said, watching Agatha suspiciously.

Mateo grumbled but sat in the chair, his legs nearly giving out as he did. Under any other circumstance, Agatha might’ve felt sorry for the man, but all she felt now was rage.

“Tori came to me upset, and with quite a story,” Agatha said, pulling her wet hair over her shoulder and wringing the water out onto the linoleum floor beneath her. She looked around and found a kitchen towel, which she used to pat the ends dry.

“What story was that?” Rio asked.

“She said that when you realized you were pregnant with her, you sent your father to tell me,” Agatha replied. “And that I turned him away and offered him money to pay for an abortion.”

Rio looked at her angrily. “Yes, I told her the truth and it upset her.”

“Only it wasn’t the truth,” Agatha said.

Rio’s reaction was slow. Agatha could practically see the moment she truly processed her words and the ramifications hit her. The anger melted from her face and her eyes widened as her jaw dropped in shock.

“What?”

“It wasn’t the truth,” Agatha said, moving around the table to sit in the chair across from Rio’s father. She crossed her legs and sat back, looking across the table at the old man. “Was it, Señor?”

Mateo didn’t respond aloud, but the way he was glaring daggers at Agatha was answer enough for her. If looks could kill, she would’ve dropped dead, but Agatha had dealt with much worse from much scarier men than Mateo Vidal in her life.

“Papi, what is she talking about?” Rio asked, stepping closer to the table.

“She’s lying, mija. Surely you can see that,” Mateo said, but Agatha could see the vein in his forehead beginning to twitch from his own deceit.

“I have two theories,” Agatha said, her eyes wandering around the kitchen. “I’d love to know which one is the truth. Theory one: you never even tried to contact me, you just told Rio that you did. But I’ll admit, even with how much you despise my family and dislike me, that seems low for you. So, theory two: you went to the ranch, but instead of coming to me, you went to my grandfather. Thought you would deal with this man-to-man. And my grandfather, mean son of a bitch that he was, told you to fuck off.” Agatha looked across the table at Mateo. “Am I close?”

“You act like you’re so different from him, but you’re not,” Mateo hissed. “You’re the same manipulative, conniving, arrogant—”

“Papi.”

Rio’s voice was soft, the syllables broken. Agatha and Mateo whipped their heads around to her, and Agatha could see the heartbreak etched in her features.

“Rio—”

“Mija—”

“Tell me the truth,” Rio said. “Just tell me the fucking truth. Please.”

Mateo’s fist clenched on the table, his hand trembling as he did so. He didn’t look at his daughter, instead keeping his gaze on the formica tabletop.

“I drove to the ranch,” he said. “I saw Jim. I told him you were pregnant, and that it was Agatha’s. I asked him to make her do right by you.” Mateo shook his head. “He laughed in my face and told me to get off his property. Said your bastard could belong to anyone, and it wasn’t his family’s responsibility.”

If her grandfather wasn’t dead already, Agatha could’ve killed him herself. A heart attack had been too good for him. She wished he had suffered more.

“And the money?” Rio asked.

“I took it out of my own savings,” Mateo told her. “I thought an abortion was the best option for you, mija. For your future.”

“And there wasn’t a little part of you that couldn’t stand me carrying a Harkness?” Rio demanded.

“Victoria is a Vidal!” Mateo argued. “She is a Vidal, and that is all that matters, Rio. She doesn’t need them, and neither do you.”

“Seventeen years,” Rio growled. “You’ve lied to me for seventeen years.”

“I did what I thought was best!” Mateo declared, pushing himself up to stand on shaky legs. “Think about it, mija. You never would’ve finished school, you never would’ve become a veterinarian. All of your hard work, for nothing. If she had known about Tori, or if you had known about her child, you would have—”

“Wait,” Rio interrupted. “What did you just say?”

Mateo’s face fell as he realized what he had let slip. “If she had known about Tori—”

“Did you know about Nicky?” Rio asked. “Did you know that Agatha had a child, too, Papi?”

Mateo stood up straighter, his expression hardening. “How could I not?”

“Oh my god,” Rio said, her words coming out breathy. “That’s why you— You always wanted me to move home, before that. You and Mami both. And then you did a complete one-eighty and started encouraging me to stay away. It’s why you hardly ever let me leave the house when we’d come for a visit, not even to the goddamn grocery store. You were afraid I’d run into Agatha, or that I’d see Nicky, and I’d know. I’d find out the truth.”

Rio collapsed onto the foot of the sofa-bed in shock, reaching up to hold her head in her hands. Agatha hated this. But the truth had to come out.

“I did what I thought was best,” Mateo said, his voice still steady with that self-righteous resolution that Agatha couldn’t stand.

“You lied to me,” Rio said. “You lied to me for seventeen years! You kept Tori from knowing Agatha, kept me from knowing Nicky! Do you even care that you have another granddaughter?”

“She’s a Harkness—”

“She’s as much of a Harkness as Tori is, and she’s just as much of a Vidal, too,” Rio told him. “Fuck! I can’t— I can’t fucking breathe—”

Agatha stood and moved to sit beside Rio on the foot of the bed and rubbed her back. “Hey, look at me. Calm, easy… Breathe with me, in and out, slow and steady… There you go.”

Rio looked at her with wide eyes. “I have to get out of here.”

“Okay,” Agatha nodded. “Let’s go.”

She held Rio’s hand as she stood, and Rio gripped hers back. Agatha pulled her toward the front door, wanting nothing more than to get out of there as quickly as possible.

“Mija, no! You can’t go. Not with her,” Mateo argued.

“I’m not going with her,” Rio told him. “I am going to my daughters. And I’m getting away from here.”

It was still raining bullets when they went outside. Agatha didn’t let go of Rio’s hand, instead pulling her down the steps and around to the passenger side of her car. Once Rio was inside, Agatha hurried around to the driver’s side, started the car, and pulled away before either of them could fully realize what they were doing.

Rio didn’t speak as they drove to the ranch. Due to the weather, Agatha had to keep her eyes on the road, but she glanced over whenever it was safe to do so. Rio looked lost, her eyes red and her cheeks wet with more than rain. It broke Agatha’s heart to see her like that.

“Rio—”

“Just drive, Agatha.”

So Agatha drove. When she got to the ranch, she parked so that Rio’s side of the car was closest to the house. She hurried around the car to help her out, but Rio was already out when she reached her. They ran to the porch, panting once they were out of the rain.

“Agatha,” Rio said as they approached the door.

“The girls are going to be so glad to see you,” Agatha said, a small smile tugging at her lips.

“Agatha,” Rio said again, “give me a minute.”

Agatha stopped and turned toward her. They were both soaked to the bone, but Rio was here. That realization seemed to hit Agatha all at once, and she pulled Rio into a tight hug.

“I’m sorry,” Agatha said, the words landing somewhere in Rio’s wet hair. She stepped back and reached up to hold Rio’s face in her hands. “I’m sorry that it had to come out that way, but you had to know, Rio. I had no idea about Tori and I would never—”

“I believe you,” Rio said, reaching up to hold Agatha’s forearms in her own hands. She didn’t push her away, just held Agatha there.

Agatha stared at her. They were older, yes, but Rio was possibly more beautiful than she even remembered. She stared at her, taking in the dark ochre of her eyes, the curve of her cheek, the pale pink of her lips. She wondered, for a moment, if Rio’s lips were still as soft as she remembered. Could they be softer?

“Agatha,” Rio said, and Agatha loved the way her name sounded when it tumbled out of Rio’s mouth. God, she had missed that.

“Agatha,” Rio said again, and Agatha raised her gaze to meet her eyes. “This doesn’t change anything. My father wasn’t the only one who lied.”

Agatha felt as if she had been slapped. Rio was right, of course, and she had been foolish to think that it wouldn’t come up. But still, part of her had hoped…

She stepped back, giving Rio her space. Rio let go of her, instead wrapping her arms around her own waist as she shivered from the cold.

“Tomorrow,” Agatha said.

Rio met her gaze. “Tomorrow.”

Agatha nodded and turned, blinking away the tears that threatened to fall from her own eyes. For a moment she had been twenty-two again, and the past seventeen years hadn’t happened. But they did, and there was nothing Agatha could do about it.

She wasn’t a witch. She couldn’t magically turn back time. Even if she wanted to.

Chapter 14: Rio

Chapter Summary

Moving forward

Rio awoke to the smell of lavender.

The bed she was in was the absolute perfect balance of firm and soft, the linen sheets were high threadcount, and the duvet smelled like lavender.

It smelled like Agatha.

Or, at least, like Agatha’s fabric softener. Rio was not in Agatha’s bed, but rather one of the big house’s guest rooms. Before she had gone to sleep, she had taken a hot shower, and Agatha had given her shampoo and conditioner and body wash. So even her hair smelled like Agatha. She hadn’t brought any clothes with her, but Agatha had given her a pair of pajama bottoms and a t-shirt to sleep in. Agatha’s clothes.

She might not be in Agatha’s bed, but Rio felt that in some way she had been claimed. She tried not to think too hard about that.

Once she was out of bed and had brushed her teeth, Rio wrapped a blanket around her shoulders and left the bedroom. She stopped at the end of the hallway, opening the door to Nicky’s bedroom just enough to peek inside. Nicky was in the bed, sprawled out on her stomach and drooling onto her pillow. A trundle bed had been pulled out from beneath the mattress for Tori, and she was curled up in a tiny ball, cocooned in her covers, with just her face peaking out. Rio watched them silently, her eyes fixated on the steady rise and fall of their chests, the same way she had watched Tori sleep when she was a baby. Then she closed the door with a soft click before continuing her trek downstairs.

She made her way downstairs, following the smell of coffee. There was a fresh pot in the kitchen, so she helped herself to a pot. She opened the fridge to find a container of vanilla creamer. Once she had mixed it in, she wrapped her blanket tighter around her shoulders and went outside.

Agatha was already there, wrapped in her own heavy throw, coffee in hand, feet tucked beneath her in the timber porch swing that Rio thought must be as old as the cabin itself. Rio leaned against the railing nearby, but instead of looking at Agatha, she looked out over the surrounding fields. The rain from the night before had left the grass slick, gleaming in the early morning sun. The sky was a pale gold, and in some directions Rio couldn’t tell where the grass ended and the sky began. In the other direction, jagged mountains rose up against the blue and pink of dawn. Below them the barn and stables were already buzzing with activity as ranchhands tended to chores and readied themselves for the day ahead.

Sometimes, when she lived in California, Rio would imagine mornings like this. When she was stuck in traffic on her morning commute, or when she sat on the patio of the apartment she and Tori had lived in for the past few years. She had wanted so badly to get away from Westview, and yet, when she looked out over the scenery around her, it was hard to remember why she had wanted to leave at all.

“Did you sleep well?” Agatha asked. Her voice was soft, but it was enough to cut through Rio’s wistfulness all the same.

“Surprisingly, yes,” Rio replied, sipping her coffee. “Did you?”

“Not particularly.”

Rio moved to sit in one of the many chairs that adorned the porch, none of them matching. The one she chose was a rocking chair, the wood well-worn from use. She wondered if Agatha had ever rocked Nicky in this chair, looking out over the fields and mountains.

“It’s tomorrow,” she said, her eyes fixed on a dark knot in the wooden arm of the chair rather than looking at Agatha.

“It is,” Agatha agreed. She didn’t say anything more, but after a moment— “Remy makes pancakes on Saturdays.”

“Agatha—”

“But if you want something else, I can ask—”

“Agatha,” Rio repeated, looking at the other woman with exasperation. “You can’t avoid this conversation forever.”

“I’m not avoiding it,” Agatha told her, bringing her mug to her lips again. “I just refuse to have it if you’re not going to look me in the eyes.”

“Fine,” Rio said, keeping her eyes trained on Agatha’s face. “Why didn’t you tell me you were pregnant?”

Agatha took a sip of her coffee. Then another. Rio was about to accuse her of avoidance again when she finally answered.

“I thought it was for the best.”

Rio felt like she had been punched in the gut. “For the best? The best for who? For you? For Nicky? Did you really think your lives would be better without me in it?”

“No!” Agatha exclaimed, clearly shocked that Rio would even consider that. “Of course not!”

“Then who was it better for?” Rio demanded.

“Better for you!” Agatha replied, as if it were obvious. “For fuck’s sake, Rio. You were in veterinary school. You were chasing your dreams, dreams that you told me about every single day for the entire five years we were together! What was I supposed to do? Ruin all that by telling you that I was having a baby so that you could drop everything and come back here to deal with diapers and spit up and colic and god knows what else?”

“I was dealing with that myself,” Rio scoffed.

“Yeah, well, I didn’t know that, either,” Agatha huffed.

“And whose fault is that?” Rio demanded.

“It’s my grandfather’s, and maybe a little of your fucking father’s—”

“I wouldn’t have had to send my dad here if you hadn’t blocked every line of communication that I had with you,” Rio argued.

“Yeah, and I wouldn’t have done that if you hadn’t broken up with me,” Agatha insisted.

Rio stared at her. “The fuck— You tried to break up with me so many times, Agatha.”

“Yeah, and every time you came back,” Agatha said, tears welling up in her eyes. “That’s just what we did. I pushed, you pulled, and we always came back to one another.”

“Maybe I got tired of constantly being the one fighting for us, Agatha,” Rio said. “Did you ever think of that? You pushed me away because you had this psychological need to have someone chase after you, but after five years I was tired of chasing you. I was tired of your bullshit. I had my future to think about. So yeah, I broke up with you. Still not a good enough reason to keep my child a secret from me.”

“Well I beg to differ,” Agatha spat. “Imagine for a second that you didn’t have Tori. Imagine if I had told you I was pregnant. What would you have done?”

“I would’ve stayed with you. You know I would have,” Rio insisted.

“Yes, I did know that,” Agatha continued. “You would’ve stayed, we would’ve gotten married. You might’ve finished school, but in the end we would’ve come back here to this ranch. Maybe we would’ve been happy for a little while, but eventually you would’ve suffocated, Rio. You would’ve suffocated in this town, under the weight of all those dreams you never got to chase. You would’ve been stuck with me, the woman you were tired of chasing. And you would be stuck with a kid that you didn’t ask for and didn’t want—”

“I would’ve loved Nicky,” Rio argued.

“I know you would’ve,” Agatha said sadly. “But it wouldn’t have been enough. You would’ve resented her, too, eventually.”

Rio shook her head obstinately. “You don’t know that.”

“I do—”

“No, you fucking don’t!” Rio insisted, standing as the fury exploded from her body. “You think you know everything! You always have. You make decisions for other people based on assumptions, Agatha. You kept my daughter from me because you thought you knew me better than I know myself.”

“I’m not wrong.”

“I’m here now, aren’t I?” Rio demanded. “I didn’t get all those dreams anyway, because I was busy raising our other daughter. And I love her, Agatha. I don’t resent her at all. I am happy. And I would’ve been happy with you, raising Nicky, raising our girls together.”

Agatha’s face twisted. “I don’t believe that.”

“Believe whatever you want,” Rio shrugged. “I don’t care. It doesn’t matter anyway. What’s done is done. You made your decisions, and I made mine. Now we just have to figure out what to do about our girls.”

Our girls. The words felt strangely right on Rio’s tongue, even in the middle of the fight they were having. She sat back down in the rocking chair and tucked the blanket back around her shoulders.

“What do you want to do?” Agatha asked.

Rio shifted uncomfortably in the chair. The problem was, she didn’t know what she wanted. After finding out her father had lied to her, all Rio wanted to do was get in her truck and go back to San Diego. But she couldn’t do that. She couldn’t leave Nicky now that she knew about her, and even if she wanted to, she couldn’t keep Tori from Agatha and her siblings.

“I’m not sure,” Rio replied. “I don’t want to go back to my father’s. I guess I’ll start looking for a place in town to rent. I doubt I have enough in my savings for a down payment to buy something myself.”

“You can stay here,” Agatha offered.

Rio sighed. “I can’t stay here, Agatha.”

Agatha scowled. “Can’t or won’t?”

“Is there a difference?” Rio asked.

“I think there is,” Agatha said, studying her. “But, in the long run, I suppose not.”

Rio couldn’t sit still any longer. She stood up and paced back and forth along the porchrail. She could feel Agatha’s eyes on her, studying her, sizing her up. She reminded Rio of the big cats she used to work with. They were trained to be friendly to their handlers, but they were still apex predators. You could never fully trust them, never turn your back on them, or they would rip your throat out in a heartbeat. Hell, they wouldn’t even do it for food, they’d do it for fun.

“How long?” Rio asked, leaning against the porch railing.

“How long what?”

“How long can I stay here?”

Agatha’s expression softened. “As long as you need.”

Rio nodded. “Then we’ll stay. At least until I figure things out.”

“Okay,” Agatha agreed.

Rio took a deep breath and let it out. She felt tired all of a sudden, like she hadn’t slept at all the night before. She reached for her coffee but it had gone cold. Then again, there wasn’t enough caffeine in the world for this.

“What do we tell the girls?” Agatha asked.

Yeah, definitely not enough caffeine.

“I was gonna start with the truth,” Rio replied.

Agatha drank the last bit of her coffee, frowning at the temperature. “Fuck.”

“You don’t want me to tell them the truth?” Rio asked.

“The truth makes a lot of people look like shit,” Agatha shrugged.

“The truth makes you look like shit,” Rio huffed.

Agatha frowned. “Yeah, that’s what I’m saying.”

“The truth is the truth, Agatha.”

“I’m just—” Agatha bit her lip, thinking. “I’d just prefer not to burn bridges that I’m just starting to build. That’s all.”

“I don’t lie to my daughter,” Rio told her.

“Fucking bullshit,” Agatha scoffed. “Every parent lies to their kid.”

“I try not to,” Rio said.

“Well could you make a fucking exception?”

“What do you want to tell them Agatha? That we’re equally at fault?” Rio demanded. “Because that’s bullshit and you know it.”

“You’re the one who broke things off, Rio. You’re the one who left. So don’t pretend you’re completely innocent in this.”

“You encouraged me to leave!” Rio shouted. “You pushed me to go!”

Agatha opened her mouth to argue, but before she could speak there was a loud thump from inside the house. Both women looked toward the window, then at each other, before standing and making their way back into the house.

Nicky was on the floor in front of the window, her legs all akimbo. Tori was standing over her, looking sheepish as Agatha and Rio came inside. Ollie was sitting on the sofa, his tablet in his hands, looking like he wasn’t paying attention at all.

“Well, well, well,” Agatha said, crossing her arms as she looked at the girls. “Looks like we have a couple of spies.”

“You two were fighting,” Tori said by way of explanation.

“We were worried,” Nicky added, pushing herself up onto her elbows.

“You were very loud,” Ollie admonished, not looking up from his game.

Agatha looked at Rio, then back. “Come here, girls. We need to talk.”

Tori pulled Nicky off the floor and they both went to the sofa, sitting on the opposite end from Ollie. They sat so close beside each other that it was like they were attached at the hip. Agatha sat in a chair across from them, and Rio leaned on the arm of that chair beside her.

“What do you know?” Agatha asked, staring at their daughters like a detective in an interrogation.

“Well we—” Tori began, but Nicky elbowed her, stopping her mid-sentence.

“We want to hear it from you,” Nicky said, looking at them expectantly.

“Nice try,” Agatha replied, clearly used to this. “But I think you’re forgetting who the adults are here.”

The two Harknesses stared each other down, and Rio suddenly felt very stupid for not realizing that Nicky was Agatha’s daughter, even without knowing her last name. The resemblance, especially when they were both being stubborn, was uncanny. Their stubbornness, however, was inconvenient as hell.

“This is ridiculous,” Rio huffed, looking from Agatha to the girls. “Your mom and I dated a long time ago. I broke up with her when I went to veterinary school. She found out she was pregnant and decided not to tell me because she didn’t want to mess up my future plans. I found out I was pregnant but couldn’t reach her, so I asked your grandfather to tell her and instead of talking to your mom, he went to your great-grandfather Harkness, who was an asshole, who told him that your mom had moved on and wanted nothing to do with me or the baby. So I never knew that Nicky existed, your mom never knew Tori existed, and now here we are seventeen years later.” Rio turned to look at Agatha. “Does that about sum it up?”

Agatha scowled at her. “I think that covers the high points, yes.”

Rio turned back to the girls. “Any questions?”

“Loads,” Nicky said, staring at them with wide eyes.

“Are we going back to Tito’s or are we staying here?” Tori asked.

Rio sighed. “We’re staying here. But only until I find a place.”

“What about after?” Nicky asked. “You can’t just leave.”

“I’ll stay in town,” Rio assured her. “Your mom and I will figure out times that you two can stay with her and times you can stay with me.”

“But why can’t we just stay here together?” Tori asked. “That way you won’t even have to do that and we can all be in the same house…”

“It just doesn’t work that way, mija,” Rio replied.

“Because you two argue?” Ollie asked, still not looking up from his tablet.

“Yes, exactly,” Rio nodded.

Agatha rolled her eyes. “Something like that, Ollie, yes.”

Tori and Nicky looked at each other, and Rio could almost see the unspoken communication happening. Side-by-side, there was no denying that they were related. Even though their skin tones were slightly different and Nicky had her hair highlighted, their faces and noses had a similar shape, and their expressions were similar, even though they hadn’t been raised together.

“But you’ll stay here until you can find a place?” Nicky asked, looking at Rio.

“Yes,” Rio replied, her hackles rising with suspicion.

“Okay,” Nicky said, seeming pleased with that at least.

“Anything else, or can we go eat breakfast?” Agatha asked.

“One more thing,” Tori said, looking between the two of them. “What do we call you?”

“What do you mean?” Rio asked.

“Well, I can’t keep calling you Dr. Vidal,” Nicky scoffed.

“And it feels weird calling you Agatha now,” Tori added.

“We hadn’t really discussed it,” Rio said, looking at Agatha, who seemed just as surprised by the question as she did.

“Ollie and I call you Mama,” Nicky offered, gesturing to Agatha.

“And I’ve always called you Mami,” Tori added, looking at Rio.

“Seems simple enough,” Agatha surmised. “Tori, if you’d like, you can call me Mama. But only if you’re comfortable with that.”

Tori nodded. “Okay.”

“And Nicky, you can call me Mami,” Rio told the other girl.

“What about me? What do I call you?” Ollie asked, finally looking up from his tablet at Rio.

“Well, um…” Rio stuttered.

“Rio is just fine,” Agatha said.

“Okay, Mami-Rio,” Ollie said. Beside him, Nicky and Tori both giggled.

“No, Ollie, just—” Agatha began.

“No, it’s okay,” Rio said. “He’s Nicky’s brother. If he wants to call me that, I’m okay with it, as long as you are.”

Agatha didn’t look entirely comfortable with the idea, but she also looked resigned to the fact that Ollie was going to call Rio whatever he wanted, with or without their approval. So instead of arguing, she just nodded. Rio wasn’t sure how she felt about the little boy calling her mami, but she meant what she said. He was Nicky’s brother, Tori clearly loved him already, and that meant he was going to be part of their lives. No matter how Rio felt about Agatha or about Ollie’s other mother, whoever that was, he was completely innocent in all this.

“Can we have pancakes now?” Ollie asked.

“Yep, I think pancakes sound amazing,” Agatha agreed, clearly relieved to be done with the conversation.

Rio followed along as they all made their way to the dining room. Last night the kids had already eaten by the time she and Agatha arrived, and she had eaten her plate of leftovers alone in the guest room. Now she watched as they all sat around the table, clearly in seats that had been claimed for some time. Agatha sat at the head of the table with Nicky and Ollie on either side of her. Tori sat beside Nicky. Rio stood in the doorway, watching them, until a throat cleared behind her.

“Excusez-moi, madame,” the man said, shuffling past her and carrying plates of food into the dining room.

“Mami-Rio, come sit with me,” Ollie beckoned, patting the chair beside him.

Rio took her seat and looked around at the table. Tori and Nicky were already talking about their horses and what they planned to do for the day. Agatha was helping Ollie cut up his pancakes into bite-sized pieces. If anyone who didn’t know them looked in and saw this scene, they might think that they were a perfectly normal family.

But they weren’t, and somehow pretending felt even worse than when she was arguing with Agatha. That was the real reason Rio couldn’t stay here, more than anything. It was far too tempting to pretend that everything was okay, to just slip into this comfortable fairytale. Moments like this, when what could’ve been screamed at her from every dark recess in her brain, would break her if she kept it up for very long. If she stayed here, she would want more, and that wasn’t something she could allow. No, even if Nicky and Tori didn’t understand the choice, Rio couldn’t stay here. She had to get out, even if it meant breaking her own heart again to do it.

Chapter 15: Tori/Nicky

Chapter Summary

Parent Trap Stage 1

Chapter Notes

This chapter kind of floats between Nicky and Tori POV.

Also, please check out the art by inkstrell which is inspired by a scene in this chapter!

“There we go, all done,” Tori declared, placing the last fake plant in Draco’s enclosure. “What do you think?”

“I think he likes me,” Ollie said from where he sat on the foot of Tori’s bed holding Draco in his lap. The lizard looked perfectly content at all the attention he was getting from his new family.

“I think we should get bunk beds,” Nicky declared from somewhere inside her closet.

“What?” Tori asked. Their moms had gone to get Tori and Rio’s things from their grandpa’s house Saturday afternoon, including Draco and Lady, and now Nicky and Tori were spending their Sunday rearranging Nicky’s bedroom.

“It would give us so much extra room to do activities,” Nicky explained, coming out of the closet with Tori’s clothes draped across her arms. “Plus I’ve always wanted bunk beds. They have them in the bunkhouse where the ranch hands sleep, and when I was a kid I would ask Mama if I could have sleepovers out there. For obvious reasons, she said no.”

Tori had to agree, sleeping in a bunkhouse full of gross old men, some of whom had criminal records, did not sound like an ideal place for a little girl.

“Do you really want to make permanent changes to your room?” Tori asked, looking around at the space. “I mean, who knows how long I’ll actually be here.”

“You’re going to be here until we go to college or move away,” Nicky said, her tone far more certain than Tori felt.

“You heard what Mami said,” Tori reminded her.

“Yeah, I heard her,” Nicky huffed. “But if everything goes according to plan, you guys will be staying here forever.”

“According to plan?” Tori repeated, confused.

“The plan to get Mami and Mama together,” Nicky replied, as if it were obvious.

“You have a plan?” Tori asked.

“Not yet, but we’re gonna make one,” Nicky told her before disappearing into the closet again.

Tori followed her into the closet, which was almost the size of her bedroom back at the trailer. “Are you sure that’s a good idea?”

“Of course it is,” Nicky huffed. “They were obviously in love before we were born. All we have to do is get them to fall in love again, they’ll get married, and we’ll be a real family.”

“I don’t think it’s that simple,” Tori sighed.

“I know,” Nicky groused. “But I’m trying to be optimistic here. Stop bringing down the vibes.”

“Okay, okay!” Tori said, throwing her hands up in surrender. “I agree with your idea. I just worry that it might make things worse if it doesn’t work out.”

Nicky hung the last of Tori’s clothes in the corner of the closet that she had cleared out for her. She walked over to her sister and took both of her hands in hers.

“Hey, don’t talk like that,” Nicky said. “No matter what happens, we have each other now. We’re sisters. Even if they mess things up, we’re gonna stick together.”

It turns out, making their mothers fall in love was not an easy task. They might be living in the same house, but Agatha and Rio could barely seem to look at one another. Rio often skipped breakfast to get to the clinic early, and she only sat through dinner after Tori begged her a week into their stay. Even so, she hardly ever spoke at the dinner table, and even then only to the kids. She and Agatha never shared a word.

If they did talk to one another, it was to argue. They were clearly making an effort to not argue in front of the kids, but Nicky and Tori had both walked up on them in the middle of a heated argument on more than one occasion. They would always pretend that they were just talking loudly, but neither of them was a very good liar.

The good news, at least in the girls’ opinion, was that the rental market in Westview was practically nonexistent. They knew that Rio was looking for places, but two weeks into her search, she was still proving unsuccessful. She had proposed a couple of options to the girls, but they would be too small for all three of them when the girls would visit. So Rio’s search continued, buying them time.

“How is this supposed to work if they won’t even talk to one another?” Tori huffed as she walked Luna around the edge of the arena. Outside it was snowing, because of course it was. Westview, Montana didn’t hold to the rules of regular seasons. One day it might be sunny and warm out, the next there would be thunderstorms, and a day later it would be snowing. Inside the arena, she and Nicky were working Luna and Scratchy, preparing for an upcoming rodeo. Their mothers were both watching from the gate, but they were as far apart as they could physically get.

“They’re infuriating,” Nicky agreed. Scratchy snorted, as if he were also agreeing.

“Who’s infuriating?”

They looked up. Lilia was also exercising a horse, a dapple gray draft cross named Fortune that was her usual mount. She trotted the gelding over to them, a brow raised in suspicion.

“Our moms,” Nicky whispered. “They barely talk to one another.”

“And when they do, they’re always arguing,” Tori elaborated.

“I’m not surprised,” Lilia said, looking over her shoulder at the two women. “Emotions like that don’t just go away, even after years apart.”

“So you think they still care about each other?” Tori asked.

“Without a doubt,” the older woman nodded.

“But they argue,” Nicky reiterated. “A lot.”

“They always have,” Lilia chuckled. “Usually ended up in the barn loft afterward, if you know what I mean.”

“Ew,” Tori gagged dramatically.

“Hey, I bet neither of you would exist if not for that hayloft,” Lilia continued. “Or the Northwest cabin.”

“The Northwest cabin?” Tori asked.

“It’s a little cabin over the mountain,” Nicky explained. “It’s mainly for ranchhands checking the cattle in the summer pasture. It’s not very big, no electricity. Just a woodstove and a bed.”

“Your moms spent a lot of time there the summer before they broke up,” Lilia told them knowingly.

“Can we talk about something other than the possible locations of our conception? Please?” Tori asked.

“Other than arguing and that,” Nicky said with a shudder, “what did they do for fun? Or for dates?”

“Well, they would go riding,” Lilia told them. “And they would race. I’ve never seen a more competitive couple. I think it was foreplay for them.”

“Again, ew,” Tori gagged.

“You asked,” Lilia shrugged, trotting away.

A piercing whistle pulled their attention back to where their mothers were watching from the gate. Agatha pulled her fingers from between her lips and shouted at them.

“Hey! Tweedledee and Tweedledum! You gonna practice barrels or trot in circles all day?”

“You’re Tweedledum,” Tori said, kicking Luna into a lope.

“I am not! You’re Tweedledum!” Nicky insisted, giving Scratchy a squeeze to catch up.

They took turns going around the barrels, weaving and turning. After a few minutes, Nicky waved Tori down and led them toward the gate.

“You girls done already?” Rio asked.

“We wanna race,” Nicky told them.

“You’ll get to race on Saturday,” Agatha replied.

“We race each other all the time,” Tori scoffed, picking up exactly what Nicky was getting at. “We wanna race you.”

“Me?” Agatha repeated, surprised.

“Both of you,” Nicky clarified, looking between the two of them.

Rio rolled her eyes. “You two want to race a couple of geriatric barrel racers?”

“Who are you calling geriatric?” Agatha exclaimed, feigning indignity.

“Agatha, you literally have rods and screws holding your neck together,” Rio reminded her.

“One ride isn’t gonna hurt anything,” Agatha assured her.

“Come on, Mami,” Nicky coaxed. “I’ve never seen you race.”

Rio rolled her eyes and looked at Agatha, but for once their gazes held something other than enmity. Without another word, they both disappeared into the barn to get their horses.

“This should be good,” Nicky said with a grin. Tori nodded in agreement.

Rio had Lady saddled first, and she came trotting into the arena. Lilia had put Fortune back in his stall and was waiting with her stopwatch in hand. Ollie had also come down from the house to watch, and he was sitting on top of the railing. Rio started warming Lady up, and a few minutes Agatha led River in, saddling up at the gate before joining Rio in circling the arena.

“Alright, let’s get this show on the road,” Lilia called after a few minutes. “Who’s going first?”

“Me!” Tori shouted, walking Luna into place. The mare seemed to sense that there was actual competition afoot, because she pranced and balked under Tori’s hands, clearly ready to run. As soon as the other horses were cleared, Tori gave the mare her head and kicked her into gear. They flew around the barrels, and Tori knew that she made good time, but that wasn’t her biggest concern right now. She really just wanted to get her ride over with so she could watch their moms.

“Me next!” Nicky called before Lilia could even announce Tori’s time. Scratchy walked over to the starting point like he could hardly be bothered to move, but as soon as Nicky gave him a squeeze he took off like a shot. Like Tori, Nicky had been steadily improving, but that wasn’t the point of this run. She nearly toppled the last barrel as she came around it, but she managed to push it back into place before running for the finish.

“16.3,” Lilia announced. “And Tori was 16.502.”

“Haha!” Nicky laughed victoriously.

“Mami’s turn,” Tori said, leading Luna around the older mares.

“Alright, alright,” Rio chuckled. Even though she was retired, Lady knew exactly what was happening, and much like her daughter, she pranced excitedly and chomped at the bit, impatient for Rio’s signal. As soon as Rio gave her the go-ahead, the old mare took off. For a brief moment, horse and rider were two decades younger. Rio’s hair whipped around as she took the turns, each motion still as polished and sharp as when she was a teen. Lady moved with sure-footed precision, twirling around the barrels like it was a dance.

“Wooo!” Rio shouted as she crossed the finish. Tori and Nicky both laughed at her exuberance as she walked Lady toward them. “How was that?”

“Super fast!” Ollie declared.

“16.902,” Lilia replied.

“Not bad, Mami,” Nicky said.

“Definitely not bad considering this girl’s age,” Rio panted.

“Alright, Agatha, you’re up,” Lilia said.

Agatha, however, was staring at Rio with a strange look on her face. She shook her head when she realized it was her turn and coaxed the red mare toward the starting line.

The girls thought Rio looked like a natural, but watching Agatha was something else. She sat so deep in the saddle and bent so close to the horse’s neck that it almost looked like they were one creature. Her hands were light and sure, but she controlled the horse with an easy confidence. Her hat flew off mere milliseconds into her run, and her hair flew wildly behind her, but it didn’t seem to bother her at all.

Lilia drew in a sharp gasp as Agatha crossed the finish, and Tori and Nicky both hurried to look over her shoulders at the time.

“Holy shit, Mama!” Tori exclaimed.

“You were so fast!” Ollie cheered.

“16.359,” Nicky grinned. “You were almost as fast as me!”

Agatha wasn’t looking at them. Her eyes were fixed on Rio as she trotted up, and Rio was looking back just as transfixed. Tori slapped Nicky’s shoulder, drawing her attention.

“Good race,” Rio said finally, holding her hand out for Agatha to shake.

Agatha chuckled and shook Rio’s hand like they were just regular competitors. “Back at ya. You and Lady still have it.”

“You, too,” Rio said. “You and Willow both.”

Agatha looked taken aback. “Oh, this isn’t Willow.”

Rio’s face fell. “What?”

“Rio, Willow would be over thirty years old. She passed a few years ago,” Agatha explained. “This is one of her daughters.”

“Well, she looks just like her,” Rio said, reaching out to pat the mare’s side. “What’s her name?”

“Oh, that’s—” Agatha began.

“Her name is River,” Tori interrupted. She might not be fluent in Spanish, but she knew the significance of the horse’s name.

Rio’s eyes widened and her hand froze in mid-air. “What?”

Agatha wasn’t looking at her. Instead her eyes were fixated somewhere in the neighborhood of her saddlehorn. “I named her River. She was born the spring after you left, shortly before Nicky was born.”

Rio’s mouth opened and closed several times as she looked between Agatha and the horse. Tori and Nicky were practically vibrating with suspense as they watched the two women.

“Agatha—” Rio began.

“Don’t,” Agatha cut her off. “It’s not a big deal, Rio. It’s just a stupid name, that’s all.”

Before anyone could say anything, Agatha dismounted, opened the gate, and led River out of the arena. Rio watched her go, a forlorn look on her face, before she shook it off.

“I think I’m gonna give Lady a few more laps,” she announced. “You girls should put your horses away for the night. Right, Lilia?”

“Right,” the older woman replied. “C’mon, girls. You, too, Ollie.”

“Aw man,” Ollie grumbled as Lilia led him away.

Nicky and Tori dismounted and looked toward where Rio was turning circles at the other end of the arena. Up the hall between the stalls they could see Agatha untacking River, huffing irritably as she did so.

“What was that?” Nicky asked.

“I think Lilia’s right,” Tori said. “They definitely still have feelings for each other.”

“So we have to get them back together,” Nicky whispered. “Right?”

Tori nodded. “Right.”

Chapter 16: Agatha

Chapter Summary

A happy family?

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Agatha was not an idiot. In spite of getting kicked out of three different schools for behavior, she had maintained straight As growing up. She had graduated summa cum laude with a degree in finance and marketing from an ivy league university. She could’ve had her pick of jobs in any marketing firm or investment company. Instead she had come back to her family’s ranch and used everything she learned about business to build the Harkness Ranch. And for seventeen years she had done just that, and done it well.

All this to say, Agatha was intelligent, both on paper and in real life. So why the fuck couldn’t she figure out the instructions for assembling a set of bunk beds?

It was Friday. The kids were at school. Rio was at work. And Agatha was losing her goddamn mind over a set of bunk beds.

She could ask one of the ranchhands for help. Several of them probably had experience with building and assembling things. But she didn’t want to. She wanted to build this thing for her girls by herself. It was a matter of pride.

“What the hell?”

Agatha muttered a curse at the sound of Rio’s voice. She was supposed to be at work, dammit. Why was she here?

“Why are there mattresses in the—? Oh, holy shit.” Rio stood in the doorway and looked around, then whistled at the mess Agatha was sitting amongst. “Wow. That’s a lot.”

“How do bunkbeds have six hundred and forty seven pieces?” Agatha asked. “It’s insane.”

“I may regret asking this, but how long have you been at this?” Rio asked.

“What time is it?”

Rio looked at her watch. “One thirty.”

“Four hours,” Agatha replied. “It took an hour for the guys to move out the old beds and bring the boxes up and then another hour and a half for me to sort the pieces alphabetically and numerically.”

“And the other hour and a half?” Rio asked.

“Mostly me staring at the piles trying to figure out exactly where to start,” Agatha admitted.

Rio looked around the floor. “Where are the instructions?”

Agatha scoffed. “Please. I don’t need instructions.”

“Agatha, considering these are bunkbeds that our daughters are going to be sleeping in and assembling them incorrectly could result in one of them getting smooshed… I think we need instructions,” Rio said, stepping over a pile of wooden slats and picking up the cardboard boxes the pieces had come in. She pulled a thick handbook out of the box with a flourish. “Aha! Got ‘em.”

“Great, now gimme,” Agatha demanded, making grabby hands. Rio handed her the book and she opened it, squinting her eyes to see. “Ugh, why do they have to make the print so tiny?”

“Do you want me to get your glasses, or—?”

“No, no, I can figure it out,” Agatha said. “I need to find piece AI and piece AZ and attach them with screw eleven.”

Rio snatched the book out of Agatha’s hands and looked down at the instructions. “Agatha, that says piece A1 and A2 and attach them with screw 11.”

Agatha sighed. “Okay, maybe I need my glasses.”

“Or, maybe, I can help you,” Rio offered. “They’re my kids, too, you know.”

Agatha wanted to say no. But then she looked around at the piles of wood and packets of screws lying around her.

“Yeah, okay,” she agreed.

“That isn’t the right fastener.”

“The instructions say eleven and the baggie says eleven…”

“Agatha, that baggie says fourteen.”

“… Oh.”

“Go get your glasses.”

“Okay, if you hold this piece like this and this piece at a ninety-degree angle, I’ll just screw this in right here.”

Agatha squeezed in beside Rio, using the allen wrench to tighten the screw.

“Any day now, Agatha,” Rio grumbled, struggling to hold the heavy pieces in place.

“Sorry, this little thing is impossible,” Agatha complained.

Rio looked down at her hands. “That’s because you’re doing it wrong. Turn it the other way.”

“What do you mean the other way? That’ll just loosen the screw,” Agatha argued.

“I mean put the short side into the screw and then rotate using the longer end. It’s easier that way.”

Agatha looked at the allen wrench for a moment, then turned it the way Rio had instructed. “That is easier, actually.”

“Told you,” Rio sassed.

“There’s no way that’s structurally sound.”

“That’s what the instructions say, Agatha. Look at the picture.”

Agatha took the manual out of Rio’s hands, squinted at the image, then looked at the cover of the book. Her eyes widened and she turned the book upright before handing it back to Rio.

“Oh. That makes more sense.”

“Mmhmm.”

Rio collapsed on the floor. “I say fuck it. They can sleep on the floor. Or in another one of the guest rooms. This is ridiculous.”

Agatha sat on the floor beside her. “How are there still so many pieces?”

“Couldn’t you have just gotten a set of bunkbeds from the bunkhouse?” Rio asked.

“There is no amount of sanitizing that would make me comfortable bringing those bunks into my house,” Agatha told her. “Plus, I wanted to get something the girls would like, and something that Tori helped pick out. I want her to feel like this is her home.”

Rio looked up at her with a soft expression. “That’s sweet of you.”

“Yeah, well,” Agatha sighed. “It won’t make up for being absent for seventeen years, but it’s something.”

Rio was quiet for a long moment. Agatha picked up the instruction manual and pretended to read the next page, but in reality the words were just a blur on the page.

“I should’ve come home and told you I was pregnant myself,” Rio said finally, her voice barely a whisper.

Agatha looked up at her sharply. “No… You can’t blame yourself for that.”

“But I should have,” Rio reiterated, this time with more force. “I never should’ve believed my father when he said you didn’t want anything to do with us. I should’ve known you wouldn’t say that.”

Agatha’s hands clenched on the manual so hard that the paper crinkled. “Then why did you believe him?”

Rio shrugged. “I don’t know. I guess… The whole time we were together, I never understood why you were with me, you know? You’re Agatha Harkness, your family owns a giant ranch and you have all this money and a huge house and a personal chef and you’re beautiful and confident and I was just… Rio Vidal. I grew up in a singlewide and went to college on scholarships and ate boxed mac ‘n’ cheese as a meal. I never really understood what you saw in me, so it made sense that you didn’t want me anymore.”

Agatha felt like someone had dropped a massive boulder onto her chest. “Rio, none of that ever mattered to me. I never cared where you came from. Hell, if anything, I thought you were a better person because of it. It made you a hard worker and a dreamer, two of the things I admired most about you.”

“Oh,” Rio said, seeming surprised by the admission.

“Nicky’s like that. Like you,” Agatha said with a fond smile. “She’s always reminded me of you. When she was little she always played pretend that she was a princess off in some fairytale kingdom, but as she got older she started pretending she was off on an African safari or exploring the Amazon. She’d pretend the horses were zebra and giraffes and rhinos.”

“Agatha—“

“She went through a whole phase where she was obsessed with the Crocodile Hunter and she’d only wear khaki and she was convinced that the river had alligators hiding in it.”

“Agatha, please stop.”

Agatha looked down at where Rio was still lying on the floor and saw tears streaming down the sides of her face.

“Shit. I’m sorry,” Agatha said quickly. “I didn’t mean to upset you. I just—“

“No, it’s okay,” Rio insisted. She sat up beside Agatha, sniffing and wiping her eyes. “I want to know everything about her. It just hurts that I missed it.”

“I’m sorry,” Agatha repeated, but this time it was for something completely different. “If I could go back and change things, I would. But—“

“But you can’t,” Rio finished for her. “Neither of us can.”

Agatha sighed. “Rio—“

“Let’s just finish these damn bunkbeds,” Rio said, pushing herself up off the floor.

Agatha stared at her for another moment, then nodded and stood up herself. “Okay. Let’s do this.”

Agatha was in a mood. She had spent the last two hours in an afterschool transition IEP meeting with Ollie’s EC teacher, his current teacher, and his future fourth grade teacher. Agatha could already tell that she wasn’t going to like this new teacher. She was older and even though everyone said she always had the best test scores in the school, she acted like teaching a kid with special needs was a huge inconvenience for her. Nevermind that Ollie was one of the smartest kids in his grade level, all she cared about was his sensory issues. Agatha was already bracing for a hell of a year the following school year.

Adding to her headache, she had walked out of the school to a missed call from Jen and a text to call her ASAP. Agatha knew it couldn’t possibly be good news, so she picked up an iced coffee before calling.

“Law Offices of Kale and Walters, Jennifer Kale speaking.”

“What’s up, bitch?”

“Agatha, this is my professional line, and I am your attorney.”

“You are also my best friend and I just sat through the most painful IEP meeting of my life, so suck it up, buttercup,” Agatha replied.

“And your bad mood has nothing to do with living with your ex?” Jen asked.

Agatha scowled. “We haven’t argued in three whole days.”

“That’s a good thing, isn’t it?” Jen asked. “You want to be civil so you can decide on a split custody agreement for the girls.”

“Yeah, I guess,” Agatha huffed.

“You guess?” Jen asked incredulously. Agatha heard a gasp through the phone. “Agatha Harkness, you’re not still in love with her, are you?”

“No!” Agatha exclaimed, perhaps a bit too quickly. “Of course not!”

“Agatha—”

“It’s been seventeen, almost eighteen years, Jen. That ship has sailed,” Agatha groused. “We’re two different people now, with too much history. I’m not in love with her.”

“Who are you trying to convince, me or yourself?” Jen asked.

Agatha didn’t reply. She turned her car off the road and onto the gravel drive to the ranch.

“Anyway, you said you needed to speak with me?” Agatha asked finally, changing the subject.

“Yeah,” Jen replied. “Your mother’s case was delayed— again.”

“Motherfucker,” Agatha huffed. “This is the third time, Jen! If we don’t get this settled soon—”

“I know, and I’m working on it,” Jen told her. “I’ve never seen a simple case like this get delayed so many times.”

“I’d say it was my mother, but I don’t know how she’d pull strings here in Montana,” Agatha replied.

“Exactly,” Jen agreed. “Also, Walters called her contacts at the state house and looked up all of Stark’s permit applications. On top of the vineyard he’s already built, he’s also planning on building a couple of housing developments, a hotel, and a golf course. And he’s put in permits to convert more agricultural property to commercial.”

“It sounds like he’s building a resort,” Agatha huffed.

“Yeah, but he chose a weird place to do it,” Jen replied. “It’s out in the middle of nowhere. It’s a two hour drive to the nearest airport.”

“For a genius billionaire playboy philanthropist, he sure doesn’t seem to be that bright,” Agatha replied. “Keep digging, Jen. If anyone can figure out what that prick’s up to, it’s you.”

“Will do,” Jen replied. “Later, bitch.”
