Rio cleared her throat. “I meant the coffee. Obviously. It’s delicious.”

“Yeah, obviously,” Tori scoffed as Nicky rolled her eyes.

The arena was filling up when they finally arrived. Tori and Nicky gave their horses a morning warm-up in the round pen before feeding them breakfast. Then they all made their way to the grandstand to watch the Saturday morning youth events: mutton busting and dummy roping. Then came the older kids with breakaway roping and team roping competitions. After lunch, they all made their way back to the stables so the girls could ready their horses.

Rio automatically went with Tori to groom and saddle Luna. Once the blue roan mare was sleek and shiny, they saddled her up and Tori led her toward the warm-up area. Rio watched as Tori walked Luna around the arena a few times before coming back to her. A few minutes later the others joined them. Nicky looked frustrated on Scratchy’s back, and Agatha looked harried.

“That stallion’s feeling himself today,” Agatha muttered.

Rio watched as Nicky rode the horse around the arena. He was clearly worked up and excited, prancing and chomping at the bit even as Nicky tried to keep him at a walk so he could warm up slowly.

“This is probably the busiest place he’s ever been and the most horses he’s been around at once,” Rio said.

“I suggested she scratch out, but of course she refused,” Agatha huffed. “She thinks he’s just excited to race.”

“I mean, she’s not wrong. That horse clearly loves to run,” Rio said. Nicky had a firm hand on the horse, and he responded to her commands, so Rio relaxed a bit. “She’ll be fine, Agatha. You and I rode horses wilder than that when we were racing.”

“Yeah, but that was us,” Agatha grumbled. “This is our daughter.”

Rio watched as the girls went through their warm-up routine, and even though Scratchy was clearly keyed up, he didn’t do anything that set off warning bells in her head. Instead of going back to the stands to watch, at an event this size, they stayed with the girls, helping them as they walked to the arena.

“Keep your hands relaxed and keep her tight on the first turn, she’ll whip around,” Rio said as she led Tori toward the arena.

“I know, Mami,” Tori replied, adjusting her grip on her reins.

“You got this, mija,” Rio assured her as they made their way to the alleyway. The music playing changed to Evanescence's “Bring Me to Life” as Tori’s name was announced over the loudspeaker, and Rio stood back as Tori kicked Luna into gear.

The mare burst into the arena at full speed, her long legs kicking up red dirt as she ran toward the first barrel. Tori took the turn tight and Luna whipped her back end around just like they’d practiced, launching herself in the opposite direction as Tori switched hands and cued a lead change. Tori’s hat flew off her head in the next turn, her dark hair blending with Luna’s mane as she ran. She got dangerously close to the barrel and it bobbed a bit, but Tori reached out expertly with one hand to steady it even as Luna ran toward the third barrel, and a moment later it was back upright. The third turn was picture perfect, and once they were in the home stretch Tori gave Luna a fierce kick and the mare galloped for the finish.

Even without seeing the final time, Rio knew it was a new record for the duo. But when the announcer read fifteen seven-oh-five, Rio’s jaw dropped.

“Holy shit, mija!” Rio shouted as Tori brought Luna to a stop in the alleyway.

“How’d we— Oh shit!” Tori exclaimed, seeing the time on the scoreboard for herself.

“That’s the fastest time so far!” Rio shouted victoriously, patting her daughter’s leg.

“That’s my fastest time ever!” Tori laughed, giddy with excitement.

“You did so good,” Rio praised.

“When does Nicky run?” Tori asked, turning Luna to trot out of the alleyway.

“I think there’s three racers between you,” Rio told her.

They found a spot to the side of the arena where they could watch Nicky’s ride. The three riders after Tori didn’t match her speed, so when Nicky’s turn came, Tori’s run was still the fastest in her age division. Rio could practically feel the excitement radiating off Tori as Nicky’s name was announced and “Man I Feel Like a Woman” by Shania Twain played over the speakers. From where they were standing Rio could see Scratchy stamping and prancing in the alleyway, and then Nicky gave him his head.

The beginning of the run was fast, but messy. Scratchy was clearly ready to run, but Nicky seemed uneasy in the saddle. Still, she took the first turn perfectly, if a little tight, signaling the lead change and switching hands. Rio thought she was pulling a bit tightly on the reins, not letting Scratchy have his full range of motion, but there was time to recover. The second turn was also tight, with Nicky leaning heavily in the saddle and holding onto her saddlehorn for balance.

In a moment of clarity as Nicky ran toward the third and final barrel, Rio knew that one of two things was about to happen: either Nicky would clear the last barrel and have the fastest time of the day, or she would lose her balance and fall. Rio wasn’t sure which, but she knew that one or the other was inevitable.

It seemed to happen in slow motion. Scratchy took the turn sharply, and his feet went too far out from under his body. He kicked up a cloud of red dirt as he tried to right himself, but he was too far over, and he fell heavily to his side. Nicky tried to catch herself with her left hand, and Rio saw the moment things went badly, when her face contorted in pain. She slipped out of the saddle as Scratchy stood back up and ran off.

Rio was over the railing before she could stop herself, but she wasn’t the first to reach her daughter. No, that was Agatha, a blur of brown hair as she sped across the arena.

“It’s okay,” Agatha soothed, cupping Nicky’s face as tears ran down her cheeks.

“What hurts, mija?” Rio asked.

“My— my arm,” Nicky replied, her breath coming in short gasps.

Rodeo staff got hold of Scratchy while paramedics came to help Nicky. Rio helped her to her feet and thankfully the only thing that seemed injured was her arm. She cradled it to her chest as they walked her out of the arena.

“Where’s Scratchy?” she asked. “And where’s Ollie?”

“The cowboys have Scratchy and Ollie…” Rio began, looking around. Agatha also just seemed to realize that her son wasn’t with them, and she looked around frantically. But when they looked toward the alleyway, they saw Tori there, holding Luna’s reins in one hand, and holding Ollie against her side with the other. Ollie’s eyes were wide and red with concerned tears, but Tori was clearly doing her best to calm him.

“I’ll get Ollie and the horse,” Rio volunteered, looking at Agatha. “You go with her to see the paramedics.”

“Thank you,” Agatha nodded.

Rio pressed a kiss to Nicky’s temple before sending them in one direction and heading toward Tori and Ollie. She flagged down the cowboy who was leading Scratchy and took his reins before leading him out of the arena.

“Is she okay?” Tori asked, blue eyes wide with fear. She was trying to hold it together for Ollie’s sake, but seeing Nicky fall had clearly shaken her.

“I think she’s probably got a broken wrist,” Rio replied.

“Oh no!” Ollie exclaimed. He looked at Scratchy with an angry expression and pulled his hand back like he was going to slap the horse’s flank. “Bad horse! Bad horse!”

Rio caught Ollie’s hand in her own before he could strike the animal. “Whoa, buddy, we don’t hit animals, even when we’re upset with them.”

“But he hurt my sister!” Ollie said angrily.

“No he didn’t,” Rio corrected. “He lost his balance and fell down. And did you see what he did when he fell? He was careful not to roll and crush your sister, because he knew he had a rider on his back. When he got up, he made sure he didn’t step on her. Ollie still looked mistrustful of the horse, but he didn’t try to hit him again. Rio sighed.

“Come on, we need to put these two away and then we can go check on Nicky.”

They took the horses to the barn, removed their tack, and put them away for the evening. Once they were settled, they hurried to where the ambulance was parked. Paramedics were wrapping Nicky’s arm to stabilize it.

“They want to take us to the hospital,” Agatha told Rio as they approached. “They’re pretty sure her wrist is fractured.”

“That’s what I figured,” Rio nodded. “We’ll follow you in the truck.”

“Are you sure? I mean, I can always take Ollie and you and Tori can enjoy the rest of the rodeo,” Agatha said.

“Agatha, that’s my daughter,” Rio said. “Do you really think Tori or I could sit here and watch the rodeo knowing that you guys are in the emergency room? Could you do that if it was Tori?”

“I guess not,” Agatha replied. “Sometimes I just forget that I’m not doing this all by myself anymore.”

“Well, I’m here now,” Rio assured her.

Before Agatha could get into the ambulance, however, a loud voice announced the winners of the event over the intercom. “... And in first place, with a time of fifteen point seven-zero-five seconds, Victoria Vidal riding Luna!”

“Go get your buckle!” Nicky said, pushing at her sister’s shoulders.

“Go get your prize, and then you and Mami and Ollie can meet us there. It’s okay.”

Tori nodded and ran past them, back toward the arena. Agatha watched her go with a faint smile still on her face before turning back to Rio.

“Divide and conquer?”

Rio nodded in agreement.

“They just took her back to x-ray,” Agatha explained. “They’re not sure if her wrist is fractured or sprained, but by the time we got here it had started swelling.” Agatha patted the second vinyl chair beside her. “Have a seat.”

The doctors admitted me to the hospital, put me on strict bed rest, gave me meds to stop the contractions and started steroids to speed up her lung development. Then she was born at thirty weeks and had to spend two months in the NICU before she could go home.”

Agatha gave her a sideways glance. “Of course you didn’t.”

“I almost called you.”

“My first night in the hospital by myself… I was so afraid that I was going to lose her. That I was going to lose the last piece of us that I had,” Agatha said, her voice barely above a whisper. “And then when she was born, and they wheeled me to the NICU to see her for the first time. “No, not at all,” Agatha agreed. “I suppose it’s only natural for them to want us together. I think that’s how most children from broken homes feel.”

“Is that what we are? A broken home?” Rio asked.

Agatha looked up at her with hooded eyes. “Hmm… Bent, I think. Not broken.” Then she took Rio’s hand in hers and tugged her toward the bar. “C’mon, Vidal. I’m buying you a drink.”

Later, Rio would blame it on the tequila. Or perhaps the whiskey. But in reality, she only had two drinks, and that wasn’t nearly enough to get her drunk. It did, however, make it even more difficult to take her eyes off the woman on her arm. When she was sixteen, she thought Agatha was the most beautiful girl she had ever seen. When she was twenty, she thought Agatha couldn’t possibly be more attractive. But those were lies. Agatha had aged like the finest wine, only getting better with the passing years.

“Mama, dance with us!” Ollie beckoned, grabbing Agatha’s hand and pulling her from the table she and Rio were sharing toward the dance floor as the band played a country line dance. Agatha easily fell into step with the others, laughing as Ollie tried his best to match the steps his sisters showed him. Rio sipped her whiskey and coke and watched them, the icy shell she had built around her heart slowly starting to melt.

Agatha had apologized. Agatha had actually, genuinely apologized. She was right, it didn’t undo the past, but it did change things. The past was gone, it couldn’t be changed, but as Rio watched the four of them dance to the music, the pain of it eased. This was now, this was the reality she had in the present, this moment. And if she wanted it, this could be her future, too. All she had to do was reach out and take it.

Rio downed the rest of her drink and made her way to the dancefloor as the music changed to a rhythmic western beat. She caught Agatha’s hand in hers and tugged her into her embrace, and the other woman went willingly, her free hand going to Rio’s shoulder and around her neck. Rio’s other hand found the small of Agatha’s back, and she began expertly moving their bodies together around the dancefloor in a quick two-step.

“Well, would you look at that,” Agatha said with a smirk, “cowgirl’s still got it.”

Rio looked down at her with a grin before spinning her out. Agatha’s hair whipped around her and she laughed loudly, relaxing even further into Rio’s lead as they danced around. Their hips swayed together, and one of Rio’s knees went between Agatha’s legs with each step. Agatha looked up at her with a fire in her blue eyes, and Rio felt drunk on more than just the alcohol she had been drinking.

The music changed again, this time to a Rolling Stones cover with slow-strumming guitar. Agatha started to step back, but Rio held her firmly.

“Don’t,” Rio whispered, a hint of desperation in her tone. Agatha studied her for a long moment, then nodded and stepped back into her embrace.

They swayed gently in each other’s arms, their bodies fitting together like puzzle pieces. Rio breathed in the scent of lavender perfume mixed with the faded tones of her own shampoo in Agatha’s hair, and she could feel the tip of Agatha’s nose nuzzling her neck.

Let’s do some living after we die…

“Wild horses couldn’t drag me away,” Agatha sang along softly. “Wild, wild horses… we’ll ride them someday.”

Throughout her life, whenever Rio thought about her future, it had never included Agatha. Sure, she had imagined it sometimes, but those thoughts were just fantasy. Even as a teenager, their lives had always seemed set on different paths, their relationship temporary. In the last few months, whenever she tried to picture the future, Agatha was there, but distant, a side character, only present when it pertained to their daughters.

Rio pulled back to look down into Agatha’s eyes, and for the first time in over twenty years, she allowed herself to imagine what it might be like to look into those eyes every day for the rest of her life.

Agatha reached up and took the hat from Rio’s head and placed it on her own with a smirk. “Penny for your thoughts, cowgirl?”

“I was just thinking, maybe our kids are onto something.”

Agatha’s eyes widened in surprise, but she didn’t seem disappointed. The hand that had been resting on Rio’s shoulders gently cupped the back of her neck, Agatha’s fingers stroking the soft baby hairs that grew there. Rio’s eyes went to Agatha’s lips, marveling at how soft they looked, and before she even realized what she was doing her head began to lower, eyes drooping slightly as she did so.

A tiny body collided with her legs a split-second later, and she almost headbutted Agatha’s forehead in surprise.

“Mama, Mami-Rio, something’s wrong with Nicky!” Ollie shouted.

Ollie’s words were more effective than being doused with a bucket of cold water. Rio and Agatha broke apart and followed the boy to where Tori and Nicky were sitting at one of the nearby tables. Tori was fanning Nicky with a napkin as she sipped cold water.

“I’m okay, really,” Nicky said, but her speech was a bit slurred. “I’m just really tired.”

“The adrenaline’s wearing off,” Rio surmised. “And you’re finally feeling the pain meds. The fact that it’s warm in here probably isn’t helping.”

“We should get you back to the hotel to rest,” Agatha concluded.

“Noooo!” Nicky whined as Rio helped her to her feet. “You guys were having such a good time.”

“It’s okay, mija, your health comes first,” Rio told her.

“But you were so close,” Nicky sighed, almost falling asleep on her feet even as Rio led her out of the concert hall to the parking lot.

Rio had to carry the sleeping teenager up to their hotel room, depositing her on the bed she was sharing with Tori. Agatha helped change her into her pajamas, and then they tucked her under the covers.

“You come wake us up if either of you need anything,” Agatha told Tori, her tone severe.

“Yes, ma’am,” Tori nodded.

“We’ll all set an alarm for two, that’s when she’ll need another pain pill,” Rio said. “And if the adrenaline’s wearing off, trust me, she’ll need it.”

They said their goodnights to Tori and Ollie before retiring to their own room. They didn’t speak as they each got ready for bed, moving around each other in the hotel room like a couple who had been doing it for decades. Agatha didn’t mention the almost-kiss, and neither did Rio.

Agatha was already in bed when Rio finished brushing her teeth and emerged from the bathroom. Rio gave her one last glance before turning down the covers on the pull-out bed and lying down, the frame creaking loudly as she did so. She laid on her side for several minutes, hoping sleep would come, but then her arm started falling asleep. She turned to her other side, and the bed groaned with the movement. After a few minutes in that position, her leg started to get stiff, so she rolled onto her back with a huff as the bed squeaked beneath her.

“Rio?”

She opened her eyes at the sound of Agatha’s voice. “Yeah?”

“Come to bed.”

“I’m already in bed,” Rio replied, confused.

Agatha sighed impatiently. “No, you idiot. Come to bed.”

Agatha pulled back the covers on her bed with a flourish. Rio sat up, looked at the pull-out bed she was sleeping on, then over at Agatha.

“Are you sure?”

“I’m not going to be able to sleep with all that squeaking,” Agatha said. “Plus, you have to drive us home tomorrow. I’d like for you to be well rested so you don’t fall asleep at the wheel and kill us all.”

Agatha did have a point, although Rio wasn’t sure how well she would sleep while sharing a bed with her. Even so, she got up off the pull-out bed and padded over to the king-size bed and crawled under the covers. She tucked herself in, careful to stay on her half of the giant mattress as she did so.

“Goodnight, Agatha,” she whispered into the dark.

“Goodnight, my— Goodnight, Rio.”

Chapter 22: Tori/Nicky

Tori parked her Subaru in front of the restaurant where Monica worked and took a deep, steadying breath. She could do this. It was going to be fine. More than fine. It was going to be great. Totally amazing. She pulled down the visor so that she could check her makeup and hair in the mirror. Maybe she had put on too much eyeliner… She could always go home and redo it and come back.

“So… Are we gonna get milkshakes or are you just gonna stare at yourself?” Nicky asked from the passenger seat.

Tori turned and glared at her. “Why are you even here?”

“Emotional support,” Nicky replied quickly. “Also, I wanted a milkshake and I can’t drive myself so… Call it killing two birds with one stone.”

A few days after her fall at the rodeo, Nicky had gone to see an orthopedist in Billings and they had fitted her with a teal cast that she had to wear for six weeks to give her wrist time to heal. She had used it as an excuse to get out of everything from driving to farm chores to final exams. Rio had coddled her for all of five seconds before Agatha saw straight through her excuses and made her do as much as she could one handed— with the exception of driving.

“I swear, if you screw this up for me…” Tori began.

“I would never!” Nicky gasped, clutching her chest with her good hand in mock offense. “I am an ally! And frankly, I am offended that you would even think—”

“Oh my god, you’re annoying,” Tori huffed, opening her door to get out. “Come on, let’s go.”

Nicky skipped beside her, seemingly bursting with energy. Tori kept her stride more measured, and tried to keep her hands from shaking with nerves.

The restaurant was crowded when they got inside, as the lunch rush was just finishing. There was a line at the counter to get ice cream and milkshakes, and Monica was clearly super busy filling all of the orders. Even so, she looked up when she heard the bell above the door ring, and she gave them a little wave and a bright smile before turning back to the customer she was serving.

“Crap,” Tori muttered. “She’s busy. Maybe we should come back another time.”

“No, it’ll be weird if you leave now,” Nicky argued. “Plus, I’m on my period. I need a mocha swirl milkshake like I need air to breathe.” An old man walked by with a plate of food and Nicky’s eyes followed it like some cracked out golden retriever. “And some seasoned fries. And maybe a bacon cheeseburger.”

“Jesus,” Tori muttered. “C’mon, let’s just get a table.”

“Yay!” Nicky exclaimed giddily, grabbing Tori’s arm and pulling her toward a booth.

A few minutes later, a harried Monica came over to them carrying a notepad.

“Hey, guys, what can I get for you?” she asked.

Tori looked up from her menu, eyes wide as she looked at the beautiful girl. “Uh… Hi.”

Monica’s cheeks flushed. “Hi.”

Nicky looked back and forth between them, hoping for any sign that the conversation would evolve, but they just kept staring at each other. Finally, she cleared her throat.

“Hey, Monica. I’ll have the bacon burger with mayo, lettuce, tomato, and pickles, seasoned fries, and a mocha swirl milkshake,” she said, stomach growling loudly as she spoke.

Monica managed to pull her gaze away from Tori long enough to jot down Nicky’s order on her notepad. Then she looked back up at the other girl.

“What do you want, Tori?”

“Gooutonadatewithme,” Tori said, her words coming out in a rush.

Monica’s eyes narrowed in confusion. “I’m sorry, I didn’t catch all that.”

Tori took a deep breath. “I was wondering if you’d like to go out on a date… with me.”

Nicky held her breath in anticipation as she looked between the two of them. Monica looked confused for a moment— she had clearly expected Tori to order food, not ask her out— but then she smiled softly.

“I’d love to,” she nodded.

Both sisters let out the breaths they were holding. Tori looked up with wide eyes. “Really?”

Monica giggled. “Yes, silly.”

“Cool, um… So, uh…”

“Monica! Order up for thirteen!” the cook called from the kitchen.

“I gotta go,” Monica said. “Tori, I’ll get you the grilled chicken sandwich and a lemon blueberry milkshake.”

“Oh, okay,” Tori said, deflating slightly as the other girl walked away.

As soon as Monica was out of earshot, Nicky let out a little whoop. “That’s what I’m talking about! Look at you, ladykiller!”

Tori scowled. “Please stop. Besides, we didn’t even make plans…”

“Tori, she agreed to go out with you,” Nicky said, speaking slowly so that her words hit home. “She likes you back. The rest is just details.”

“But what if I blow it? What if I don’t have any good ideas?” Tori asked.

“Well, what were you thinking?” Nicky asked.

“Um… I hadn’t actually planned that far ahead,” Tori said, blushing.

Nicky shook her head. “Oh my god, you’re a disaster.”

Monica came back with a tray of food and set it down on the table between them. She looked at Tori with a soft smile.

“It’s really busy, so I can’t stay and talk,” she explained. Then she slid a piece of paper across the tabletop. “But this is my number. Call me sometime and we’ll figure out when we can go on a date, okay?”

Tori grabbed the paper and clutched it tight. “Ye— Yeah, that sounds great. I’ll call you and we’ll… Yeah.”

Monica giggled and hurried back to the kitchen.  Tori unfolded the paper and looked at the phone number like it held all the secrets of the universe. Nicky just shook her head in exasperation and looked down at her food.

“Fucking lesbians,” she muttered before stuffing a handful of fries in her mouth.

Tori and Nicky were convinced that her mothers were the most confusing people on the planet. It had been two weeks since the rodeo. Two weeks since they had danced together and held each other closer than anyone could possibly consider platonic. Two weeks since they had seen them almost kiss— right before Nicky almost passed out from her pain meds, terrible timing on her part. Two weeks since Tori had gone to their hotel room for Nicky’s next dose of pain meds to find them in bed together. Sure, they had been fully dressed, but they were wrapped around each other so tightly that it would’ve taken a crowbar to pry them apart.

But they still weren’t together, at least as far as the girls could tell. Agatha still slept in her bedroom, Rio slept in the guest room. Rio left for work at the clinic early in the mornings, and Agatha worked around the ranch all day. They barely seemed to interact outside of the occasional dinner that they all ate together, and that only happened when both women happened to finish work at a reasonable hour.

When they did all have dinner together, Nicky and Tori were never sure what to expect. Some evenings their mothers seemed perfectly fine, carrying on conversation like normal people. Some evenings they barely spoke at all, barely even looked at one another. And some evenings the dinner table discussions would devolve into arguments that neither teenager could really figure out the cause of, just that at least one woman would leave the table in a huff, stomping their boots as they went.

“What have you three been up to today?” Rio asked after they had all filled their plates.

“Wade taught me how to rope today!” Ollie announced proudly as soon as they sat down.

“He did?” Agatha asked, her brows shooting up.

“Mmhmm,” Ollie hummed around a bite of his food. Everyone else had meatloaf, but since he wasn’t a fan of the texture, he was eating nuggets instead. He swallowed and started talking again. “Maybe someday I can rodeo like Nicky and Tori.”

“Heck yeah, buddy!” Tori said, raising her hand for a high-five, which Ollie eagerly returned.

“We’ll make a cowboy out of you yet,” Nicky teased.

“What about you girls?” Rio asked.

“Not much,” Tori began. “We went to town for lunch and stopped by the diner. And then—”

“Tori asked Monica out on a date!” Nicky exclaimed.

Tori glared at her. “I was just about to tell them that.”

“You were taking too long,” Nicky shrugged.

“You asked her out?” Agatha asked, excitedly.

“Yeah,” Tori said, blushing.

“Good for you, mija,” Rio praised with a grin. “What are you doing for your date?”

“I’m not sure yet,” Tori replied quickly.

“She doesn’t have a clue,” Nicky told them.

“I just don’t want it to suck!” Tori defended. “I have lots of ideas, but none of them seem good enough.”

“What kind of ideas?” Rio asked.

“Well… I was thinking maybe we could go see the new Star Wars movie.”

“Does Monica like Star Wars?” Agatha asked.

“Um… I don’t know,” Tori replied. “But she likes stars.”

“Movies aren’t great for first dates, mija,” Rio told her gently. “You want to do something that inspires conversation. Save movies for down the road.”

“Movies are only good dates when you want to makeout the entire time,” Agatha supplied.

“Mama!” Tori exclaimed.

Agatha shrugged. “It’s true. Tell them, Rio.”

Rio’s cheeks flushed. “Your mama has a point.”

“Are you two speaking from experience?” Nicky asked, pointing her fork at them and raising her brows in a way that made her look exactly like Agatha.

“Maybe,” Agatha said cheekily, cutting her eyes at Rio, who just shrugged innocently.

“Okay, well if you two have so much experience, maybe you can give Tori some ideas,” Nicky challenged.

“Yes, please,” Tori agreed eagerly.

“First, start with what Monica likes,” Rio said. “Do you know any of her interests? Her hobbies?”

“She’s really into space, like NASA and planets and stuff,” Tori told them. “She’s in the school choir, so I think she likes music.”

“I think there’s a planetarium in Bozeman,” Agatha suggested.

“That’s a good idea,” Rio agreed.

“Bozeman’s really far away, though,” Tori argued.

“The drive will give you two a chance to talk and get to know each other,” Rio pointed out.

“But what if it’s awkward?”

“Then let it be awkward,” Agatha shrugged. “Dating is hard, hon. And relationships are even harder. There’s gonna be times that things are awkward. Part of the test is seeing how the two of you work through it. That’s one way you know if things will work out or not.”

“Okay, so the planetarium is a maybe,” Tori agreed. “What are some things we could do that are actually in town?”

“There’s the bowling alley,” Rio suggested.

“Ew, that place smells like old man feet,” Nicky said, wrinkling her nose in disgust.

“Does she ride?” Agatha asked. “You could have her come over and go for a trail ride.”

“I’m not sure if she rides,” Tori told them.

“You could always teach her,” Agatha said, wiggling her eyebrows suggestively.

“Mama!” Tori exclaimed, at the same time Rio said, “Agatha!”

“What?” Agatha asked, feigning innocence. “I meant it literally! Geez!”

“Sure you did,” Rio said, rolling her eyes.

“This is why Tori isn’t inviting her over here for the first date,” Nicky said. “You guys are weird.”

“Hey!” Agatha exclaimed. “We are your mothers! We are not weird!”

“Eventually you do have to invite her over, mija,” Rio added. “I want to meet her properly.”

“Can I show her my chickens?” Ollie asked, looking up from his nuggets for the first time in the entire conversation.

“Oh my god,” Tori groaned, putting her head in her hands. Nicky patted her back supportively.

“Seriously, when you guys were dating, what did you do for fun around here?” Nicky asked, trying to change the subject. “Please keep it PG-13.”

“Well, we both liked riding, so we did a lot of that,” Rio supplied. “We would hang out together at the jackpots and rodeos on the weekends. We went to a lot of barn dances during the summer.”

“Do you remember when we went to that bar out in Silver Springs?” Agatha asked.

“And you almost got arrested for punching that drunk woman?” Rio replied.

“I only punched her because that skank was hitting on you!” Agatha defended.

“She was hitting on anything with legs,” Rio pointed out.

“Yeah, but you were mine,” Agatha growled.

The use of past tense wasn’t lost on anyone at the table. Tori and Nicky shot each other a meaningful look, and Rio’s eyes dropped to her plate. There was a moment of awkward silence before Agatha cleared her throat.

“Do you remember that one time we drove up into the park and watched a wolf pack tear apart a dead elk?” Agatha asked.

Rio looked up, her eyes gleaming. “Yep.”

“That’s… different,” Tori remarked, staring at them with wide eyes.

“When they were finished eating, your Mama jumped out of the truck and started howling with them,” Rio said, her lips turning up in a slight grin. “I thought she was gonna get herself killed.”

“Holy shit—ake mushrooms,” Nicky said, covering the curse word when Agatha glared at her. “That’s crazy!”

“I was a little wild back then,” Agatha admitted, daintily wiping her mouth with a cloth napkin.

“A little?” Rio teased.

“Maybe more than a little,” Agatha conceded.

“Also, I’m pretty sure you haven’t grown out of it, mi am—” Rio cut herself off, her mouth snapping shut so fast that they could hear her teeth gnash together.

They all knew what she was going to say. Tori stared at her mother in open-mouthed shock as Nicky looked rapidly between Rio and Agatha, her head on a swivel. Agatha’s face went pale, and though she was clearly trying to keep her expression neutral, she couldn’t hide the tears that immediately welled up in her eyes.

“I’m, uh, not very hungry,” Rio said quickly, standing up from the table. “I think I’m gonna head upstairs and shower before bed.”

“But it’s not even seven o’clock!” Tori argued.

“I’m just tired, mija,” Rio said, and she suddenly looked it. Her eyes flicked from Tori’s face to Nicky’s and finally to Agatha’s, but only for a moment. “Good night.”

With that, Rio hurried from the dining room, and a moment later then could hear the rapid thud of her boots as she went up the stairs.

Nicky immediately turned to her other mother. “What are you doing?”

“What are you talking about?” Agatha asked, confused by the accusation in her voice.

“Go after her!” Nicky insisted.

“Your Mami is tired,” Agatha argued. “If she wants to call it a night early, then—”

“She called you my love,” Tori said.

“No she didn’t,” Agatha scoffed.

“She almost did,” Nicky disagreed.

“Yeah, she was about to say mi amor,” Tori added.

“No she wasn’t,” Agatha insisted.

“Mama, we’re not stupid,” Nicky told her.

“I never said you were,” Agatha said.

“Then stop treating us like we are!” Tori demanded.

Ollie dropped his fork onto his plate with a loud clatter, interrupting their argument and drawing their attention. “I’m done. Can I go play on my tablet now?”

Agatha sighed. “Yes, honey, you can go play.”

Ollie nodded and got up from the table. Once he was out of the room, Agatha sighed and looked back at her daughters.

“My relationship with your Mami is… very fragile,” she began.

Nicky groaned. “You guys are killing me.”

“It didn’t look very fragile when you were sleeping together,” Tori muttered.

“Wh— How did you—?” Agatha stuttered.

“When I came into your room to get Nicky’s pain meds, I saw you two together,” Tori told her.

“Nothing happened,” Agatha told them.

“Yeah, but you two looked really cozy,” Tori pointed out.

“Mama, at least admit that you’re still in love with each other,” Nicky sighed.

“It’s not that simple, sweetheart,” Agatha said, her tone bittersweet. “Even if we did still love each other— and I’m not saying we do— there’s a lot of history and emotional baggage between us. Plus, we have to think about what’s best for the two of you, and Ollie.”

“What’s best for us would be our moms together,” Nicky said, as if it were the most obvious thing in the world.

“Yeah, you two could at least try. For us,” Tori added.

Agatha sighed. “Girls… Things are good between your Mami and I right now. Okay? Maybe not perfect, but good, far better than I could’ve imagined after… everything. We’re here together, you’re together, we’re figuring this co-parenting thing out after years of doing it alone. And it seems to be working pretty well. So why complicate the good thing we have going by trying to fix our romantic relationship?”

“Because you love each other,” Tori said.

“It’s really obvious to literally everyone,” Nicky told her.

Agatha shook her head. “I’m sorry girls, but the answer is no. You’re just going to have to trust that as the adults in the situation, Rio and I know what’s best for all of us. And right now, that’s not the two of us being together romantically. That’s all there is to it.”

“But—” Tori began.

“No buts,” Agatha said, cutting her off. “This is not up for discussion. It’s a decision between Rio and I, and you two need to drop it. Do you understand me?”

Tori sighed. “Yes.”

Agatha looked at Nicky, whose jaw was still set stubbornly, but after a brief staring match Nicky dropped her gaze and gave a tiny nod in agreement.

“Good,” Agatha said. “Now… go upstairs and finish your homework.”

“But Mama, it’s summer break. We don’t have homework,” Tori pointed out.

“Just… go,” Agatha said, waving vaguely toward the door with one hand and lowering her face into the other hand in exasperation.

The girls begrudgingly made their way upstairs. Once they were in their room, Nicky collapsed onto her bed with a huff, spreading out like a starfish, while Tori sat in her pale purple office chair and pulled her knees up to her chest.

“I think we need a new plan,” Nicky said, staring up at the ceiling.

“A new plan?” Tori repeated. “You say that like we’ve had a plan.”

“I’m just saying, maybe we need to try harder to get them together,” Nicky told her.

Tori sighed. “You heard what Mama said.”

“Yeah, and we both know it was a bunch of bullshit,” Nicky argued. “They’re in love, Tori! It’s so painfully obvious.”

“But what if they get together and then break up?” Tori asked. “Maybe Mama’s right and how things are right now is the best we can ask for.”

“No,” Nicky insisted, sitting up and shaking her head. “I refuse to accept that. They’re meant to be together. Don’t you think so?”

“Of course I think so,” Tori agreed. “But—”

“Then no buts,” Nicky said, repeating her mother’s words from earlier. “They’re going to get together. We have to keep believing that. Do you believe it?”

“Yes,” Tori replied, nodding with certainty.

“Good,” Nicky said, lying back. “Now we just gotta figure out a way to make them realize it.”

They were quiet for a few minutes, both silently scheming. Finally, Tori spoke up.

“Maybe we could get them to go on a date?” she suggested. “We’d have to be sneaky so they don’t realize it’s a date, but—”

“Good idea. But you heard them downstairs. Our options in this town are limited,” Nicky replied.

“Yeah, I don’t even know how you’d find a wolf pack eating an elk,” Tori agreed. “But maybe we can get them to go for a ride together.”

Nicky raised her head off the bed. “You’re a genius.”

Tori beamed. “Yep. That’s why I’m TweedleDee and you’re TweedleDum.”

“I am not TweedleDum!”

“Yes you are!”

“No I’m not!”

“Yes you are!”

Chapter 23: Rio

Chapter Summary

Parent Trap Stage 3

Rio braced herself against the stall door and held the newborn foal still as Hank drew blood. A few feet away, the owner held the broodmare back as she glared at both vets, ears pinned back in fury that they would dare touch her baby.

“There we go,” Hank said, limping away. A horse had stepped on his foot earlier in the week, so now instead of a cowboy boot he wore one of those plastic orthopedic walking boots on one foot. Rio let go of the foal and it wobbled back over to its mother, and the three of them left the stall. Hank gave the vial to Rio, and she squeezed the droplets onto the test strips. Once everything was done and both foal and mare seemed healthy, they bid the rancher goodbye and headed back to the truck.

“You’ve been a real lifesaver this week, Rio,” Hank sighed as they got back into the truck. “Helping me with all these house calls, staying after hours… I don’t know what I’d do without you.”

“It’s all in a day’s work, Doc,” Rio assured him as she pulled out of the drive.

“Wish we still had your girl working with us,” Hank said. “She’ll make one hell of a vet one day.”

Rio smiled softly. “Yeah, I hope so.”

“You’re a lucky woman,” Hank told her. “I always hoped one of my kids would decide to follow in my footsteps. Thought I’d pass my practice on to them, if they did. But none of them stuck around. Henry’s a doctor in Bozeman. Sadie’s a teacher, she married a financial analyst and they live somewhere in New Jersey. I’m proud of them, don’t get me wrong, but I miss ‘em something fierce.”

“Maybe one of your grandkids will take it over,” Rio suggested.

Hank just chuckled. “No, I think I’ll be retired long before they grow up. But that’s alright.” He reached over and patted her shoulder. “The clinic will be in good hands with you and Nicky.”

Rio’s eyes widened and she turned to him in shock before quickly looking back at the road, her head turning back and forth comically as the words processed. “Doc… Hank…”

“I don’t expect you to say anything right now, and I don’t want to pressure you,” he said. “But just know, if you’re interested, when the day comes, my practice is yours.”

Rio swallowed hard, tears welling up in her eyes. “Thank— Thank you, Hank. That means a lot to me.”

Hank gave her shoulder one more firm pat before turning back to look out the windshield. “Let’s stop at the Rambeau’s place for lunch, shall we? It’s hot enough, I could go for some ice cream.”

“I think that sounds like a great idea,” Rio agreed. “Apparently my daughter, Tori, asked Monica out on a date.”

“Ah, to be young and in love,” Hank said whimsically. “With none of the complications that adulthood manufactures. Sometimes I think we would all be better off if we learned to love the way children do.”

“Irresponsibly?” Rio asked.

“Turquoise is your favorite,” Tori pointed out. Rio knew that was true; the color of the ring even matched the wrap on Tori’s cast that she still wore on her arm.

“Yeah,” Nicky said, her voice rising. “But how did he know that?”

“Maybe it was just a lucky guess?” Tori suggested.

“No,” Rio sighed. “He knew.”

Rio reached into her pocket and pulled out a small clipping from the Westview Gazette, the town’s local newspaper. It was a story about a local rodeo from four years ago. There was a picture of Nicky on Bullseye, the gelding she had raced before Scratchy, and the horse was wearing teal leg wraps and a teal saddle pad. The band on the cowboy hat Nicky was wearing in the picture had a small turquoise medallion on the band.

“I found a whole stash of newspaper clippings and photographs hidden in the jewelry box,” Rio told her. “He kept track of you. He watched you grow up.”

“Then why didn’t he say something?” Nicky demanded, standing and pacing away from the bed. Her hands closed into fists at her side, and for a moment Rio thought she might punch something and break her other hand. It’s what Rio had done, when she found the clippings. Her knuckles were still bruised, and there was a new hole in the wood panel walls of her parents’ old bedroom.

“I don’t know, mija,” Rio said.

“Why didn’t he tell you about me?” Nicky asked, turning back toward her. “Why didn’t he try to get to know me? I don’t understand.”

Nicky was shaking now, and Rio knew exactly how she felt: like a bottle of soda, shaken and shaken until it was about to burst. She stood from the bed and put her hands on both of Nicky’s shoulders, and the girl stilled beneath her touch.

“I don’t understand it either,” Rio told her, sighing sadly. “I don’t know why and I guess we never will. I hate that he couldn’t get over whatever issues he had with your other grandfather enough to know you. I’ll never understand why he made the choices he did. I just… I wanted you to know that in some capacity, he was watching you. He was thinking of you.”

Nicky looked down at the ring in her hand. She turned it over, ran her finger over the flattened inside. Then she slipped it onto her ring finger, and it fit perfectly.

“I’m only wearing this because today is his funeral,” Nicky told her. “I don’t forgive him.”

“That’s okay,” Rio assured her.

“I’m going to keep it because it was my grandmother’s, and I never knew her,” Nicky continued. “Do you, I mean— Do you think she would’ve liked that?”

Rio heard the question Nicky left unspoken as well. Do you think she would’ve liked me?

“I think she would’ve loved that,” Rio nodded. “And I know she would’ve loved you.”

Nicky nodded and looked down at the ring on her finger. “Okay. Then I’ll wear it.”

Rio pulled her daughter into a tight hug. “Thank you, mija.”

Nicky looked up at her with concern. “Are you okay, Mami?”

Rio squeezed her a little tighter at the question. The honest answer was no, she wasn’t okay. She was tired and angry and sad. She didn’t miss her father’s yelling or his domineering nature or the way he always thought his way was the right way and screw everyone else. But she missed his hugs. She missed his laugh. She missed having someone in her corner rooting for her no matter how badly she felt she had screwed up. She missed her Papi.

Another set of arms wrapped around Rio from behind, and she realized Tori had also wrapped her in a hug. She also realized that instead of answering Nicky’s question, she had started crying.

“No, Nicky, I’m not okay,” she admitted. “But I will be.”

Rio returned to work at the clinic two days after the funeral. Dr. McCoy had assured her that she could take more time if she needed it, but after a week of dealing with her father’s arrangements, Rio needed to get back to normal.

There was nothing quite like treating an abscess on a two thousand pound bull that wanted nothing more than to stomp Rio into the ground to distract her from her grief.

“Hold him steady!” Rio shouted, holding a scalpel in her hand. Logan had hold of a rope and was holding the bull’s head to the side so that Rio could get to the giant lump on the side of his neck. The bull was in a chute, so he couldn’t stomp her, but one wrong move and he could easily pin her wrist between his body and the metal chute and do some serious damage. Rio angled the blade just right and as soon as the bull stopped fighting, she sliced into the bottom of the wound, releasing a stream of disgusting mess to crash onto the floor.

“Oh, Jesus!” Logan exclaimed, turning his head and gagging.

Rio’s nose wrinkled at the stench, but she kept working. She pressed on the lump, careful not to cause the bull any more discomfort than necessary, and more pus drained from the wound. Once it stopped draining, she mixed a solution of clean water and iodine and pumped it into the opening, cleansing the abscess from the inside out. Then she packed the empty space full of antibiotic-soaked packing and they let the bull go back into his pen.

“Good work, doc,” Logan said, patting her on the back as he passed.

Rio nodded, stripping her gloves off with disgust. She had barely finished throwing them away when her phone started ringing in her pocket. She looked at the caller ID with surprise and glanced around to make sure she was alone before answering the call.

“Val?” she asked. She hadn’t expected a call from any of her old coworkers.

“Hey, stranger!” the woman on the line replied, her accent thick as it rattled from the speaker. Ever since her phone got waterlogged the regular speaker had stopped working and Rio had to answer calls on speakerphone. “How are you? How’s Montana?”

“Hey,” Rio sighed. “I’m good. Montana’s… Montana’s great, actually.”

“Nice,” Val said. “How’s your dad? How’s Tori? Has she settled in, or does she miss West Coast life?”

“Tori’s doing great,” Rio said, leaning against the wall. “My dad, well… He actually passed away about a week ago. Heart attack.”

“Oh, Rio, I’m so sorry to hear that,” Val replied. “Is there anything I can do?”

“No, everything’s taken care of. But I appreciate the offer,” Rio assured her. “I’m sorry I haven’t kept in touch. There’s just been a lot happening.”

“I’m sure you’ve been busy,” Val acknowledged. “Listen, I can’t talk long, but I wanted to give you a heads up about something.”

“Yeah?” Rio asked.

“Hela’s leaving.”

Rio blinked, both surprised and confused. She didn’t know what her old boss leaving had to do with her. “Oh?”

“Yeah, she’s transferring to a zoo in Denmark, believe it or not,” Val continued.

“Oh, well… Good for her, I guess,” Rio said.

“That means there’s going to be an opening for team lead,” Val told her. “And nobody here has the level of experience they want for a position like that.”

“I’m sure they’ll find someone,” Rio remarked.

“Rio, they want you back.”

Rio’s heart pounded in her chest. “That’s… I mean, I’m flattered, but…”

“I just wanted to give you a heads up so that you have some time to think before the zoo executives actually call you,” Val continued. “You know how they are, they’ll want a decision almost immediately.”

“Well, I could tell them my decision right now,” Rio replied. “The answer is no.”

“Seriously?” Val asked. “This is the kind of promotion you were working toward for years, Rio! The pay’s better, and there’s travel to other zoos to build the breeding programs. This is literally your dream job.”

“It was my dream job,” Rio told her. “But… things change.”

Val sighed. “Okay. But, hey, keep in touch, okay?”

“Yeah, you too,” Rio said. “And thanks for the heads up. Talk to you soon.”

Rio sighed and hung up the phone. Val was right, at one point the lead position on her team back at the zoo was her dream job. What Val didn’t know was that five years ago, when Hela got the position, Rio had also been offered the job. She had turned it down at the time because the job required frequent travel to other zoos, and Tori had been eleven at the time, too young to stay by herself, and Rio was raising her by herself. So the job went to Hela, and Rio had wondered for years if she had made the right decision. Now the universe was once again dangling it in front of her like a carrot, and once again she was refusing to take it.

“Hey, doc?” Logan called, poking his head out of the office. “You’ve got an appointment waiting for you in the lobby.”

Rio pushed all thoughts of San Diego out of her mind to focus on the here and now, and headed back into the office. She went to the lobby where a man and a girl who looked to be around Nicky and Tori’s age were waiting with an older black labrador. The man looked smarmy, and like he would rather be anywhere but here. The girl looked like she had been crying. Rahne handed Rio a file folder, and Rio read the name off the label.

“Tony Stark and Jarvis?” Rio called, looking up from the chart. The man and girl stood up, and the girl beckoned for the dog to follow, but when the lab tried to stand, its back end collapsed. Rio watched sadly as the girl tried her best to get the dog to stand, but it couldn’t seem to do so. The girl got to her knees and tried to help, but the man just stood by and watched.

“Could we get some help over here?” he asked finally.

Rio went behind the desk and grabbed a towel, which she put under the dog’s belly. She was then able to lift his back end so that he could make it to the exam room. Once they were there, the girl sat on the floor and held the dog’s head in her lap while the man sat in the corner, checking something on his phone.

“I’m Dr. Vidal,” Rio said, introducing herself. “I take it that you’re Mr. Stark?”

“You can call me Tony,” the man said, barely glancing up from his phone.

“Great,” Rio said through clenched teeth. She looked down at the girl. “And you are?”

“Morgan,” she said. “You’re Tori and Nicky’s mom?”

Rio forced her expression to remain neutral. She remembered what her daughters had said about Morgan Stark with clarity, but she had to remain professional. She was just a kid, after all.

“Yep, that’s me,” Rio replied. “What seems to be going on with Jarvis? I noticed in his chart he’s been dealing with hip dysplasia and arthritis for a little over a year now. And now it looks like he’s having some weakness in his back end?”

“I’ve been giving him his medicine every day like Dr. McCoy said,” Morgan told her. “And I’ve been giving him short walks to try and help him lose weight. But yesterday he was really having a hard time when we were walking, and he could barely make it up the steps to our deck. This morning when I took him outside he just collapsed and couldn’t get up. He even peed on himself, but I cleaned him up before we came in.”

“That’s good. You sound like a very responsible dog owner, Morgan,” Rio assured her. “Has he been eating and drinking like normal?”

“He didn’t eat all of his dinner last night, and he didn’t eat any breakfast this morning,” Morgan told her. “I know I’m not supposed to give him any scraps, but I offered him some of my breakfast burrito this morning to see if he’d eat it, and he didn’t even eat that. But he’s been drinking water whenever I bring the bowl to him.”

“I see,” Rio nodded. “Mr. Stark, is there anything you’d like to add?”

The man looked up from his phone. “Oh, no, it’s her dog. I just pay the bills. She knows way more than I do about him.”

Rio looked back at Morgan. “Can you think of anything he might’ve done to injure himself or cause any soreness?”

“No,” Morgan replied, shaking her head. “He’s been getting lazier and lazier lately. I just thought he was getting old.”

Rio nodded. “Okay. I’d like to get some x-rays to rule out any injuries and see how his arthritis is progressing. And possibly some blood work to rule out any other underlying issues. You guys just hang tight, and I’ll get Logan to come and take him back for the x-rays, okay?”

Rio ordered the tests, but she already had a sinking feeling about what was wrong. The tests only confirmed it, and twenty minutes later she went back into the exam room.

“I’m afraid I don’t have good news,” Rio told them. “Jarvis’s blood results came back normal, but his x-rays show clear progression of his arthritis. That’s why he’s unable to walk at this point.”

“Okay, so… You can give him medication for that, right?” Morgan asked.

“The only thing we can give him at this point is pain medication to manage his symptoms,” Rio told her. “It might alleviate his pain to the point where he can start walking for short distances again. But the medications will make him very sleepy. So he’ll be comfortable, but he won’t be the same active dog you’re used to. And long term use of these strong pain medications can cause damage to important organs, like his liver and kidneys.”

“So… What do we do?” Morgan asked, looking from Rio to her father, who was typing something on his phone.

“At this point, it becomes a quality of life discussion,” Rio said sadly. “It’s really up to you and your dad. Like I said, we can try to manage Jarvis’s pain, but…”

Tony Stark finally put his phone down and sighed dramatically. “He’s old, sweetie. He can’t walk. He’s hurting. I think you know what the doctor’s getting at.”

Morgan cuddled the dog’s head close to her chest. “No. He can get better.”

“I’m so sorry, sweetie,” Rio said, squatting down beside the girl to pat her shoulder. The chart said that the dog was twelve years old, so the girl had probably grown up with him.

“Don’t worry, kiddo,” Tony said, kneeling down on her opposite side. “If you want, we can get another dog. A new puppy. What do you say?”

“I don’t want a puppy,” Morgan said, tears welling up in her eyes.

Tony looked at Rio with a beseeching expression, but Rio wasn’t touching that discussion with a ten foot pole. She stood and cleared her throat.

“Why don’t I just leave you two to discuss your options for a few minutes?”

“No, that won’t be necessary,” Tony said. “I think today’s the day.”

Morgan looked up sharply. “What? Daddy, no!”

“Morgan, he’s in pain,” Tony said, the tiniest modicum of sympathy in his tone and expression. “He’s old. He’s lived a good life. He’s been ridiculously spoiled with the best treats and toys and food that money could buy. And he’s been lucky enough to be loved by the best girl in the world.” Tony wrapped his arm around Morgan’s shoulders. “But now it’s time to let him go, before he’s in even more pain.”

Rio prepared the solution, and a few minutes later, the old dog was gone. Morgan went out to their car, too emotional to stay inside, while Rio helped Tony fill out the paperwork to have the dog’s remains cremated.

“Thank you for everything, Dr. Vidal,” the man said earnestly.

“You’re very welcome, Mr. Stark.”

“Please, like I said earlier, it’s Tony. I did want to ask you a question before I go,” Tony said. “Your father recently passed, didn’t he?”

“Yes, he did,” Rio replied. “Did you know him?”

“Not personally, no, but you know… Small town.” Tony studied her with a calculating gaze. “Rumor has it that you’re going to be selling his property?”

“Yes, it’s an older model singlewide. Sits on about six acres with a barn and horse pastures,” Rio told him. “Are you interested?”

“I’m interested in a wide variety of parcels in this town,” Tony answered. “One more thing, rumor has it you’re well acquainted with Agatha Harkness?”

Rio could feel her hackles rising. “That’s one way of putting it, yes.”

“You’re living with her, aren’t you?” Stark asked.

Rio’s fist clenched in her pocket. “I don’t see how that’s any of my business.”

“I suppose it’s not,” Tony shrugged. “But, a word of advice to you: I wouldn’t get too comfortable on the Harkness ranch.”

“Oh? And why’s that?” Rio asked.

“Well, I know Agatha has been experiencing some difficulty with the inheritance process. It’s causing her some financial strain.”

“I wouldn’t know anything about that,” Rio said.

“Oh. That’s interesting,” Tony said. “Well, I just wanted to give you a heads up. I would hate for this town to lose an asset such as yourself.” He gave her a smile that he probably thought made ladies weak in the knees, but only gave Rio the ick. “Have a good day, Dr. Vidal.”

“You, too, Mr. Stark,” Rio replied.

Stark headed out to the parking lot, pausing in the doorway just long enough to put on his overpriced sunglasses. Rio frowned as she watched him go.

“What the hell was that about?” Rahne asked, smacking the gum she was chewing.

“No idea,” Rio told her honestly.

But she intended to find out.

Chapter 26: Agatha

Chapter Notes

A reminder that flashbacks are in italics

Also, I don’t actually know how inheritance law works. Just go with it.

Agatha sat in her grandfather’s office— no, her office— and stared at the financial reports in front of her. Statement after statement from the bank, from her accountant, and they all said one thing: the ranch was going under. It was prime season for the cattle to be sold, but she couldn’t sell anything until the lawsuit with her mother was resolved. So now she had hundreds of cattle eating more and more food, costing her more money, and not bringing in a single dime.

On the other side of her desk sat an aerial image of her ranch that Jen had sent her the day before. Almost in the center of the plot, almost exactly where her house currently sat, was a large swath that was marked out with crosshatches. At the bottom of the page, written in Tony Stark’s barely legible handwriting, was the explanation for the plan: Big Sky Data Center. After her contacts had pulled all of Stark’s permits, they had dug deeper, and found plans to build what would be the third largest data center in the country.

To anyone not from Montana, it probably made sense to build a giant data center in the middle of nowhere. But Agatha knew that something like that would cause far more damage than it was worth. Not only would it destroy the land it was built on, making it useless for ranching or for wild animals, it would produce so much noise that it would disturb local wildlife, it would use and pollute fresh water sources that the animals needed. Agatha had thought that Stark’s plans to make this place a tourist attraction were bad, but somehow this was even worse than all of that.

Agatha looked up at the wall above her desk. Photos of her family members, six generations of Harknesses that had lived on this land and worked it. If Agatha lost the ranch to her mother, she had no doubt that Evanora would sell out as soon as possible, and Stark would be the first one to sweep in and purchase the land. He’d break ground on the data center before the ink was dry on the paperwork.

The first time Agatha ever thought about running the ranch, she was ten years old. It was the third summer that her mother had sent her to stay with her grandfather. Jim Harkness wasn’t any kinder than his daughter, and if possible he seemed even less adept at raising a girl than Evanora. But somehow those things actually seemed to work in Agatha’s favor. He worked her like a boy during the day, never softening his approach because of her sex. Unlike Evanora, who had resented being made to work her entire childhood, Agatha thrived. She loved the animals, loved the land, and she loved the freedom she had here, so far away from the rigid expectations of her mother and the boarding school Evanora enrolled her in.

 

“What are you doing?” Agatha asked, finding her grandfather in the office one night after dinner.

He looked up at her from his work, his long white hair falling in his face. Then he shook his head dismissively.

“You should be in bed,” he told her. “Did Lilia help you with your hair?”

“Yes,” Agatha replied, bristling at the mention of the female ranch hand who had taken up the unofficial role of babysitter for her. She didn’t go back to bed, instead making her way over to her grandfather’s desk and peering down at the ledgers he was studying. “What’s this?”

He studied her, as if debating whether or not it was worth explaining to her, but after a moment he relented. “Last year’s ledgers. I’ve been offered a deal on the Laufey farm. I’m trying to determine if it’s a good purchase or not.”

“How do you know if it’s good or not?” Agatha asked.

“It’s all a great big math problem,” he told her. “Every cow we raise needs food, it needs shelter. All that costs money. Feed costs, land taxes… It can all be broken down to figure out the exact price to raise a cow up to size for processing. Then we compare that price to the market price of the animal when it’s sold for beef. If the cost of raising it is less than the cost it sells for, then we’ve made a profit. We have to make sure we can get enough money for each cow to make it worth our time and effort raising them out.”

Agatha looked down at the ledgers. “I like math.”

Jim chuckled. “You don’t need to worry yourself with it, little one. This will be your uncle Elijah’s job when he’s older. Or Eric’s, if he ever settles down.”

“Why can’t it be my job?” Agatha asked.

Hiram shook his head. “This is a job for cowboys, Agatha. That’s just how it is.”

 

Agatha didn’t take well to being told that she couldn’t do something. Her grandfather’s words had had the opposite effect than intended. It had lit a fire in her, and she had been determined to prove him wrong. Perhaps if Grandpa Jim had realized that his two sons would be dead before him, Enoch from a drunk driving accident and Elijah from an early heart attack, he would’ve spent more time preparing her. But Agatha wasn’t sure her grandfather could’ve anticipated the battles that she would fight to keep this place running.

“Knock knock.”

Agatha looked up from her work. Rio stood in the doorway, her hair damp from a shower, wearing a fresh t-shirt and sweatpants.

“Hey, you,” Agatha greeted, tucking the papers away. “How was your first day back at work?”

“It was fine. I smelled like shit and antiseptic, so I decided to shower before dinner,” Rio replied, coming closer. “What are you working on?”

“Oh, you know, same shit, different day,” Agatha sighed.

Rio sat in one of the stiff chairs across from her, crossing one leg over the other. She looked around at the office, her nose crinkling. “Do you ever think about changing things up in here? Making it your own.”

Agatha shrugged. “I’ve considered it. But I don’t want to come across as soft, you know?”

“You, soft?” Rio asked, a grin tugging at the corners of her lips. “Never, baby.”

Agatha grinned back at her. The last week hadn’t been easy, but things between them had been good. Rio spent every night in her bed, and Agatha would have it no other way. They hadn’t told the kids about the change in their relationship yet, but Agatha thought perhaps over dinner…

“I had an interesting client today,” Rio said conversationally.

“Oh yeah?”

“One of your neighbors. Tony Stark.”

Agatha bit back the curse that threatened to escape her at the mere mention of the name. “What was he in for?”

“Had to put his dog down,” Rio answered.

“I’m sure he was distraught,” Agatha replied, fighting to keep her voice neutral. “But that sounds pretty routine for you. What made it interesting?”

“As he was leaving, he mentioned that he knew that we were living together, and he advised me not to get too comfortable here. He said something about you having issues with the inheritance process and financial difficulties,” Rio told her, studying her reactions. “Any of that mean anything to you?”

Agatha could feel her blood pressure rising with every word Rio said. She wanted to throw something. Instead she stood from her chair and paced behind the desk.

“That cocksucker,” she muttered. “He had no right to tell you any of that, no fucking right.”

“So what he said is true?” Rio asked. “What the hell was he talking about, Agatha?”

Agatha gripped the back of her chair so tightly that her knuckles turned white. “It’s not something you have to worry about, my love.”

“If it’s something that could make you lose this place, it definitely sounds like I should be worried,” Rio argued.

“It’s not going to get to that,” Agatha huffed. “Jen’s taking care of it.”

“Taking care of what, exactly?” Rio asked.

“It’s none of your—”

“Agatha, I swear to god, do not say it’s none of my business if it could possibly affect where I live and where our children live,” Rio said, sitting forward in her chair. “Tell me what is happening. If we’re going to make whatever this is between us work, then we have to communicate. We have to tell each other things, even when we don’t want to.”

Agatha walked around to the front of her desk and leaned against the edge. Rio was right, she knew it, but she hated to admit it. Even before her grandfather had passed, Agatha had carried a lot of the weight of this place on her shoulders, all by herself. Having someone to share those burdens with might be nice, but it wouldn’t be an easy adjustment.

“My grandfather’s will split his assets fifty-fifty between me and my mother,” Agatha told her. “I don’t know what he hoped to achieve with that. I don’t know if he thought my mother would suddenly show some interest in this place, or if he thought forcing us to share it would heal our relationship.”

Rio snorted. “Fat chance of that.”

“Yeah, exactly,” Agatha agreed. “I think he thought, worst case scenario, I would buy out my mother’s share of the place and at the very least she would get her fair share and be taken care of for the remainder of her life. Not that she wasn’t living comfortably already, but you know… I think he felt like it was wrong to leave his daughter with nothing, even if she is a raging cunt.”

“Understandable,” Rio agreed.

Agatha sighed. “Well, instead of just taking a generous buyout, my mother decided to contest the will. She claims that Grandpa Jim wasn’t of sound mind when he had the will redone. See, he had an earlier will that split everything between her and her brothers, but both of them had passed away since he had that one drawn up, so if we had left that one as it was, she would’ve inherited everything by default.”

“What does Stark have to do with all this?” Rio asked.

“He wants to buy this place and build a data center here,” Agatha told her. “Has for years. He’s made multiple offers, and I’ve always turned him down. All I can figure is that he plans to buy it from my mother as soon as she wins her case.”

“Will she? Win her case?” Rio asked.

“Absolutely not,” Agatha said. “We have multiple witnesses and doctors who will attest that my grandfather was of sound mind when he had his will redone.”

“So what’s the problem?”

Agatha looked back down at the ledgers on her desk. “The problem is that while the lawsuit is in litigation, I can’t do anything with the ranch’s assets. I can’t buy anything, I can’t sell anything. I’ve been making ends meet by tapping into my personal portfolio, but I can’t make that work indefinitely. And for some reason, the suit keeps getting delayed with the courts.”

Rio’s eyes narrowed as she put the pieces together. “So even if you do win against your mother, the process might push you out of business in the long run?”

Agatha nodded. “That’s my biggest concern.”

Rio looked down, studying the antique rug that Agatha’s great-grandmother had purchased from Europe and had shipped to the ranch in the thirties. Agatha hated when she did that; it made it impossible for her to read the emotions in Rio’s expressions. Finally, the other woman looked back up at her.

“Do you ever think of just letting it all go?” Rio asked.

Agatha almost laughed at the question. Every fucking day, she wanted to say. Sometimes multiple times a day. Multiple times in her life she had contemplated setting the whole place on fire, taking the insurance money, and running away. But she didn’t. She stayed. She fought. And every time, she came out on top.

She would do it again.

“No,” she answered instead. “I can’t just let it go, Rio.”

“Why not?” Rio asked. “I mean, fuck, Agatha, your grandpa didn’t even want you to have this ranch in the first place.”

“Yeah, well, life fucked up his misogynistic plans,” Agatha replied. “And now it’s mine. I’m not about to give it up.”

“Why, though?” Rio demanded. 

Agatha shook her head. “Not everyone is like you, Rio. Not everyone that grew up here hates this place. Not everyone wants to travel and leave their mark on every corner of the world. Some of us are happy with just one little corner.”

“I wouldn’t call it little,” Rio said. “You’re a shark, Agatha, the way you’ve made this place grow.”

“I know. Because I’m exceptional,” Agatha said proudly.

“Exactly. You could’ve done anything else. You’re a businesswoman, you’re a genius. You could’ve gotten a job with any investment firm in the country and they would’ve been lucky to have you. But you stayed here, and I just… I’ve never understood it.” Rio shook her head. “When was the last time you even took a break and got away from this place? There’s a whole world outside Westview, and you’re just stuck here.”

“I’m not stuck,” Agatha argued. “I can leave whenever I damn well please.”

“Then why don’t you?” Rio glared up at the photos on the wall. “Why do you work so hard to prove yourself to a bunch of ghosts?”

“I don’t care about them,” Agatha spat. “Fuck them! I do this for me. Because I love this place. And I do this for the kids, for their future.”

Rio scoffed. “And what if none of them want it?”

Agatha sat back against the desk. The thought that none of her children would want this place had occurred to her before, but she had mostly ignored it. That was an issue to address later. But it seemed like later had suddenly become now.

She took a deep breath and looked up at the ceiling, at the wood beams her ancestors had hewn from logs they cut in the nearby hills.

“If none of them want it, then fuck it,” Agatha replied, defeated. “Stark can have it and build his goddamn data center. He can build a hundred of them for all I care.”

Rio seemed to realize she had pushed too far. Her face softened, as did her tone when she said her name, “Agatha—”

A bell rang in the other room, and Agatha stood, smoothing down the dress she was wearing as she did so.

“C’mon, dinner’s ready,” she said, reaching out for Rio’s hand.

Rio looked at her hand, recognizing an olive branch when it was offered, and took it, her calloused palm rubbing against Agatha’s. They made their way to the dining room, where the kids were already seated. All three of them had concerned expressions.

“Why the long faces?” Agatha asked, taking the seat at the head of the table. Rio sat beside her and placed her cell phone on the table.

“We heard you arguing,” Ollie said.

“Oh? You were eavesdropping again?” Agatha asked, raising a brow at her daughters.

“No, we couldn’t understand what you were saying. Just that you were arguing. Loudly,” Nicky replied.

“We thought you two were done fighting,” Tori said. “Since you’re, you know, together now.”

Rio’s head shot up from where she had been looking down at the screen of her phone. “What?”

“Mami, please,” Nicky scoffed. “You two are so obvious.”

Rio’s cheeks flushed. Agatha rolled her eyes.

“Your Mami and I—”

“You’re sleeping together,” Tori pointed out. “Mami has been sneaking into your room for the past week.”

“How did you—?” Rio began.

“You’re not that sneaky,” Tori remarked. “You stubbed your toe the first night and I’m pretty sure the entire house heard you cussing.”

“Mami has had a hard week. She needed a shoulder to cry on,” Agatha told them.

“She needed more than a shoulder,” Nicky mumbled.

“Excuse me? What is that supposed to mean?” Agatha asked.

“Mama, we share the same Amazon account,” Nicky deadpanned.

Agatha thought for a moment, then the realization struck her. “Oh.”

“I told you we should’ve ordered that from a different website, but no, you had to get one day shipping and you didn’t care if it helped line Jeffrey Bezos’s pockets,” Rio muttered.

“I stand by that decision,” Agatha said, taking a sip from her wine.

“Ew,” Tori said, wrinkling her nose at them.

“What did they order?” Ollie asked.

“Grown up toys,” Nicky replied.

“Like what?” Ollie asked.

“Boring stuff, you wouldn’t like them,” Nicky continued. She pointed her fork at Agatha. “We can discuss my future therapy bills later.”

Agatha rolled her eyes. She really shouldn’t be surprised that her daughters were dramatic. They belonged to her and Rio, afterall.

Rio’s cell phone buzzed again, and Rio clicked the button to refuse the call. Agatha’s brow raised, but Rio just went back to the conversation at hand.

“Well, this isn’t how we planned on telling you, but yes, your Mama and I are… together. We’re trying, anyway.”

“But you were fighting,” Ollie puzzled.

“Yes, we tend to do that,” Agatha said. “Mami and I don’t always see eye to eye about things.”

Ollie’s brow wrinkled in confusion. “But you’re the same height.”

“It’s a figure of speech, mijo. It means we sometimes disagree about things,” Rio said.

Agatha froze. She looked at Rio. “You called him mijo.”

Rio’s eyes widened. Clearly the endearment had slipped out without any forethought. “Is that not okay, or—?”

“No, it’s fine,” Agatha said, blinking away sudden tears.

Ollie didn’t seem fazed at all. He just stabbed at the carrots on his plate. “Eli said his moms argue sometimes.”

“I bet they do,” Agatha commented. Rio’s phone buzzed again, and this time Agatha read the caller ID before Rio hit the red button: Hela.

“So since you’re together, that means we’re staying here, right?” Tori asked.

“I—” Rio began, but her phone buzzed again. She scowled as she looked down at the screen. “I’m sorry.”

“Just take the call, my love,” Agatha said, but her eyes narrowed in suspicion.

Rio sighed and stood, picking up her phone before walking out of the room. “Hello?”

“Rio, darling, it’s good to hear your voice.”

Agatha shouldn’t feel jealous. She knew that. But something about the way the other woman purred Rio, darling as a greeting set her teeth on edge.

“Who’s Hela?” Nicky asked, clearly having seen the name on Rio’s caller ID as well.

“Hela?” Tori repeated, tone thick with confusion. “The only Hela I know is Mami’s old boss. They worked together at the zoo.”

Somehow Tori’s explanation didn’t make Agatha feel any more at ease. She stood abruptly, the legs of her chair scraping noisily on the floor as she did so, and all three kids looked at her with concern.

“I’m just gonna go check on Mami,” she said, laying her napkin on the table beside her plate.

She searched downstairs for Rio, until she heard her voice distantly through the office. Instead of inside, she found the French doors off her office open, and saw Rio standing on the porch. Agatha made her way outside, the evening air slightly chilled as it hit her bare skin. Rio’s back was to her, and she was looking out over the distant hills. Thunderstorm clouds were starting to make their way across the mountains, and the air smelled like rain. Rio didn’t know Agatha was there, and before she could get her attention, the woman on the phone spoke again.

“This is the opportunity of a lifetime, Rio. It’s your dream job. You worked toward it for years. I don’t understand why you would say no.”

“Like I told you before, and like I told Val earlier today, sometimes dreams change,” Rio replied. “I’m happy where I am.”

The woman on the phone scoffed. “Please, Rio. We both know you spent your entire life trying to get away from that place. Now that your father is gone, I don’t understand why you’re so attached.”

“It’s a long story,” Rio sighed.

“Don’t tell me you’ve met someone,” Hela said, laughing as if the thought was absurd. When Rio didn’t reply, her laughter became even louder. “Oh my word! That’s it, isn’t it? You met someone?”

“More like we reconnected,” Rio replied, her voice low.

“And what? Now you’re playing house with some cowgirl?” Hela asked. “Rio, you’ve got too much potential to waste it on that kind of life.”

“I’m happy, Hela,” Rio said, but her tone didn’t sound happy. She sounded defeated.

“Uh-huh. I’m going to pretend that I believe you,” Hela said. “Are you sure about this, Rio? An offer like this only comes around once every decade or so. If you say no, someone else is sure to jump at the opportunity.”

“I’m sure,” Rio told her.

There was silence on the other end of the line for a moment, then: “Wow. This cowgirl of yours must be something else, for you to throw away your dreams like this. Tell me something, Rio: Would she do the same for you?”

Rio didn’t respond, and that was all the answer the other woman needed. It was all the answer that Agatha needed, too.

“At least tell me you’ll think about it,” the woman on the phone said.

“Hela—”

“You should at least take some time to consider it, Rio,” Hela pointed out. “Hell, talk to your cowgirl about it. Maybe she’ll surprise you.”

Rio was quiet for another minute. Then she sighed. “Fine. I’ll consider it.”

“Good girl,” Hela said, and the familiarity with which she said the words made Agatha’s hackles rise again. “I’ll talk to you soon, darling.”

“Goodbye, Hela,” Rio said before disconnecting the call. She shoved her phone into her pocket and leaned heavily on the railing of the porch, like there was an invisible weight on her shoulders that was too heavy to bear.

“Everything okay?” Agatha asked, trying and failing to keep her voice neutral.

Rio turned, her lips set into a deep frown. “Yeah, everything’s fine.” She made to walk past Agatha, back into the house. “Has Remy brought in dessert, or—”

“Rio,” Agatha said, stopping her with a hand on her arm. “Who’s Hela?”

Rio’s frown deepened. “No one. Let’s go back inside.”

“Tori said she was your boss in San Diego,” Agatha said. “But she seemed a little familiar for an old colleague.”

Rio’s expression morphed to confusion and then surprise. “Are you seriously jealous right now?”

“I don’t know. Do I have a reason to be?”

“No,” Rio insisted. “God, no, Agatha. Hela was my boss for the last, I dunno, five or six years. Before that we worked on the same team.”

“You never dated?” Agatha asked.

Rio bristled. “We went out once. That was enough for me to realize it wasn’t going anywhere.”

“And why was that?”

“Because Hela doesn’t want kids. Can’t stand them, actually,” Rio replied. “Not that it’s any of your business.”

Rio stomped past Agatha into the office as thunder began rumbling in the distance.

“So what did she want?” Agatha asked, following behind her.

“Nothing,” Rio said.

“Didn’t sound like nothing,” Agatha remarked, crossing her arms over her chest. “Weren’t you the one saying we need to communicate with one another, even when we don’t want to?”

Rio glared at her, clearly incensed that Agatha was turning her words back around on her. Personally, Agatha thought it was a clever move, but she tried to push down the smirk that wanted to take over her expression.

“Fine,” Rio huffed. “Hela’s moving to another zoo. She called to offer me her position as team leader at the San Diego Zoo. That’s all.”

“Oh,” Agatha said, sitting in the same chair Rio had sat in earlier. But you said you would stay, she thought. “And you told her you’d consider it?”

“I told her no, but when she kept pushing I said I’d consider it,” Rio grumbled. “But I’m not— I mean— I’m here, now. With you. So it’s not really up for consideration, is it?”

“I don’t know. Is it?” Agatha asked. You said you would stay. The words replayed in her mind over and over again.

Rio’s eyes narrowed. “What do you mean?”

“I mean, if you and I weren’t together, would you accept the job?” Agatha asked. Just because RIo had said she would stay didn’t mean that was what she really wanted.

“That’s not— It doesn’t matter. We are together,” Rio replied.

“But if we weren’t,” Agatha pressed.

“I don’t know!” Rio exclaimed. “What do you want me to say, Agatha?”

“Just tell me the truth, Rio. If you and I weren’t together, would you accept this job?”

“Yes!” Rio replied. “Yes, okay? It’s my fucking dream job, Agatha!
