I’d be leading the program and San Diego, I’d be traveling to other zoos around the world to work on conservation projects, I’d be leading seminars… It’s literally everything that I worked toward for years. Is that what you want me to say?”

Agatha felt tears welling up in her eyes. “And you’re okay giving all that up?”

“Yes!” Rio insisted, but there was a shadow in her eyes. As soon as the word left her lips, her entire demeanor deflated. “I mean… I guess. I— Fuck!”

There it was. A tiny part of Agatha wanted to say I told you so, but there was no triumph in it. For once in her life, she had wanted to be wrong, and for Rio to be right. But, deep down, she had known.

“What do you want, Rio?” Agatha asked, her tone even in spite of the turmoil she felt inside.

Rio looked up at the pictures on the wall before turning to Agatha. She dropped to her knees in front of the chair and took Agatha’s hands in hers.

“Come with me,” Rio said, her voice thick with desperation. “Let your mother have this place, or sell it to Stark, I don’t care. Just… Let it go, and come with me.” Rio squeezed Agatha’s hands. “We could be happy, Agatha. You, me, the kids. We could have a life together away from here. Just… Let this place go and come with me.”

It was a question Rio hadn’t dared to ask seventeen years before. Back then neither of them had been willing to ask the other one to let go of their dreams. But at the time, they only had themselves to think about. Now there was more on the line.

Agatha looked around the office and shook her head. “Rio… No. I can’t.”

Rio’s expression turned stony. She stood and pulled her hands free of Agatha’s.

“That’s not fucking fair,” she spat. “You can’t just expect me to give up my dreams while you get to keep yours, Agatha.”

“I don’t expect you to give yours up,” Agatha replied.

“Then what?” Rio said. “Because we can’t be together if I’m in California and you’re here. It doesn’t work like that.”

“I know,” Agatha agreed sadly.

“You know? You—” Rio’s face fell as the realization hit her. “You bitch.”

“Rio—” Agatha began.

“No, you know what, fuck you!” Rio shouted. “If you don’t want to fight for this, for us—”

“What’s there to fight for?” Agatha asked.

Rio looked at her with tears streaming down her face. “Fuck you, Agatha, and fuck this house and this ranch and this whole town. Fuck all of it. I’m done.”

Rio stormed out of the office, just as the rain started pouring down outside. A strong gust of wind blew the curtains around the French doors, and Agatha automatically moved to close them.

“Mama?”

Agatha turned away from the windows. Nicky and Tori were standing in the doorway of the office, and Ollie was peering around from behind them. From the looks on their faces, Agatha surmised that they had heard everything.

“I’m sorry, kids,” she said.

Tori took off running upstairs, crying as she went. Nicky looked back and forth between her mother and her retreating sister before taking off upstairs after Tori. Agatha sighed and made her way toward Ollie.

“You and Mami broke up again?” Ollie asked.

“Yeah, buddy. It looks that way,” Agatha replied.

“But you love her.”

Agatha took a deep breath. “I’m afraid that, unlike in the movies, sometimes that just isn’t enough.”

“Oh,” Ollie said sadly. He reached out and took Agatha’s hand in his. “I’m sorry, Mama. I love you.”

His words surprised her. “I love you too, Ollie. But, why are you sorry?”

“Because you’re hurting,” he said. “When Mami was hurting, I asked Lilia how to fix it. And she said that sometimes when people are hurting, we can’t do anything to fix it, so we just tell them we’re sorry and that we love them.”

“Yes, that’s… That’s exactly right,” Agatha told him. “Ollie, I think I’m gonna go to bed early tonight. Can you make sure you pick up your toys and take a shower before bed?”

“Okay, Mama,” he said, turning to wrap his arms around her waist. He gave her a quick squeeze before releasing her and heading to the living room.

Agatha made her way upstairs. The door to the guest room was shut, and all she could hear from inside was silence. The door to Nicky and Tori’s room was also shut, but she could hear the faint sound of crying inside. She should check on her daughters, she knew, but she couldn’t bring herself to do it. Instead she went to her own room and closed the door behind her.

She changed out of her dress, brushed her teeth, and got ready for bed. It felt like her body was going through the motions on autopilot, but her mind was numb. She should be upset. Agatha had the self-awareness to realize that she should feel upset. But instead she just felt numb. This ending was inevitable, it had been from the start, and she had known it. It was true seventeen years ago, and it was true now. As much as she loved Rio and she knew that Rio loved her, sometimes that wasn’t enough. Sometimes two people just weren’t meant to be together.

She crawled into bed and the sheets were cold. She buried her face in the pillow, only for the smell of Rio’s shampoo filled her senses. Agatha hugged the pillow to her chest, breathing in the clean, earthy scent, and the tears began to flow.

She wasn’t sure they would ever stop.

Chapter 27: Tori

Chapter Notes

I am just… so sorry

Tori stared at the ceiling of the bedroom she shared with Nicky, tears silently streaming down the sides of her face. She was lying in Nicky’s bed, the top bunk, because if she was on the bottom bunk Nicky would see her cry. Below her, she could hear Nicky slamming drawers shut and kicking things. She heard a nasty crack and wondered exactly what Nicky had broken in her anger, but she didn’t have the heart to look to check.

“This is bullshit,” Nicky said, kicking the side of the bedframe again. “She can’t just leave! I mean… That’s child abandonment.”

The day after they overheard their moms fighting, Rio had announced that she had accepted a job back at the zoo in San Diego. Within days she had arranged for an apartment, packed her things, and put in her two-week notice with Dr. McCoy’s office. It didn’t matter what Tori or Nicky said, Rio was dead set in her decision to move back to California.

Tori’s heart ached for her sister. She couldn’t imagine how hurt Nicky felt. She had just met her other mother for the first time in her life, and now Rio was leaving her. At least before Rio hadn’t known Agatha was pregnant, hadn’t known that Nicky existed. Now she knew that Nicky was here, and she was still leaving. If Tori really thought about it, she was angry on her sister’s behalf.

On top of how angry she was for Nicky, Tori was dealing with her own shit. Rio had leased a two bedroom apartment, so Tori had a room with her if she wanted it. But Rio was giving her the option to go back to San Diego with her, or stay with Agatha and Nicky and Ollie. She was seventeen, Rio reasoned, so she was old enough to make the decision on her own. But Tori didn’t feel old enough to make that decision and, frankly, it felt unfair that her mother would put that kind of pressure on her. How was she supposed to choose between the mom who had raised her and the family she had just met? Rio and Agatha had both assured her that there would be no hard feelings no matter what she chose, but that didn’t make her feel any better.

“There has to be something we can do to change her mind,” Nicky said. “I mean, they were together! They were back together for a whole week. They were sleeping together and everything! They clearly love each other. We just need to make Mami see that and—”

“Nicky, just stop,” Tori sighed, wiping angrily at the wet lines on her cheeks. “Just.. stop. Please.”

Nicky clambered up the ladder at the side of the bed. “But Tori, we can’t just give up.”

“Why not? They have,” Tori said, sitting up. “Don’t you get that? Our moms have given up, Nicky. They know that they love each other, and they don’t care. It’s not enough. They know that what they’re doing is hurting us. They don’t care. They just… They’ve given up. They’re gonna do what they’re gonna do and there’s nothing we can do to change it.”

“But—” Nicky began.

“I need to get out of here,” Tori said, throwing her blankets aside. “Move.”

“What?” Nicky asked.

“Move please,” Tori repeated.

Nicky scrambled off the ladder and Tori hurriedly climbed down after her. She kicked off the pajama pants she had been lazing around in and yanked on a pair of jean shorts and sneakers before heading out the door. She knew that her hair was a mess, and her eyeliner was probably smeared, but she didn’t care.

“Where are you going?” Nicky called after her.

“I just need to get out of here,” Tori said, hurrying toward the front door.

“Tori? You okay, sweetheart?” Agatha called as she ran past her office.

No, Tori was not okay. But nobody in her family could fix this. Tori grabbed her purse and keys off the hook by the door, went outside to her car, and took off. She wasn’t even sure where she was going at first, but a few minutes later the outline of the town rose up against the horizon.

She didn’t know she had started crying again until the air conditioning inside the Rambeau’s diner hit her cheeks. She wiped angrily at her cheeks as she looked around the restaurant, but Monica wasn’t there. She searched her pockets for her phone to text her, but apparently she had left it at home.

“Tori?”

She turned around to see Coach Danvers sitting in a booth with Maria, Monica’s other mom, sharing lunch.

“Hi, Coach Danvers. Hi, Captain Rambeau. Is Monica around?” she asked.

“She just went back to grab something out of the coolers,” Maria replied. “I’ll go get her.”

“Thanks.” Tori shuffled nervously as the woman disappeared behind the counter into the kitchen. She looked back at Coach Danvers and tried to force a smile, but it wouldn’t quite come. “Are you having a good summer, Coach?”

“So far so good. You?” Coach Danvers asked, her voice thick with concern.

“Never better,” Tori lied. A minute later, Maria returned with Monica in tow.

“Hey!” Monica greeted, her smile fading slightly as she took in Tori’s expression. “Is everything okay?”

“Yeah, um… Well, no. I was hoping we could talk if you have a few minutes,” Tori told her.

“Yeah, I can take a break,” Monica assured her. “Mom, can you tell Uncle Robby that I’m taking my break?”

“Sure thing, baby girl,” Maria replied.

Monica took Tori’s hand in hers and led her out of the restaurant and down the street. It was a short walk to the park, and once they were there Monica led her over to a white gazebo surrounded by bushes where they could have some privacy and shade. She sat Tori on one of the wooden benches inside and pulled a napkin out of her pocket, which she used to wipe Tori’s face.

“What’s going on?” Monica asked, her voice soft and steady. “Is this about your grandfather?”

“No,” Tori replied, shaking her head. “No, it’s…” She took a deep breath. “I think I might be moving.”

Monica’s hand froze in mid-air where she was wiping off Tori’s smeared eyeliner. She dropped her hand to her lap, looking at Tori with a mixture of surprise and sadness.

“Oh,” she said, confused. “Why?”

Tori sighed and explained everything. Monica knew a little about Tori’s family history, but now Tori gave her the full story, including everything that had happened over the last couple of weeks, ending with Rio’s offer to let Tori choose whether she would go with her to California or stay in Westview.

“I don’t want to go back,” Tori said, her voice cracking as she tried to hold in her emotions. “I love it here. I love the ranch, and I love Agatha and Nicky and Ollie and the animals. I love my friends and I lo— I mean, I really like you. I don’t want to leave. But—”

“But she’s your mom,” Monica concluded for her, her tone sad but nonjudgmental.

“Yeah,” Tori sighed. “It’s just been the two of us for my entire life. She’s always been my best friend. She was so young when she had me, it’s kind of like we grew up together. And I just… I can’t let her go back by herself. I just can’t.”

Monica nodded. “I understand.”

“You do?” Tori asked.

“My mom’s military,” Monica reminded her. “It’s always really hard whenever she leaves on deployment. I know it’s not the same thing, but I at least kind of understand.”

Tori nodded. “Nicky still thinks we can convince them to get back together.”

“Nicky’s stubborn,” Monica said. “But your moms are grown women. You can’t control the choices they make, unfortunately. Even if it feels like the wrong choice for so many reasons.”

Tori felt tears welling up in her eyes again. “You’re really smart. You know that?”

Monica grinned bashfully and lowered her head. “I try.”

“I really like you,” Tori said, her tone sad. She took Monica’s hand in hers and squeezed. “You won’t be mad at me if I leave with my mom?”

Monica squeezed her hands. “No. Of course not.” She shook her head. “Is there a part of me that wants to ask you to stay? Hell yes. I like you a lot, too, Tori. But… I understand why you feel like you need to go. And besides, you moving doesn’t have to mean we break up.”

Tori’s mood immediately lightened. “It doesn’t?”

“No, silly, of course not,” Monica replied with a smile. “It just means we have to try the whole long distance thing a little earlier than we planned, that’s all. I mean, eventually we were both gonna go to college anyway. I’m planning on joining the Air Force like my moms. We were gonna have to be in a long-distance relationship eventually, so… why not start now?”

“You’d really do that for me?” Tori asked.

“What can I say? I really, really like you, Tori Vidal.”

Tori had never been in love before. She wasn’t entirely sure what it felt like. She always thought it was a racing heart and sweaty palms and butterflies. And maybe she had felt that when she first met Monica. But now it was different. Now, being around Monica made her feel peace, and like her heart was so big it might just burst out of her chest.

“I really, really like you, too.”

Monica looked up at her, with her big brown eyes and soft smile, and Tori felt her heart skip a beat.

“I’d really like to kiss you,” Tori said, the words leaving her lips before she could really process them. It was the truth, though. She wanted to kiss Monica so badly that it hurt. She didn’t know when she might get the opportunity again, and she thought that if she moved back to California without ever kissing Monica, she would definitely regret it for a very long time.

Monica’s smile just broadened and she gave Tori a small nod. They were sitting so close that their knees bumped together, so close that Tori could smell the floral shampoo that Monica used in her hair. Tori leaned in closer, her eyes flickering from Monica’s lips to her eyes and back again, until they were a hair’s breadth away from one another. Then, blessedly, Monica closed that last tiny bit of distance and pressed their lips together.

When Tori was six, she kissed Mikey Taylor on the playground, and he retaliated by throwing a rock at her. When she was twelve, she kissed her best friend, and afterward the girl called her a freak. When she was fourteen, she had her first real girlfriend, and they kissed, but it was awkward and wet and they both had garlic breath. They broke up shortly after that. But that limited experience made Tori think that everyone was lying when they talked about how nice kissing felt. All those colorful descriptions of kisses being like fireworks couldn’t possibly be true.

Her first kiss with Monica, though? It was better than the Fourth of July. Monica’s lips were even softer and fuller than Tori had imagined, and when they kissed it was like a series of tiny explosions going off behind Tori’s eyes and deeper, down in the depths of her chest. For a moment Tori thought she might actually burst with it, and shoot off the bench like a bottle rocket, straight into the air. But Monica was right there, her hands in Tori’s, her lips on hers, grounding her to that spot so that she couldn’t fly away. 

They held hands on the way back to the diner, not because Monica was leading her, but because they didn’t want to let go of each other. And when they got to Tori’s car, Monica kissed her again, a quick peck this time, but a kiss nonetheless.

“I’ll talk to you later,” Monica said, giving her a wave before going back inside.

When Tori got back to the ranch, Agatha and Nicky were both on the porch waiting for her.

“You left your phone on the bed,” Nicky told her, voice accusatory.

“I know, I’m sorry, I know I’m not supposed to drive anywhere without my phone. I just forgot,” Tori said.

Agatha didn’t fuss. She just pulled her into a tight hug. “It’s okay, sweetheart. We were just worried.”

When Agatha finally let her go, Tori looked around at the two of them. “I went to talk to Monica. I needed to talk to someone who’s outside of… all this,” she explained, moving her finger in a circle to imply them and the rest of their family dynamic. “Plus I needed to talk to her about something.”

Agatha seemed to know exactly what Tori was talking about before she even said it, based on the bittersweet expression on her face. Nicky, however, was slower on the uptake. Or, if she knew, she was in denial.

“What did you have to talk to her about?” Nicky asked.

Tori took a deep breath, bracing for the worst. Agatha wrapped her arm around her shoulders.

“I had to tell her that I was moving back to San Diego with Mami.”

Agatha squeezed Tori’s shoulders, and Tori could tell that she was trying to be supportive and not cry or show her real emotions. Nicky, on the other hand, had the opposite reaction.

“No!” she exclaimed. “You can’t go! Mama, tell her she can’t go!”

“It’s her decision,” Agatha replied. She bent to kiss Tori’s forehead “I don’t want you to go, sweetheart, but I understand why you’re going.”

Tori nodded. Nicky let out a loud shriek of frustration.

“That’s not okay! I can’t— I can’t lose both of you! I just got you back,” Nicky said, her anger evolving into tears. “Mama—”

“Do you want to go with them?” Agatha asked.

Nicky’s eyes widened in surprise. “What?”

Agatha swallowed hard. “It’s not really fair that Rio gave Tori a choice, but I’m not giving you one. So, if you want, you can move to San Diego with them. I’m sure Mami’s apartment has room for one more.”

Nicky froze in indecision for a long minute. She looked from Agatha to Tori and back again, before her face crumpled into tears again.

“I can’t— I can’t just leave!” she sobbed, reaching for Tori. “But I don’t want you to go, either.”

“I know,” Tori said, hugging her tight. “I’m sorry.”

“You won’t hate me if I don’t go with you?” Nicky asked.

“Not as long as you won’t hate me if I go,” Tori assured her.

“I could never hate you. You’re my sister and I love you,” Nicky told her.

Tori sighed, hugging her sister tighter. “I love you, too.”

Chapter 28: Nicky

Chapter Notes

CW: underage drinking, homophobic language, biphobic language, physical violence

“How’s that look?”

Nicky squinted at the phone screen. Tori was on facetime, and Nicky was supposed to be helping her decorate her room in the new apartment in San Diego. Their room, Tori liked to say, because when Nicky visited they would share it. Rio had bought a set of bunk beds so that Nicky would have a bed of her own whenever she visited. And Tori was making sure to incorporate some of Nicky’s favorite colors and things as she decorated. Like the turquoise and leopard print bedspread she had just put on the second bunkbed.

“Looks great,” Nicky replied. Tori hopped down off the top bunk and Nicky could see her own comforter, a purple floral quilt that looked like something Agatha would’ve chosen.

“How’s Draco doing?” Tori asked.

Nicky looked across the room at the bearded dragon. He stared back at her menacingly through the glass.

“He still hates me,” Nicky told her. Rather than subject the dragon to another cross-country drive, the girls had decided that Draco would stay with Nicky. The bearded dragon had seemed friendly enough while Tori was there, but as soon as she had left, he had decided that Nicky was his mortal enemy. Every time she tried to handle him he either tried to bite her or tried to run away and hide somewhere she couldn’t get to him. He was still eating well, because that creature would never turn down a meal, but he made sure Nicky knew that he was displeased with her service every time she fed him.

“He doesn’t hate you. He just misses me,” Tori assured her. Tori sat on her bed, holding the phone in one hand. “How’s Ollie?”

“He’s fine. He definitely misses you,” Nicky replied. “He said something about clicker training his chickens?”

“Oh, yeah, we talked about that last week when we facetimed,” Tori said. “I’ll call him again tomorrow.”

“Have you talked to Monica?” Nicky asked.

“I talked to her last night, actually,” Tori beamed. “She agreed to be my girlfriend.”

Nicky’s brows crinkled in confusion. “I thought she was already your girlfriend?”

“I mean, we were dating, and we kissed a couple of times, but we hadn’t really made it official…”

“I’m sorry, you kissed her?” Nicky exclaimed, sitting up in bed. “When did that happen?”

“Um… Before I left Westview.”

“Yes, obviously,” Nicky scoffed. “But when exactly? And why didn’t you tell me?”

“In my defense, we had a lot going on,” Tori said. “Both literally and emotionally.”

Nicky collapsed back onto her pillows again. “Yeah, fair point, you’re right. So… How was it?”

“It was nice.”

“Just nice? Not amazing, incredible, mind-blowing, breathtaking, phenomenal—”

“Okay, you walking thesaurus, calm down,” Tori chuckled. “It was… All those things, all at once. Like… Like fireworks going off and crowds cheering and then just… quiet. Like she and I were the only people in the world.”

“That sounds amazing,” Nicky said, a soft smile on her face.

“It was,” Tori admitted. “I know this sounds really stupid but… I’m really hoping I can make it work with her. She’s pretty freaking amazing.”

“Well, she’d have to be to deserve my sister,” Nicky said.

Tori was quiet for a minute before changing the subject again. “How’s Mama?”

Nicky shrugged. “I dunno. I haven’t been talking to her.”

Two weeks had passed since Tori and Rio went back to San Diego, and for those two weeks Nicky had made a pointed effort to ignore her mother whenever possible. She could count on one hand the number of times they had spoken more than one sentence to one another.

“You can’t be mad at her forever,” Tori said.

“Yes I can,” Nicky replied. “I’ll be mad at her for as long as I want to. And I’m mad at Mami, too.”

Tori could be the nice sister, the empathetic daughter, the reasonable one. Nicky was gonna be the one who held a fucking grudge.

Nicky’s phone buzzed with a notification. It was a group chat she had once been part of with Morgan and some others. She clicked the notification, and an invitation to a party popped up on her screen.

“Hey, where’d you go?” Tori asked.

Nicky clicked off of the invitation. “Sorry, I paused you. I got an invite to a party this Saturday.”

“Oh, cool. Are you gonna go?” Tori asked.

Nicky shrugged. “I don’t know. I’m not really in the mood. Plus, Mama would never allow it.”

“Nicky, you can’t just stay home and sulk all the time,” Tori told her. Then she grinned mischievously. “Besides, what Mama doesn’t know won’t hurt her.”

“Tori Vidal! And here I thought you were supposed to be the good sister!” Nicky exclaimed.

“I can be sneaky,” Tori insisted. “Plus, I’m tired of you being sad all the time. You need to go out and do something fun for a change.”

Nicky opened up the invitation again, keeping Tori’s facetime call open in the corner of her screen. She checked the invite list, and both Maximoff twins were on it, as were Cassie, Kate, and Yelena, and some of her other friends. Morgan was also on the list, but so were most of the people in their class at Westview High.

“I mean… I guess I could check it out. For a little while.”

“There’s the spirit!” Tori cheered. “Just remember, don’t do anything that I wouldn’t do.”

Friday night came, and Nicky found herself driving down a dirt road into a field in the middle of nowhere. She’d told Agatha that she was going over to hang out with Kate, and Agatha hadn’t asked many questions— apparently auntie Jen was coming over for a wine night. Plus, it wasn’t entirely a lie. Kate was going to be at the party. If Agatha had assumed that they were going to be at Kate’s house, then that was on her.

The party was in full swing by the time Nicky arrived and she parked her truck in a row of other vehicles along the edge of the gravel drive. An old, rundown barn sat back in the distance, and a giant bonfire lit up the evening sky. Some people were carrying around sparklers, and there was a group of football players with boxes of cheap fireworks arguing about the best time to set them off. Some people were roasting hotdogs and marshmallows at the bonfire, and there were coolers with every variety of beverage imaginable. Someone had set up speakers that were blasting music loud enough to wake the dead. The entire field smelled like cheap booze, cigarettes, weed, and shitty vape flavors.

Nicky made her way through the crowd, looking around for her friends. She had texted them when she arrived, but no one had messaged her back yet. She had barely been out of her truck for ten minutes and was already sweating bullets, so she went to the coolers to grab something to drink. One was full of beer, the next full of hard seltzers and ciders. She wrinkled her nose; surely someone had brought some water, or at least some soda.

“Here, have some of this.”

Nicky looked up to see Morgan standing in front of her, two red solo cups in her hands. She held one out to Nicky, and she peered down at the pinkish liquid inside.

“What is it?” Nicky asked.

“Lemonade punch,” Morgan said, rattling the cup. “It’s good, try it.”

Nicky took the cup and took a sip. The drink was ridiculously sweet, with just a hint of lemon that made her face pinch and her tongue tingle. But it was blessedly cold, so she took another sip.

“It is good. Thanks,” she said.

Morgan smiled. “I was hoping you’d be here. I heard Tori and her mom moved back to California.”

“Yeah, they did,” Nicky replied.

“That sucks. I’m really sorry it didn’t work out,” Morgan told her, and Nicky was surprised that she actually sounded genuine.

“Yeah, me too.”

“I’m glad you’re here, though,” Morgan said. “I’ve missed hanging out with you.”

Nicky couldn’t necessarily say the same, but she wanted to give Morgan the benefit of the doubt. Maybe Nicky telling her off and losing her friend group for a while had made her realize that she needed to change some things.

“Come on, drink that, and let’s dance,” Morgan said, tapping her cup against Nicky’s and gesturing to the group of people who were gathered between some string lights and starting to dance. Nicky quickly drank about half the cup, her face wrinkling at the sweet and tart taste.

“Hey, what did you say was in this?” she asked.

Morgan shrugged. “Some kind of punch. Beth said she put strawberry lemonade and fruit punch and pineapple juice and Sprite and tequila…”

Nicky nearly spat out the drink in her mouth. “Tequila!?”

“Yeah,” Morgan said with a frown. “It’s a party. Everybody’s drinking. Just chill.”

Nicky stared at the drink in her cup, then around at everyone else. Morgan was right, everyone else also had a red cup or a can or a bottle of some sort of alcohol. Plus, she was there to have a good time, and she wanted to forget about everything else. What could it hurt?

She downed the rest of her punch, tossed her empty cup into a nearby trash can, and followed Morgan toward the dancers.

For the next hour, Nicky danced with Morgan and some of their other classmates. Every few songs, they would take a break, down a cup of punch, and go back to dancing. With every dance and every drink, Nicky could feel herself loosening up. The night felt hazy, the music sounded more intense but somehow muddled.

“C’mon, let’s take a break,” Morgan said, leading her away from the dancers and the drink coolers. They each had a fresh cup of punch in their hands.

They walked around the edge of the field, close to the tree line. Morgan sighed, seeming relieved.

“I’m glad we can still be friends,” she said with a giggle. “We can just put that whole thing with Tommy behind us.”

“What thing with Tommy?” Nicky asked, genuinely confused.

“The thing where you liked him,” Morgan replied.

“I thought you were over him by now,” Nicky said.

“Oh, yeah, definitely,” Morgan said, her tone colored with disgust. “I would never date a guy like that. Ugh. I can’t believe I ever dated him in the first place. It’s so gross.”

Nicky wasn’t following. She thought it might be the alcohol, but she was pretty sure that Morgan wasn’t making any sense. “What?”

“Well, you know why we broke up, right?”

“I thought it was because you were jealous of him and Kate?” Which wasn’t even a thing,” Nicky thought, but didn’t say out loud.

“Oh, no, turns out I had nothing to be worried about there,” Morgan said.

Nicky chuckled. “I could’ve told you that.”

“Oh, so you know?” Morgan said, a sly smile on her face.

“That Kate’s gay? Yeah, she and Yelena are totally together. Everybody knows that,” Nicky replied, sipping her drink.

“Not just Kate,” Morgan said. “Tommy’s gay, too.”

This time, Nicky really did spit out her drink. “Do what?”

“Tommy’s gay,” Morgan said. “That’s why we broke up.”

Nicky wiped her face. “Morgan, just because a guy doesn’t want you doesn’t automatically make them gay.”

“That’s not even what I mean,” Morgan scoffed. “He told me that he’s into guys. He said that if I was gonna be jealous of him hanging out with girls, then I should be jealous of guys, too, because he’s into them, too.”

“So he’s bi?” Nicky asked.

“That’s not a thing,” Morgan scoffed. “I think he’s just into guys and dating girls as a cover. Well, I’m not gonna be some beard, or whatever they call it. Eventually he’ll decide he’s just completely gay.”

“Morgan, that’s not… That’s not how that works,” Nicky argued. “People don’t decide to be gay or straight or bi. They’re just born that way. And Tommy telling you was probably a big trust thing for him, and you just… blew it. That’s so rude.”

“Look, I know your moms are… whatever,” Morgan scoffed. “But I thought you of all people would get it.”

“Yeah, I don’t think I’m ever gonna get you,” Nicky said, gesturing in Morgan’s general direction. “And I don’t think this friendship is gonna work out if you think that way.”

“Are you serious?” Morgan demanded. “I thought with that freak gone—”

“Watch your fucking mouth!” Nicky shouted. She glanced around and realized that people were looking at them, and some were even starting to walk toward them.

“I thought you’d be normal,” Morgan continued. “But I guess you’re a stupid dyke, too, just like your sis—”

Morgan didn’t finish her sentence, because Nicky threw her drink in her face. The sticky liquid covered her face, dripped from her hair, and splattered all over the t-shirt she was wearing.

“You fucking bitch!” Morgan spat, tossing her own drink aside before launching herself at Nicky.

Growing up, Nicky had learned many things from the cowboys who worked on her family’s ranch. Many of them had checkered pasts, some had criminal records. All of them, though, had taken a protective role over the kids. She had learned how to rope a steer, how to tie a quick release knot, how to win at poker, how to cheat at poker, and, most importantly, how to throw a punch. It was that last one that permanently ended her friendship with Morgan Stark.

Nicky’s first punch hit somewhere in the vicinity of Morgan’s cheekbone. It shocked Morgan for a moment, then she fought back, swinging wildly at Nicky’s face. Had it been an actual punch, Nicky might’ve blocked it, but the wide swing caught her just outside the eye. Nicky’s second punch, delivered with her left hand, hit Morgan’s jaw. When Morgan came back up, her eyes were watering, and her mouth was filled with blood.

“I’m gonna fucking kill you!” Morgan spat, her speech slurred slightly.

Nicky pulled back for another hit, but it never made contact. Instead a pair of arms wrapped around her from behind and pulled her away, as another boy grabbed Morgan and pulled her in the opposite direction.

“Let me go!” Nicky shouted, wiggling against the lean but strong arms pulling her away.

“Nope, not gonna happen, slugger,” Tommy said, practically putting her over his shoulder to carry her. Suddenly the ground started swaying beneath her, and Nicky thought being carried didn’t sound like such a bad idea after all.

Tommy sat her down on a picnic table bench on the opposite side of the barn. Suddenly Billy was there along with Teddy, who immediately handed her a bottle of water. Kate and Yelena crowded around her, and Yelena carefully prodded at her face.

“Ouch, what the fuck?” Nicky said, pushing Yelena away.

“Oh, yeah, that’s gonna be a shiner,” Yelena declared proudly. “But you definitely won the fight.”

“I would’ve knocked her out if someone hadn’t stopped me,” Nicky grumbled, glaring at Tommy, who threw his hands up in surrender.

“Honestly, I enjoyed that,” Cassie admitted, coming forward with a ziploc baggy full of ice. “Morgan had it coming.”

“I heard she might be moving at the end of the summer,” Kate said. “Apparently her parents are getting a divorce and her mom’s moving back to California.”

“Maybe she’ll move to San Diego and run into Tori, and Tori can beat her up, too,” Billy suggested.

“Tori would never!” Cassie exclaimed.

“Yeah, I’m the violent sister, remember?” Nicky reminded them.

“You’re only violent when the other person deserves it,” Tommy said. “And Morgan deserved it. She shouldn’t have said that about your sister.”

“Or what she said about you,” Nicky said. Tommy’s cheeks turned red and everyone else looked embarrassed as well, not making eye contact with her or Tommy. Even tipsy, Nicky realized that something wasn’t right. She didn’t know if what Morgan had said was true or not, but if it was, Tommy definitely hadn’t been out to their friends yet.

“Drink,” Billy said, pointing to the bottle of water still in her hand.

“Yes, mom,” Nicky teased, opening the bottle and downing half of it in one go.

Nicky stayed at the picnic table, holding the ice pack with one hand, sipping water with the other. Her friends came and went, checking on her and hanging out. She had almost finished her bottle of water when another bottle was dangled in her face.

“Here you go, slugger,” Tommy said, handing her the bottle. He took a seat beside her, and it took Nicky a moment to realize that they were alone.

“I’m sorry—”

“I just wanted to—”

They both stopped mid-sentence, realizing they were talking over one another. Tommy’s cheeks turned pink all the way up to his ears, and Nicky bit her lip.

“I uh, I just wanted to thank you,” Tommy said. “I know you were mostly defending Tori, but you spoke up for me, too, and I just—”

“Is what Morgan said true?” Nicky asked. Her eyes widened when she realized what she had said. “Fuck, nevermind. You don’t have to tell me. I’m sorry. I don’t know why I— It just slipped out.”

“Probably because of the punch you were drinking,” Tommy muttered. “You’re a nice person, Nicky. People don’t realize it, but you are. Most people wouldn’t give Morgan a second chance, but I know you were trying to. She’s just… not a good person.”

“That’s putting it nicely.”

“Yeah, well, there are a lot of names I want to call her, but as a guy, it seems misogynistic,” he said.

“She’s a giant bitch and a flaming cunt,” Nicky supplied for him. “You’re allowed to agree if I say it.”

“Then yes, I agree,” he nodded. He smirked at her, and it made her stomach flutter. Nicky sipped her water, content to the subject drop. But then he spoke again. “She was telling the truth, though. Well, kind of. I, uh… I’m not gay. I like girls, sometimes. But sometimes I like guys, too.”

“So you’re bi? Or pan?” Nicky asked.

“I’m not sure what the difference is, but I like the flag with the purple more than the flag with the yellow,” Tommy replied.

“Fair enough,” Nicky nodded. “Are there any guys you like right now?”

“I used to have a crush on David, from the soccer team, but he’s turned into an asshole,” Tommy told her. “And I was talking to this guy from Eastview for a bit, after I broke up with Morgan, but he started dating someone at his school.”

“Cool,” Nicky said. “Any girls you like?”

Tommy looked at her incredulously. “You’re okay with it?”

“With what?”

“With me liking girls and guys? I mean, you heard what Morgan said.”

“We’ve already established that Morgan’s a bitch,” Nicky reminded him.

“Yeah, but… I dunno. A lot of people seem to be thrown off by the idea of someone liking both genders,” Tommy said.

Nicky shrugged. “I mean… to me, I feel like it just means that you like the person and don’t just care about what’s in their pants. Right?”

“Yeah,” Tommy nodded.

“Then it makes perfect sense,” Nicky shrugged.

“And would you ever date a guy like that? A bi guy?” Tommy asked.

The weight of the question wasn’t lost on Nicky. She swallowed hard, suddenly feeling like her mouth was full of cotton. “Yeah. I totally would.”

Tommy’s eyes flickered down, to her lips, then back up to her eyes. “I, uh… Yeah, I really like this one girl.”

“That’s nice,” Nicky said.

“She’s a little scary, though,” Tommy admitted. “She’s got a mean left hook. Absolutely brutal.”

Tommy was going to kiss her. Nicky had never been kissed before, but somehow she knew it was going to happen. He reached up and moved the ice-pack from her face, tilted his head, and gently, ever so gently, kissed her lips. Tommy’s lips were slightly chapped, and she could feel a little bit of scratchy hair on his chin. He kissed her softly, careful of her growing bruise, and it made Nicky feel like something worthy of being treasured and adored.

When he pulled away, Nicky wanted to kiss him again. She wanted to kiss him again and again, but something stopped her. There was a pit in her stomach, a worry gnawing at her, and she couldn’t brush it aside.

“Was that okay?” Tommy asked.

“Yes,” Nicky said, and she could feel her cheeks flushing.

“I probably should’ve asked this first, but, um… Would you like to go out sometime? Like on a date? Like… we would be dating and you would be my girlfriend?” Tommy asked.

Nicky bit her lip. Part of her wanted to say yes. But there was still that gnawing anxiety in the pit of her stomach, stopping her.

“Nicky?” he said, his face etched with concern. She had taken too long to answer.

“I don’t… I mean…” Nicky sputtered.

Tommy’s face fell, then hardened. “Oh. I see, then.”

“No, Tommy, it’s not you,” she said. It’s not you, it’s me. Ugh, how pathetic.

“You said the bi thing didn’t matter,” Tommy said, his eyes watering.

“It doesn’t, I swear,” Nicky told him.

“Then do you just not like me or—”

“I do like you,” Nicky said.

Tommy stared at her, dumbfounded. “What is it, then?”

Nicky took a deep breath. “Do you know what you’re going to do when you graduate?”

Tommy’s look of confusion only grew. “What?”

“Just… go with me, for a second,” Nicky assured him. “Do you know what you’re going to do when you graduate?”

“I dunno, go to college, I guess,” Tommy answered.

“For what?” Nicky asked.

Tommy suddenly became more withdrawn. “I haven’t really thought about it, to be honest. I mean… I’m kind of the odd one out in my family.”

“What do you mean?”

“Well, dad’s super smart, like he’s so good with numbers it’s almost like he’s a robot,” Tommy explained. “And mom, well, she might just be the school secretary but what a lot of people don’t realize is that she practically keeps this town running. She knows every kid at school and their families, knows what they do, what they need. It’s kind of crazy. And Billy, well, he’s super creative, you know? He imagines whole worlds with his writing and drawing. And I’m just… Tommy. I get average grades. I try to be the funny one and the athletic one. But I don’t know what that means for me after I get out of school, you know?”

“Yeah, that makes sense,” Nicky said. “But what do you want to do?”

“I dunno. I know that I want to help people,” Tommy said. “Mom’s always talking about kids who are abused, kids who don’t have enough food. I’d like to help them somehow, but I don’t know what that means.”

Nicky nodded. “I think that’s a good place to start.”

Tommy looked at her with confusion. “But why are you asking me this? What does it have to do with anything?”

Nicky took a deep breath. “I know what I want, Tommy. I know that next year I’m going to apply for pre-veterinary programs. I’m gonna go to college for pre-vet and that’s gonna take four years. Then I’m going to apply to veterinary school, and that will take another four years of school and then two years of clinicals before I become a vet. And then… I don’t know. I don’t know if I’ll come back to Westview to work, or if I’ll travel. But… I don’t want to be tied down.”

“You think I would tie you down?” Tommy asked.

“I don’t know,” Nicky sighed sadly. “All I know is, I’ve watched my moms for the last few months. They were together when they were our age, but they broke up because their futures were going in two different directions. And it broke them. Even now, all these years later, they can’t get over it. I just don’t want to do that to myself. Can you understand that?”

“Honestly… No,” Tommy replied, shaking his head. Nicky opened her mouth to try to explain more, but he placed his hand on hers, and that stopped her. “But I can respect it.”

Nicky’s eyes widened. “Really?”

“Yeah,” he said. “I don’t like it, if I’m being honest. I really like you and I would love to prove that we’re not like your moms. But I respect your decision.”

Nicky blinked back tears. “Dammit, Tommy Maximoff. Why do you have to be so nice?”

He smirked. “I can be the nice one. You can be the one who throws the punches.”

Nicky shook her head, but the movement made the world sway in her vision. She grabbed Tommy’s arm for support as it felt like the world started moving under her feet.

“Oh shit,” she mumbled, her stomach churning with more than just nerves.

“Yeah, I think it’s time we take you home,” Tommy said, rubbing her back. “Billy!”

Tori had two thoughts, as Tommy drove her home in her truck, Billy following behind in his Subaru: 1) Don’t throw up, and 2) Mama’s gonna kill me.

By the time they pulled into the drive to the ranch, the world had almost stopped spinning— almost. But as Tommy walked Nicky up to the porch, her legs felt more and more like jelly, and her stomach started lurching yet again.

“You okay there, slugger?” Tommy asked nervously. “You’re looking a little green.”

“I’m oh— Oh, no,” Nicky said, turning sideways and leaning over the porch railing.

That was how her mother found her, puking into her azaleas, with Tommy rubbing her back and Billy making gagging noises from the window of his car.

“What the hell?” Agatha asked.

“Aw, fuck,” Nicky muttered.

“Hi, Ms. Harkness,” Tommy greeted. “Tommy Maximoff. I was just making sure Nicky got home safely.”

“Thank you,” Agatha said. “I’ll take it from here.”

Nicky retched again, and the liquor burned far more coming back up than it had going down.

“Bye, Nicky, hope you feel better!” Billy called from the car.

“Good luck,” Tommy said, giving her a wry smile before going to the car.

Nicky gave them both a mock-salute as they drove away. Then she leaned back over the railing with a groan.

“Nope, up you get,” Agatha ordered, peeling her off the railing. “Jesus Christ, you smell like cheap tequila.”

“Ooo, someone’s been partying.”

Crap, she had forgotten that Auntie Jen was visiting. Nicky raised her head to glare at the woman who was leaning against the living room doorframe.

“What happened to your eye?” Jen asked. Dammit, Nicky forgot she was supposed to be keeping her head down.

Agatha yanked Nicky around so fast that the world started spinning again. She pushed Nicky’s hair out of her face roughly and inspected her bruise closely.

“You should see the other guy,” Nicky slurred, smirking in spite of the trouble she was in.

“I thought you were going to Kate’s?” she asked.

“I said I was gonna go hang out with Kate, I didn’t say we would be at her house,” Nicky replied.

Agatha looked at her furiously. “Nicole Elizabeth Harkness.”

“Damn, using her full name,” Jen muttered.

“You know good and well that lying by omission is still lying,” Agatha chastised.

“So? You did it to Mami for seventeen years,” Nicky grumbled.

“What?” Agatha exclaimed. “You cannot seriously be trying to use that to justify your own lying.”

“I’m just saying it’s a little hypocritical,” Nicky argued, turning toward the stairs. “And maybe if you hadn’t lied to her, she’d still be here.”

“Don’t try to turn this around on me,” Agatha said. “You’re still in deep trouble, young lady.”

“Oh, fuck you,” Nicky shouted, her head splitting in pain.

“What did you say to me?” Agatha exclaimed.

“I said fu—” Nicky’s words were cut off by more vomit. Instead of the stairs she hurried to the nearest bathroom and launched herself at the toilet, barely making it before puking. Even when she thought it should be impossible for her stomach to have anything left in it, she kept retching, dry heaving until the muscles in her stomach and ribs ached.

“Yep, you’re definitely Rio’s daughter,” Agatha said, rubbing soothing circles on her back. “Tequila was always lethal to her, too. And rum puts her to sleep. Vodka and whiskey, my child. Vodka and whiskey.”

Nicky sat up with a groan and then slumped against the wall. “I don’t ever want to drink alcohol ever again.”

“Even better,” Agatha nodded.

“Knock knock,” Jen called, stepping into the small space. “I come bearing ginger ale and saltines.”

“Noooo,” Nicky groaned.

“Yes,” Agatha argued, taking the drink from Jen and holding it up to Nicky’s lips. “It’ll help, I promise.”

“I’m gonna leave you to it,” Jen said. “Just remember, it’s seventeen years too late for an abortion. Love you, Nicky, feel better.”

“Bye,” Nicky called. Agatha sat beside her against the bathroom wall and passed her a saltine, which NIcky nibbled on. “I’m sorry, Mama.”

“Good, you should be,” Agatha replied. Then she sighed. “At least you got home safe. That Maximoff boy seems like a winner.”

Nicky groaned. “I don’t wanna talk about that.”

“Okay,” Agatha said. The silence stretched between them.

“He kissed me,” Nicky said.

“I take back what I said about him being a winner.”

“No, he is,” Nicky sighed. “He asked me to be his girlfriend.”

“And?”

“I said no.”

Agatha looked at her with confusion. “I thought you liked him?”

“I do,” Nicky replied. “But I don’t want to start something and have feelings for him and then have to break it off. I don’t want to end up like you and Mami.”

Agatha’s face twisted with regret. “Oh, sweetheart, don’t let what happened between me and your Mami influence your life choices. We are horrible role models.”

“You loved her. You still love her,” Nicky said. “And she loves you. But you were right. Sometimes it’s just not enough. And I don’t want to do that to myself.”

“Nicky, you can’t go through your life afraid to love someone just because you’re afraid it might not work out in the end,” Agatha told her. “As much as it hurts to lose your Mami, I don’t regret loving her. Hell, I got you and Tori out of the deal. Loving Rio, and being loved by her in return, has been one of the greatest experiences of this lifetime.”

“Then why won’t you fight for it?” Nicky asked. “Go after her!”

“And say what, Nicky?” Agatha asked.

“I don’t know! Ask her to come back!” Nicky could hear herself shouting like it was another person speaking, but she couldn’t control her own volume, even though it made her head ache.

“I already tried that,” Agatha huffed.

Nicky let out a huff and tried to stand up. “You’re so fucking stupid.”

“Oh, I’m stupid.” Agatha’s voice dripped with sarcasm as she stood up, her arms out to try and keep Nicky steady in spite of the argument they were in. “What do you want me to do, Nicky? Chase after her? Go with her? Sell this place and drag you and Ollie with me to California, away from everything and everyone you’ve ever known? Do you realize what all that entails, Nicky? Do you want to say goodbye to all your friends? Sell Scratchy and the cows and everything just to chase after them?”

Nicky felt tears welling up in her eyes. Dammit, she didn’t want to cry. She fucking hated crying.

“I just want them back.”

The anger in Agatha’s body instantly melted, and she pulled Nicky into a hug that was all softness and warmth. Nicky went willingly, hanging onto her mother for support and letting her tears stream into the shoulder of her shirt.

“I know, sweetheart. I want them back, too.”

Nicky sighed. She didn’t know how to fix this.

“I’m tired.”

Agatha gave her one last squeeze before releasing her from her hug, although she placed one of Nicky’s hands on her arm for balance. “Okay, then, let’s get you to bed. Easy, now, baby steps, come on.”

“Am I in trouble?” Nicky asked as Agatha helped her up the stairs.

“Oh, most definitely,” Agatha replied. “You’re grounded until you’re twenty-seven.”

“I don’t think you can do that,” Nicky argued as she went to her own bathroom to brush her teeth.

“You’d be surprised what I can do,” Agatha replied, pulling out a fresh pair of pajamas. Once Nicky had brushed her teeth, Agatha helped her change and tucked her into bed like she was five. “I’ll make sure there’s Advil and Gatorade for you in the morning. You’re gonna need it kiddo.”

“Hmm, mother of the year,” Nicky declared.

Agatha gave her a wry smile then kissed her forehead. “Goodnight, Nicky. Love you.”

Nicky rolled over and buried her face into the pillow before muttering, "Love you, too."

Chapter 29: Agatha

Chapter Notes

Once again, a reminder that I have no idea how inheritance law works and I just need you guys to suspend your disbelief for the sake of PLOT okay?

Agatha sat on the porch, sipping her coffee, staring out over the fields and the river and the mountain, just like she had done every morning since she was seventeen years old. For many of those years she spent the mornings beside her grandfather. Some mornings they would discuss their plans for the day, what chores needed to be done, accounts that needed to be checked. Other mornings he would tell her stories about his childhood, about her family, things his father and grandfather had passed down to him. Still, other mornings, they would just sit in silence, sipping coffee, each lost in their own thoughts. The silence had made Agatha uncomfortable at first, like she had done something wrong. But eventually she had realized that it was possible for two people to simply exist in the same space, that silence could be comfortable.

After her grandfather died, Agatha had made peace with her mornings alone. Neither Ollie nor Nicky were early birds, and she didn’t see that changing for a very long time, if ever. But for the last few weeks that Rio was there, they had spent their mornings together. Some mornings were spent talking about their days, others were spent talking about the girls, but many were spent in silence, simply sitting beside one another. The silence was never awkward, never heavy. It simply was, and they simply were, two humans existing in the same space, breathing the same air, staring at the same mountains.

“You up for some company this morning?”

Agatha looked up from her coffee to find Lilia standing at the bottom of the porch steps. She had been so lost in her thoughts that she hadn’t even noticed the other woman coming up from the barn. She nodded and Lilia joined her, sitting in one of the nearby chairs.

“There’s coffee in the kitchen if you want some,” Agatha offered.

“I’ve already had my morning cup. Doctor says I have to limit my caffeine intake,” Lilia told her.

Agatha raised a brow. “You limiting your caffeine intake?”

“Caffeine I can do without. It’s the whiskey I would miss,” Lilia replied with a crooked grin. Agatha just smirked and shook her head at the older woman. Lilia watched her for a moment before changing the subject. “What’s happening with your mother’s lawsuit?”

“We have a hearing on Wednesday, if it doesn’t get stalled again,” Agatha replied. “Jen is hopeful that if she can just get all of our evidence in front of a judge, the whole case will be thrown out.”

“And if it isn’t?”

Agatha didn’t have an answer for that question, so she didn’t give one, instead sipping her coffee in silence.

“What happens after the hearing?” Lilia asked.

“Business as usual,” Agatha shrugged.

“Hmm,” Lilia hummed. “And how much longer do you think business as usual will keep this place afloat?”

Agatha knew what Lilia meant. Cattle ranching was a shit business. Beef prices fluctuated, feed prices rose, inflation was a royal pain in the ass. Politicians sat in offices making decisions about things they knew nothing about, playing god with the lives of people and animals and the Earth as a whole while they breathed in manufactured air from a box. Meanwhile, Agatha sat here on her porch, trying to carefully balance legacy and profit.

“I don’t know,” she admitted grimly. “I don’t know, and that scares me.”

Lilia was one of the few people on the planet that Agatha would admit fear to, mainly because Lilia probably already knew. Lilia was an expert on reading people, which made her an excellent ranch manager. She ran the bunkhouse like a well-oiled machine, keeping men and women alike in line. She could also read Agatha, a fact that was as unsettling as it was helpful. It was a boon when Agatha needed something and Lilia could deliver without having to ask first. But when Lilia saw through her to the parts Agatha would rather stay hidden and forced them to the surface, it felt more like a curse.

“At least you’re honest about it,” Lilia sighed.

“I don’t remember my grandpa ever being scared,” Agatha grumbled.

“Oh, he was plenty scared. Just wasn’t big enough to admit it,” Lilia scoffed. “He had the same fears that you have. Scared of making the wrong choices. Scared of losing this place. Scared of letting people down. Those kinds of fears are too big for one person to carry alone, but for most of his life, your grandfather had people to share the load. He had your grandmother, and then he had your uncle Elijah, and then he had you, whether he wanted you or not. He passed all those fears down to you, and now here you sit, trying to carry them all on your own with no one to help you.”

“Someday I’ll have Nicky,” Agatha replied, thinking of the hungover girl upstairs in her bedroom.

“Agatha, be honest with yourself,” Lilia said, her voice serious. “Nicky doesn’t want this. She just doesn’t know how to give it up yet.”

“Maybe Ollie—”

“Honey,” Lilia interrupted, raising a brow.

Agatha sighed. Even without his special needs, Agatha didn’t see Ollie ever taking over this place. The only one of her children she thought might actually be interested was Tori and, well, Tori was gone.

“Fuck,” Agatha huffed, covering her face with her hands. “What am I supposed to do, Lilia? Running this ranch has been my goal for over twenty years. It’s what I’ve always wanted. I don’t… I don’t know how to give that up.”

Lilia leaned back in her chair. “First you have to decide if there’s something you want more.”

Agatha wiped the tears that had come unbidden from her face. “I think we both know that there is.”

Lilia nodded. “Then you’ll figure the rest out.”

Agatha didn’t feel so certain of that. As the next few days passed, rather than becoming more assured, her uncertainty just grew. Nicky was distant, barely speaking a few words to her in passing each day. Ollie had become withdrawn since Rio and Tori left, refusing any kind of physical affection, preferring to use gestures and a communication app on his tablet to interact rather than actually talking. For Agatha, any distance from her children felt like a special kind of torture.

On Monday afternoon, her phone lit up with a familiar contact. A broad smile spread across her face as she answered the call and raised the phone to her ear.

“Tori, sweetheart, hi. How are you?”

“Hi, Mama,” Tori replied, and Agatha could hear her let out a long sigh. “I’m okay, I guess. How’s, um… How’s things?”

“Good,” Agatha said. “Everything’s good.”

“That’s, um… good,” Tori said.

God, this is awkward, Agatha thought, and yet she didn’t know how to fix it.

“How’s Luna? And Lady?” Tori asked.

“They’re doing just fine,” Agatha assured her. “In fact, I think Nicky did some training with Luna today. She’s trying to keep her in good shape for whenever you…” Whenever you visit. Agatha let her voice trail off. She couldn’t finish the sentence because she had no idea when that might be. The realization that she didn’t know when she’d see her daughter in person again, when she would get to hug her and see her and actually interact with her, made her chest ache. “I miss you.”

She hadn’t meant to say it. In all of their talks in the few weeks since Tori had moved back to California with Rio, she had managed not to say it. She thought it, sure, she thought it every single day, multiple times a day, not just when they were on the phone together. But she had tried not to say it out loud. They missed each other, and they both knew it, but saying it out loud wouldn’t make things any better. In fact, Agatha feared it might make things worse.

Based on the sniffles she heard on the other end of the line, Agatha thought it might’ve already made things worse.

“I miss you, too,” Tori mumbled between quiet sobs. She was clearly trying to keep Agatha from hearing her cry, but failing. It broke Agatha’s heart, but she wasn’t sure if it would be better to say something to comfort her, or to just ignore it, and pretend that Tori’s attempts to keep quiet were working.

“How’s your Mami?” Agatha smacked herself in the face. That was not what she meant to say. Asking about Rio, talking about Rio at all, had been yet another unspoken rule between them, and here Agatha was breaking it as well. If she could shove her foot in her mouth, Agatha would surely choke on it.

“I don’t know. Fine,” Tori replied. “She mostly just goes to work, comes home, goes to work, comes home. She says that once Hela fully hands everything off to her it’ll be better, but I don’t know.”

“And does she… I mean… Does she seem happy? With her job, I mean.” Agatha silently cursed herself once the question was out of her mouth.

“She definitely doesn’t seem very happy,” Tori answered. “I don’t think moving back here is the dream come true that she thought it would be.”

Agatha hated that Tori’s words gave Agatha a feeling of vindication. She had wanted Rio to be happy, had thought that her leaving and going back to San Diego would be the thing that made her happiest. But at the same time, the petty side of her was happy that Rio seemed to be as miserable as she was.

“I could be wrong, though,” Tori said, pulling Agatha’s attention back to their conversation. “Mami and I haven’t really been talking much.”

“You’re not giving her the silent treatment, are you?” Agatha asked.

“No,” Tori replied quickly. “I mean, I’m not trying that hard to engage in conversation either, but… Mami doesn’t say a lot. She just seems to be existing. Sometimes I feel like… well…”

“Like what?” Agatha pressed.

“I feel like I miss her, too,” Tori said, her voice barely above a whisper. “Is that weird? I mean, this apartment’s barely a thousand square feet. We’re so close to one another. But I just feel like she’s so far away now.”

“I’m sorry, sweetheart,” Agatha said, her heart aching. “I don’t… I wish I knew how to fix it.”

“Yeah, me too,” Tori hiccupped.

The silence stretched, like it could fill the thousands of miles that lay between them. Agatha fidgeted restlessly, the weight of Tori’s absence bearing down on her like a physical thing.

“You know my door is always open,” she said finally. “I can book a flight for you whenever you want. Just say the word.”

“I know, Mama,” Tori said sadly. What she left unspoken felt just as loud. She wouldn’t ask, not yet. She wouldn’t come back for a visit, at least not for a little while. Tori had made her choice, just like the rest of them. There was a shuffling sound on the other end of the line, and then Tori spoke again. “I should go. Mami will be home soon. Or, well, she should be.”

“Okay,” Agatha said, the word catching in her throat like barbed wire. “I love you.”

“Love you, too, Mama,” Tori replied. “Talk to you later.”

“Okay,” Agatha replied. The line cut off with two beeps. She stared at the phone in her hand.

It wasn’t goodbye. It was just talk to you later. But it cut just the same.

Agatha strode into the courthouse wearing her sharpest suit, black blazer and slacks paired with a dark purple blouse. Her hair was pulled up into a neat twist, and she looked every bit the put-together professional.

Inside, her emotions were in turmoil.

Beside her, Jen carried a suitcase full of documentation. Their heels clicked in tandem against the tile floor as they took their place on one side of the courtroom. Agatha didn’t even spare her mother a glance on the opposite side of the aisle. The judge took his place at the front of the room, and they all took their seats for the proceedings to begin.

Her mother’s attorney laid out their farce of a case. Their only evidence to back up that Agatha’s grandfather hadn’t been of sound mind when he drew up his will was one police report for a DUI shortly after the death of Agatha’s uncle, and a few eye-witness accounts from locals that Jim had seemed depressed after losing both his sons. Agatha didn’t argue that point, but grieving his children was a far cry from being senile.

When it was Jen’s turn, she put into evidence statements from Jim Harkness’s doctors stating that he had been of sound mind all the way up until the moment of his death. She also had witness reports from his friends and employees, the people who interacted with him every day. Finally, she called an in-person witness, something that surprised Agatha.

“I have the attorney who drew up James Harkness’s will here to make a statement,” Jen told the judge. “Matt Murdoch.”

The attorney was an old man, almost as old as Agatha’s grandfather. They had been old friends, since before Murdoch was blinded by a chemical spill on his family’s farm when he was just a child. The old man made his way to the stand, feeling around for his seat, and sat down.

“Mr. Murdoch, do you remember drawing up end of life documents for Mr. James Harkness?” Jen asked.

“Of course,” Murdoch replied.

“And you were the same attorney who drew up his original will, correct?”

“Yes,” Murdoch nodded.

“Why was Mr. Harkness changing his will?” Jen asked.

“Jim’s original will divided his estate between his three children,” Murdoch explained. “Unfortunately, both of Jim’s sons preceded him in death.”

“That would’ve made Mr. Harkness’s daughter, Evanora Harkness, his sole beneficiary, correct?” Jen clarified.

“Correct,” Murdoch said.

“And Mr. Harkness didn’t want his daughter to be his sole beneficiary?” Jen asked.

“No. He wanted to make sure his assets were divided between his daughter and granddaughter.”

“Why would he do that? If he left his estate to his daughter, wouldn’t it eventually pass through her to his granddaughter, Agatha Harkness?”

“Theoretically,” Murdoch replied. “In reality, Jim knew that Evanora had no interest in his family’s property. He feared that she would sell it with no consideration for Agatha. He wanted to ensure that his granddaughter inherited the ranch, not his daughter.”

“If that’s the case, then why didn’t he just leave the property to Agatha outright? Why even include Evanora Harkness in the will?” Jen asked.

“Well, it’s not… It’s not decent to say,” Murdoch said. That caught Agatha’s attention.

“Mr. Murdoch, please remember that you’re under oath, and that your answer may help ensure that your friend’s final wishes are met,” Jen coaxed. “Why did Mr. Harkness include his daughter Evanora in his will?”

“Because she blackmailed him,” Murdoch replied.

“What?!” Agatha exclaimed, at the same time her mother’s lawyer shouted, “Objection! Hearsay, your honor!”

“This entire case relies on expert witness testimony. The entire case is hearsay,” Jen scoffed.

“Overruled,” the judge declared. “Ms. Kale, Mr. Murdoch, please continue.”

“Can you elaborate on what you mean by blackmail, Mr. Murdoch?” Jen asked.

“Jim was far from a perfect man,” Murdoch said. “He had a series of affairs throughout his marriage. He was involved in a few legal issues through the years. His daughter knew about these things and threatened to make them public if she wasn’t equally included in the will.”

“Before this, was Evanora Harkness included in James Harkness’s will at all?” Jen asked.

“Prior to her threats, Jim was going to leave her a lump sum of money, but leave his granddaughter, Agatha, the ranch itself. The ranch is worth considerably more than the lump sum,” Murdoch explained.

“Thank you, Mr. Murdoch. That’s all I have for you,” Jen said.

Evanora’s attorney stood, fidgeting nervously. “Mr. Murdoch, did you witness any of these supposed threats?”

“Oh, yes,” Murdoch nodded. “Evanora came to my office with her father. She made no secret of what she was doing.”

“I see,” the attorney said, looking defeated all of a sudden. “No further questions.”

“What?” Evanora exclaimed. “You complete imbecile!”

“Council, please get your client under control,” the judge commanded. Once Mr. Murdoch was out of the witness chair, the judge turned to them. “I’m ready to make my ruling. In light of the overwhelming evidence, it is clear that James Harkness was of sound mind when he had his final will and testament drawn up. Therefore, Evanora Harkness’s case is thrown out. Furthermore, in light of today’s testimony, I am declaring Agatha Harkness as sole heir and beneficiary of James Harkness’s estate.”

Agatha’s jaw dropped. She knew that they would win the case, but she hadn’t expected that. Beside her, Jen was practically vibrating with excitement at their win. Before Agatha could fully process what had happened, Evanora elbowed her way across the courtroom and was in her face.

“You ungrateful little bitch,” she hissed. “He wanted me to abort you, you know. The grandfather you loved so much. When he found out that I was pregnant, he told me to go get an abortion, even offered to pay for it. I refused.”

“Oh, don’t pretend you’re some kind of saint, mother. We both know that you only refused because you planned on extorting my father once I was born,” Agatha scoffed, rolling her eyes. It wasn’t like her mother’s words surprised her; hell, her grandfather had played the same card when Rio ended up pregnant with Tori and when Agatha was pregnant with Nicky.

“I should’ve just gone through with it. It would’ve been better if you had died before you even left my body,” Evanora sneered. “You have daughters. Someday, you’ll understand.”

Evanora turned to leave, but Agatha grabbed her wrist with enough force to leave a bruise on her old skin, stopping her.

“I will never understand the hate you have for me,” Agatha told her. “Because from the moment I knew my children existed, I have loved them with every fiber of my being. They will never know the kind of pain that I have experienced, because they know that I love them more than anything in this world.” She released her grip on her mother’s wrist. “Goodbye, Mother.”

Evanora’s attorney herded her away, and Agatha left with Jen before the old woman could start anything else. As soon as they were out of the courthouse, Agatha rounded on her friend.

“Why didn’t you tell me about Murdoch?” Agatha demanded. She thought she deserved at least a heads up.

Jen threw her hands up in surrender. “I’m sorry I didn’t warn you. But I was afraid that if I told you ahead of time, if you knew that your mother had coerced your grandfather into including her in the will, you’d do something stupid.”

Agatha hated to admit that Jen had a point. She crossed her arms over her chest in irritation, but before she could say anything more, she saw a familiar face just down the sidewalk.

“Ugh, what’s that prick doing here?” Agatha asked, pointing toward the man.

Jen followed her gesture and frowned. “Tony Stark? No idea.”

The man’s head whipped around at the sound of his name, and Agatha wanted to strangle Jen. Before they could flee, he was walking toward them.

“Hello, neighbor,” he greeted cheerily. “I hear congratulations are in order.”

“I suppose,” Agatha replied through gritted teeth. “Stark, if this is yet another offer to buy the ranch, then I have to tell you, your timing is absolutely terrible.”

“It’s not,” he replied. “Actually, it’s the opposite.”

Agatha’s brow wrinkled in confusion. “What?”

Tony looked around. “There’s a park across the street. Walk with me?”

Agatha exchanged a look with Jen, who looked just as baffled as she felt. Agatha nodded, and she followed her neighbor to the small park across from the courthouse. They walked in silence for a few minutes, and Agatha grew more and more agitated with each second.

“Listen, Mr. Stark—”

“I’m moving back to California,” he told her. “My wife has agreed to try couples therapy in an effort to save our failing marriage. She wants to move back to our Malibu beach house, and I agreed.”

“Oh. Well, I’m… sorry?” Agatha offered.

“When I came here, I wanted to build something different, out from under the shadow of my father’s legacy,” Tony said. “Turns out, I just tried to build that same legacy but in a different zip code. And the thing about a legacy is that there’s not much of a point to it if there’s no one to pass it on to. If I stay here, I’ll lose my family. Without my wife and my daughter, there’s no point to any of it.”

Agatha felt her heart beginning to soften. “I agree, Tony.”

He looked up at her use of his first name. It was the first time Agatha had ever been so informal with him.

“I’ve withdrawn all of my applications for building permits. The vineyard and winery will be the only thing I’ve changed about the property.”

“Okay?” Agatha said, confused by this turn of events.

“I’d like to sell it,” he said. “And, if we can come to a deal, I’d like to sell it to you.”

Agatha’s eyes widened in surprise. “Oh.”

“I know, it’s a complete about face,” he acknowledged. “But I want the place to go to someone who will do something with it, and who will pass it on to the next generation. You’re that person, Agatha.”

Agatha worried her bottom lip as she considered his words. Not so long ago, she would’ve leapt at the offer. But things had changed so rapidly in the last few weeks, and now the decision wasn’t so simple.

“I’ll need some time to think about it,” she replied honestly.

Stark nodded. “I’ll send over an offer by the end of the week. You can consider it and let me know what you think.”

“Alright,” Agatha agreed. Stark offered his hand, and Agatha shook it. Then he turned to walk away. “Hey, Tony?”

He turned back to her.

“I hope it works out. With your family.”

He gave her a tense smile. “Thank you. I hope the same for you.”

Agatha stared at his back as he walked away. Buying Stark’s property would almost double the size of the ranch. It would be the biggest expansion in the ranch’s history. It was what five generations of her family had dreamed of. And yet, now that it was within Agatha’s reach, she wasn’t sure if she wanted it at all.

“We need to talk.”

Nicky looked up from where she stood in the kitchen, filling up two bottles with milk replacer powder and hot water. Agatha thought her two highland calves were overdue for weaning, but Nicky still gave them a bottle each every morning and evening. She looked down at her work, then back at Agatha, a confused expression on her face.

“I can help you,” Agatha volunteered. It was completely out of character, and the surprise on Nicky’s face said as much. Agatha had adamantly refused to help with the calves ever since she brought them home, so such an abrupt about face was probably throwing up all sorts of red flags in Nicky’s head.

They walked down to the barn in silence. It was late evening, and the sun was beginning to sink down below the mountain peaks. The weather had turned unseasonably cold, and for a moment Agatha wondered if it might snow. A snowstorm in the middle of summer wasn’t completely unheard of for this part of Montana, but it wasn’t exactly normal. Agatha thought the cold weather was oddly fitting. Why should it be warm when she felt like her heart had turned to ice?

Nicky had the calves in one of the smaller pastures near the barn, where they often kept heifers right before their first calving, so they could keep an eye on them. They came running as soon as Nicky opened the gate, and she giggled as they started nosing her. They were at least double the size they had been when Nicky first brought them home, healthy and well-groomed, friendly. All of those things were a testament to how well Nicky had cared for them. She hadn’t just given them grain and milk replacer, she had poured love into them, and that showed.

The black and white calf nudged Agatha’s arm with her nose, lips quivering as she sought out the bottle Agatha was carrying. Agatha quickly turned the bottle over, and the calf took hold of the nipple and began suckling. It was loud and messy, and the calf instinctively headbutted at Agatha’s arm the way it would at a cow’s udder, as if that would make more milk flow into its mouth.

“I named that one Ivy,” Nicky told her. “This one’s Betty.”

Agatha smirked. “You’re never escaping the Swiftie allegations.”

Nicky shrugged. She held the second bottle tight as the orange calf, Betty, suckled.

“You said you wanted to talk?” she said nervously. Agatha couldn’t blame her for her anxiety. Agatha had plenty of her own about how this conversation would go, but she had put it off for as long as she could bear.

“I need to ask you a question,” Agatha said. “Before I do, I want you to know that, whatever your answer is, I will still love you. There will be no hard feelings. But I need you to be honest with me. Do you think you can do that?”

Nicky stared at her with wide eyes. “Okay.”

Agatha drew in a shaky breath. “Good.”

Still, the question didn’t come. Agatha waited until the calves had finished their bottles, and then she and Nicky retreated back to the gate. The calves stood on the opposite side, bellowing bossily at them, and Nicky offered them a few pets and ear scratches. Soon enough, though, the calves realized that the milk bar was closed for the night, and they ambled away, grazing on the grass along the fenceline.

“What do you want, Nicky?” Agatha asked finally.

Nicky’s brow furrowed in confusion. “What?”

“In life,” Agatha clarified. “When you imagine your future, if you could have everything you want in life, what does that future look like?”

Nicky looked down and kicked at the dirt, the toe of her neon pink Croc catching on a rock. “I don’t know.”

“Bullshit,” Agatha barked, her tone more abrupt than she meant. She took a deep breath to steady herself. “I know you. I know you’re a planner. You already have it all mapped out in your mind, don’t you?”

Nicky still wouldn’t look at her; instead she studied the grass and the dirt like it had all the answers. Finally she nodded, but she didn’t offer a verbal response.

“Let me see if I have an idea,” Agatha said. “You graduate next year. You get accepted to multiple colleges because, well, you’re far more brilliant than you give yourself credit for. I don’t know which one you’ll choose, but I know you want to major in pre-vet, just like Mami.”

At the mention of her other mother, Nicky’s head shot up. She opened her mouth like she wanted to argue, but Agatha just shook her head.

“It’s okay. I’ve always known you were like her,” she said with a slight grin. “You’ll go to college. You’ll excel. You’ll graduate and then you’ll get accepted into vet school. Hell, I wouldn’t be surprised if you get into UC-Davis, just like Mami. You’ll go to vet school, and then…”

Agatha let her voice trail off, studying her daughter’s face. Nicky looked enthralled at the description, as if Agatha was laying out her perfect fairytale ending.

“I don’t know what happens after that, Nicky,” Agatha told her honestly. “I need you to fill me in, sweetheart.”

Nicky swallowed hard and her hands gripped the metal gate until her knuckles turned white. Agatha slid her hand over until it covered Nicky’s, trying to give her courage with her touch.

“Part of me wants to stay here,” Nicky said, her voice barely above a whisper. “I don’t want to leave you.”

“I know,” Agatha said, her voice bittersweet. “But what about the other part?”

“I want to see Antarctica,” Nicky said. “I want to see penguins.”

Agatha nodded. “What else?”

“I want to go to Africa and see lions and elephants and gorillas. I want to see tigers in India and polar bears in Alaska.” With every declaration, Nicky’s voice grew in volume, excitement and confidence building with each syllable. “I want— Mama, there’s so many things I want.”

Agatha knew that want. It was all-encompassing, soul-shaking. She had it, and Rio had it, too. The only issue was that they had wanted two different things.

“What about this place?” Agatha asked. “The ranch. Do you want the ranch?”

Nicky’s confidence faced, replaced not by uncertainty, but by fear. She looked at Agatha out of the corner of her eye, hesitation etched in her features.

“You won’t be upset?” Nicky asked, her voice a whisper again. It was all the answer Agatha needed, but she shook her head anyway.

“I won’t be upset. I promise,” Agatha assured her.

Nicky looked straight ahead, at the calves grazing a few yards away. Agatha could see the tears in her eyes, and she hated that she might be the cause of them.

“No,” Nicky said, her voice soft but sure. “I don’t want it.” She looked at Agatha finally. “I’m not you, Mama. I can’t do what you’ve done with this place. I’m just… It’s not for me.”

Agatha nodded. She had known the answer before she ever asked the question. She just needed to hear Nicky say it.

“I never wanted anything like I wanted this place,” Agatha told her honestly. “Growing up, well… I haven’t told you much about your grandmother, and there’s a reason for that. She wasn’t kind. She was controlling, sometimes cruel. The only place that I ever felt like I could be free was here, on this ranch. Sure, your great-grandpa disciplined me, he expected work. But I always knew what to expect from him. And when I was done with chores, I was free to wander and roam in a way that I couldn’t back home with my mother. To me, this ranch always meant freedom.”

Nicky nodded in understanding.

“Your Mami was the opposite,” Agatha continued. “This town was too small for her. She was always bound for something bigger, everyone said so. So when she saw how devoted I was to this place, she didn’t see freedom. She saw chains. She still sees it that way.”

“I don’t understand why you’re telling me this,” Nicky said, confused.

The sun was finally setting. Dusk fell over them, casting the entire ranch in shadow. Agatha’s eyes could just barely make out the outline of Nicky’s profile in the dark, and she was struck by it. Everyone had always said how much Nicky looked like her, but in the darkness, looking at just the shape of her face, her lips, her nose?

She looked so much like Rio.

“Mama?” Nicky asked, her voice thick with concern.
